<div align="center"><b>Famous Schools of Dark Magic in Salamanca, Toledo, Norcia, and Visignian Cave</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 1-1</div>
Finally, we know that, as recently as our grandfathers' memory, there were famous schools of this dark magic [goetia] in Salamanca and Toledo in Spain, at Lake Norcia in Italy, and in the Visignian cave.

<div align="center"><b>Scotus of Parma Drives Bishop Gebhard Truchsess Mad</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 2-2</div>
(He was the one who sent the apostate Bishop of Köln, Gebhard Truchsess [von Waldeburg], mad.)

<div align="center"><b>Fictitious Planetary Technique Sent to Charlemagne</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 2-2</div>
Add to these the fictitious planetary technique, inscribed on thin pieces of wood or metal, which was sent to Charlemagne by one of Bede's pupils-this is how these people lie and substitute Alkindi for Alcuin-useful (of course!) for obtaining a complete and more than natural understanding of the arts and sciences, and for achieving rank, riches, physical strength, enjoyment of love, etc.

<div align="center"><b>Celio Calcagnini's Fable on the Origin of Magic</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 2-2</div>
'VAIN FUTILITY once conjoined with MARVEL in a furtive copulation and gave birth to a MAGIC with the name 'favourable'. Dreading lest by chance TRUTH, (with which [Vain Futility] is perpetually engaged in hostilities, which she has also fought in close combat, and in which she has often been beaten), would kill off her offspring, handed it over, by conjugal agreement, to a married couple, SHAMELESSNESS and FEAR. But straight away they secretly laid it in the lap of INCOMPREHENSIBILITY to be brought up under the influence of the cradle-songs of CONJURING-TRICKS. [Magic] soon grew up and then, furnished with little wings, it flew off to the FORTRESS OF FOOLS, and there built a nest and gave birth to CREDULITY who, in gratitude for the kindnesses of her parent, nourishes [Magic] and loves her back], as though [Magic] should shortly die if she did not.'

<div align="center"><b>Jews and Arabs Effect Remarkable Things with Magical Book</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 3-3</div>
The Jews and Arabs in Spain used to leave this book to their descendants as an heirloom, and by its means used to effect a number of remarkable and incredible things.

<div align="center"><b>Book on Evoking the Dead in Recent Times</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 4-4</div>
a book on magical technique [artis grimoyriae] which teaches [its readers] how to evoke the dead, and was going the rounds in our fathers' lifetime.

<div align="center"><b>The Nobleman from Liège's Pact with the Devil</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 6-6</div>
Caesarius of Heisterbach tells the story of a nobleman from the territory of Liège who made a pact of this kind.²² The evil spirit, however, was heard speaking but was not seen, and at this point the young man, along with everything else he did, was ill-advised enough to deny his Creator with a gesture of renunciation and do homage to the devil. He, in his turn, promised [the young man] riches and honours.

<div align="center"><b>Archdeacon Theophilus's Pact with Evil Spirits</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 6-6</div>
On this subject, too, I should make reference to what Archdeacon Theophilus did, according to Sigebert. 'In order to recover his position, he sought help from the Prince of evil spirits, who ordered him to deny Christ, the Son of God, and His mother Mary, along with every tenet of Christianity, to confirm this denial in writing, to sign what he had written, to hand over the signed document to him, and to bind himself to his service'. In the latter instance, the witness was a magician, in the former the Superintendent of Iniquity.²³

<div align="center"><b>The Magicians of Mantes-la-Jolie and Their Book to the Sibyls</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 6-7</div>
Sometimes they do this via a little book of humble requests which they present to the Devil. In our own day, for example, magicians from Mantes-laJolie in Normandy, wrote a little book which they were going to offer to the Sibyls, the guardians of necromantic magic. Briefly, it said that (i) they begged the Ladies to deign to consecrate the [magicians'] books of magic so that evil spirits [spiritus] would obey them quickly in every way in conformity with the formulae of invocation contained in the books, and that when they were invoked they would appear to them, without doing them harm, in the shape of a good-looking human being, and that they would not have to make any circle or enter houses or go into the fields; (ii) that the Sibyls would stamp their sign or character on the books, (there were three of them), so that the books would have more effectiveness and dominance over the foresaid spirits [spiritus]; (iii) that the petitioners were to be kept safe from judges' traps and the punishment [they inflict]; (iv) that everything they started and their endeavours to get a friendly reception at the courts of princes were to be successful and prosper, and that they were to make money by gambling whenever they wanted; (v) that their enemies should not be able to do them harm. In return for these [requests], they promised in their turn that, on the very day their books were consecrated, they would regard the foresaid Sibyls as their mistresses and princes in perpetuity and offer them an individual soul every year for as long as they lived, (that is, they would murder and sacrifice a human being, or certainly initiate him into their ritual of unbelief.) But according to their binding agreement, the Sibyls were to provide these people in good faith with what they had demanded. (They were all burned in Paris, along with their books. [Pierre] Crespet tells this story in his book The Hatred of Satan, discourse 15.

<div align="center"><b>The German Woman Leaping from Her Bath</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 7-7</div>
Perhaps the less common form applies to the explicit pact in the example of a German woman who leaped backwards out of a large basin she was using as a bath and began to cry out that the distance she jumped away from Christ was the same distance she was approaching the evil spirit. (I shall speak about this at greater length in Book 5).

<div align="center"><b>The Witch's Confession Before the Devil</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 9-9</div>
'This woman, in hope of saving her life, gave an account of everything from beginning to end. This acknowledgement of her requires individual headings to express it all. First, she said that her [Novice] Mistress brought her in front of the judgement-seat of their prince, he being the Devil in the form of a king seated upon a throne of majesty. The [Novice] Mistress had instructed her beforehand what she should do, so she came forward and first was obliged to deny her baptism and abandon all the teachings of the Christian faith'. (Here you have apostasy.) 'Then she had to cast aside all the Church's sacraments, and trample underfoot the cross and images of the Blessed Virgin Mary and other saints.' (Here you have sacrilege.) 'They do not do these last in front of the Devil but do them elsewhere later on. It is enough that they promise to do them'. (Here you have the profession). 'Then she made her vow by means of a solemn engagement, [putting her hands] in the prince's hands. She vowed and promised she would always be faithful to him, be obedient to him, and obey all his commandments'. (Here you have the vow.)

'Then, in the midst of her oath-taking, she touched [some] characters written on the outside of a large book containing pages which were hidden away, and did homage or perpetual vassalage', (here you have homage,) '[saying] she would never return to the faith of Christ, or keep God's commandments, but that she would pay attention only to those [issued] by the prince himself, and that in future she would be obedient to him and could come every time when summoned to [his] revels and assemblies. [She also promised] to do what the other women did during those [assemblies], and also that she would be present at their nocturnal sacrifices and would [make] the customary prayers and attend the ceremony of adoration'. (Here you have idol-madness.) 'She would fulfil, to the best of her ability, whatever vows [the prince] might happen to put in front of her and bring as many people as she could to [make] this same profession'. (Here you have the declaration.) 'On his part, however, the prince himself, with surpassing effrontery, promised to give this woman in front of him everlasting happiness, grandiose things, immeasurable things, and whatever pleasures she wanted to have in this world, and that at long last, after this life, she would obtain greater gifts [than these]'. This is what Grillando says.

<div align="center"><b>Jean del Vaulx's Pact with the Devil</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 9-9</div>
Jean del Vaulx from Stavelot, a seer [sortiarius], used to add further that the Devil had convinced him that after this life seers would be like evil spirits and would be changed into evil spirits and be given lordship in the air.

<div align="center"><b>Théodore Maillot and the Evil Spirit's Deception</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 13-14</div>
In Book 1, chapter 9 of his Worship of Evil Spirits, Nicolas Rémy describes Théodore Maillot as follows.

'When he was an old man, he obtained the governorship of a province in a huge kingdom. [This province] comes under the jurisdiction of Lorraine. While he was still a young man, he was desperate to marry a young woman from a very splendid family. All hope of his being able to achieve this had been torn from him and snatched away because he had no money and was of humble stock. He was an apprentice engaged in trade which, at that time, meant he was low-born and despised. So, he saw no sufficiently honourable way of even making his love known. Therefore, as people in hopeless situations, when expectation itself deserts them, easily turn to any kind of plan and embrace it without any thought whether they should shun it or follow it, Maillot went to a fellow-servant from Germany [because] he had been told that this man had an evil spirit who was prepared to be compliant in every respect. He opened up to him and told him what was making him ill and begged him not to begrudge him a remedy if he could provide him with one. His kindness, [he said], would not go unrewarded. The German willingly seized his opportunity because, in accordance with his pact, in a few days' time he either had to free and disengage himself from the evil spirit by bringing him someone else who would transfer this debt to himself or incur another one, or be put to death and killed at the hands [of the evil spirit] who would twist and break his neck, (because this stipulation had been added to the pact.)

So, in order to carry out his plan, he agreed they should meet the following day at daybreak in an out-of-the-way room which would be locked and have its shutters drawn. They had scarcely got there when the doors suddenly opened and in came an elegant young woman with a generous figure. (The evil spirit wanted to show himself in this form first so that Maillot would not become terrified at his frightening appearance.) She announced she was the woman who would easily get him the marriage he wanted so badly, provided he was willing to follow the instructions she would give him. He was all agog and eagerly waited to hear whatever she would tell him [to do]. The first thing she started with was an instruction to abstain from theft, drunkenness, lust, blasphemy, and every other sin which defiles the soul. [Then she said] he should cultivate devout behaviour, support the needy from his resources, and weaken himself twice a week by fasting. He was not to neglect [the Church's] feast-days, he should pray every day, and he must unremittingly attend to all the things it was proper and fitting for a Christian to do. If he was willing to bind himself by a formal vow [to do] all this, the result would be that there would be no difficulty in his obtaining the marriage he wanted. Once she had said this and appointed a day by which she would receive his answer, she left hastily. Maillot, who could see that such holy and respectable conditions made a benefit of such importance to him obtainable, now began to think there was no point in delaying and that he should gratefully and willingly accept [her proposal]. But while he was giving the matter more and more thought and being driven hither and thither in different ways between hope and fear, a member of the household, who was a priest, guessed from the expression on his face that he was concealing something which was perplexing him. He approached him gently, found out what was troubling him, and with his advice prevailed on Maillot not to stoop to any further conversation with the evil spirit. The German was thus deceived in his expectation, and it was not long before he paid the penalty laid down in his pact, because not many days later, on a level open road, he tumbled from his horse, fell heavily to the ground, and was found to have died in an instant'. This is what Rémy says.

<div align="center"><b>The Exposed Fraud of the Vanishing Sacrificial Victim at Paneas</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 23-23</div>
Astyrius disclosed a fraud, the perpetration of which Eusebius rescued from oblivion. 'At Caesarea Philippi, which the Phoenicians calls "Paneas", a sacrificial victim is brought to springs which burst out there from the lower slopes of Mount Paneion. (These springs are said to be the source of the [River] Jordan.) On one of the pagans' feast-days [the victim] was slaughtered, thrown [into the water] and, through the force and power of an evil spirit, vanished from people's sight in an extraordinary way. Those who were present thought this a most remarkable miracle. Astyrius happened to be there on one occasion while this was taking place, and when he saw so many people astounded by it, he was very distressed by their mistaken belief. So, he looked up at the sky and humbly entreated God, the ruler of everything, through Christ to restrain the evil spirit who had shat⁷¹ on the people by leading them so badly astray, and to control him because of the fraud and deceits he is in the habit of practising against human beings. When he had prayed to God this way, the sacrificial victim suddenly floated on top of the springs, and so the [spectacle] it had been their custom to find so astonishing was completely ruined, so much so that in future no miracle would ever take place there again'.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil's Deception of Claudia's Ship</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 25-25</div>
(The Devil made this happen so that the ship which was bringing Mother Idaea would look as though it was following Claudia's belt).⁷⁶

<div align="center"><b>The Candle Illusion of Donkey-Headed Diners</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 25-25</div>
Likewise, if one were to make a candle out of ass's semen and wax, and this candle was the only light during a dinner-party, all the diners, (if one can possibly believe the Bishop of Paris), would look as though they had donkeys' heads.⁷⁵

<div align="center"><b>Zedekiah the Jew's Illusions</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 25-25</div>
In this category one must include the illusions of a Jew, Zedekiah, who lived at the time of Louis the Devout [778-840]. He would throw a man into the air, tear him to pieces, collect the pieces together, and reunite them again, and he would also swallow a cart laden with hay, together with its horses and driver, in the presence of the entire population.

<div align="center"><b>The Delusion of Praestantius's Father</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 25-25</div>
However, when an evil spirit convinces human beings they have been changed into animals, he does not merely change the organ but sometimes the medium as well, and he always changes the imagination at the same time, as in the case of Praestantius's father, described by St Augustine. The Devil, you see, can put together and arrange semblances of things in such a way that even while someone is awake, (which is what St Augustine says), he will dream as people who are asleep do, and think he is feeling things he does not feel, the way mad people do.

<div align="center"><b>Magicians' Illusion of a Springing River</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 26-26</div>
An example of the first is when magicians put a bow made from a particular wood in a room and shoot an arrow made from a different wood from a string of a particular thickness. When they do this, they make it seem as though a river as wide as the flight of the arrow has sprung up in that place.

<div align="center"><b>The Counterfeit Snake and the Breaking of Pottery</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 26-26</div>
Likewise in the incident reported by Nicetas about a counterfeit snake.
'When, from a rather high place in the palace at Constantinople, Michael Sicydites saw a skiff which was carrying pots and dishes, he [uttered] a magical incantation and made a sailor get up and carry on breaking the pottery until he had reduced it to dust. But not long afterwards he seized hold of his beard and began to weep and, once his mental blindness had cleared, he started to wail that he had been driven [to do it] as though by the wrath of a divinity. Asked why he had treated his merchandise this way, he told a painful story. He was concentrating on his rowing, [he said], when he saw a dreadful snake stretched out on top of the pots. With unblinking eyes, it turned towards him as though it was going to devour him and did not stop coiling itself up until the earthenware had been shattered. Then suddenly it vanished from his eyes'.

<div align="center"><b>Eon's Bewitching of Eyes</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 26-26</div>
William of Newburgh testifies to the conjuring-tricks of Eon, a heretic and a magician. 'He used to bewitch [fascinasse] people's eyes so that they thought there was a great crowd of men in front of them, and an abundance of delicious food fit for a king, and the most magnificent furniture'. At length, however, he was sentenced at the Council of Rheims, and while he was being led to his death he quite frequently said to the earth, 'Earth, open up!' a fruitless attempt we read about in Robert's Chronicle.

<div align="center"><b>Jakob Melstinki's Impersonation of Christ and the Apostles</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 26-28</div>
This is how the evil spirit amuses himself with conjuring-tricks and, not content with imitating miracles by means of conjuring-tricks, tries to represent those very conjuring-tricks to heretics [as miracles] by means of crafty deceits as well. I have read an example [of this] which was so delightful and amusing that, because it diverted me, I cannot deprive the readers of this book of it and so, for the sake of giving them respectable pleasure, I am now happy to undertake the trouble of transcribing it for them.

'At the time of Sigismund I, King of Poland, Jakob Melstinki, governor of the town of Brzeziny [Brezin], in a particularly frivolous frame of mind, appropriated to himself the authority and name of Christ and, as the Twelve, chose the two Zatorskis from Krakow, while the remaining [ten] were thieves like himself. He gave each of these the name of one of the Apostles and called himself Jesus Christ. While they were walking through villages all over the place, they used to work wonders after the fashion of jesters and conjurors. They would secretly suborn other cheats and thieves to play dead, and then resurrect them; they would first put fish in muddy bogs where fish could never be born, and then in the name of Christ catch them with their hands; they would put loaves in ovens when there weren't any there beforehand, and then draw them out in the name of their 'Christ', to the astonishment of the simple-minded common people.

On one occasion, [Melstinki] came with his apostles to the monastery of Czestochowa, famous for its icon of the Blessed Virgin. They stayed there for several days and secretly persuaded one of their number to act as though he were under the influence of an evil spirit, and by these means used to obtain food. He, you see, would walk through the guest-houses and snaffle pieces of meat from the kitchen, and throw them to his companions who would make the sign of the cross over them and eat them. When a large crowd of people had gathered in front of icon of the Blessed Virgin, [Melstinki's] tricksters brought their possessed companion to the altar, wearing two shirts so that he could put the things he needed into the space between. They actually put pebbles inside his undershirt and while they were bringing him to the altar, he broke free in a raging fury from the hands of those who were leading him, rushed up to the altar which was laden with offerings, snatched up some coins, and hid them in the space between his two shirts. At this interruption to the divine office, one monk fled from the altar. The other monks hurried to loosen [the possessed man's] belt. At that point, only the pebbles fell to the floor, while the money remained in his double shirt. The monks thought the money had been changed into pebbles by an artifice of the evil spirit and they tried, by means of exorcisms, to turn [the money] back into its former shape. 82 When the pebbles remained as they were, however, a monk full of indignation threw his holy book on the floor, saying, "We have never had an evil spirit like this one. Away with him to all the devils!"

These tricksters fled to Silesia with the genuine money, and there they turned aside in a certain village to the house of a noble married woman. But because her husband was not there, she was unwilling to give them admittance and they merely asked for a napkin or a piece of cloth so that they could celebrate Mass. The woman offered them a little bundle of cloth and then they said, 'We shall take this with us, and Christ will bless you so that your flax grows more abundantly. Tell us if you have another one'. Since they wanted to take this as well, the woman refused because she was afraid of her husband, but they secretly rolled up a scrap of kindling along with fire into the cloth and gave it back to the woman. Consequently, a chest was set on fire because of the cloth and the house was set alight because of the chest, and when the husband came back his wife said this had happened because she had treated Christ and His Apostles badly.

Her husband, however, incandescent with rage, said, 'This man was an absolutely good-for-nothing thief, not Christ!' So, he and his neighbours went in pursuit of them and caught up with them in one of the villages. Then the pseudo-Christ said to the man who was called Peter, 'My suffering and the cup I am about to drink are approaching'. Peter said, 'I see they are near at hand for me as well, Lord. But [Melstinki] said, 'Peter, I shall be obliged to escape through the window', and Peter said, 'I shall not abandon you as long as I live. Wherever you run away to, I shall follow'. So, he fled through the window and the rest of the 'apostles' also escaped in whatever way they could. The country folk followed them, and felled them with their sticks and clubs, saying, Prophesy to us, Christ and your apostles, in which wood did these sticks grow?' Having been punished by these blows, therefore, they later amended their life, saying, 'It is difficult for us to undergo Christ's Passion and the Apostles' sufferings'. This is what the chronicles of Poland say.

<div align="center"><b>The Juggler's Sword and Spear Performance in Athens</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 28-28</div>
'In front of the Painted Porch [in Athens] I saw with my own two eyes a juggler swallow a cavalry-sword which was sharp at one end and had a dangerous point, and not long afterwards I saw the same man plunge a hunting-spear, lethal end first, right down into his guts; and, do you know, from behind the blade of the spear, where the shaft of the inverted weapon went into the man's throat, there appeared a pretty, effeminate boy who climbed up and did a dance, writhing round [the shaft] in a series of complicated bends and stretches, as though he had not a tendon or a bone in his body, to the astonishment of every one of us who was there'.

<div align="center"><b>Giants and Magicians' Illusions in the North</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 28</div>
In his book dealing with the north, Saxo Grammaticus writes that giants and magicians performed an unheard-of kind of wonder by means of various conjuring-tricks and, with their supreme skill in deluding [people's] eyes, disguised their own faces and those of other people with a variety of images, and hid their true appearances with convincing masks.

<div align="center"><b>Shape-Shifting and Wind-Selling Lapps, Finns, and Biarmi</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 28</div>
Olaus Magnus writes about Lapps, Finns, and Biarmi, [saying] that they take whatever shape they want and likewise that they spy on what their friends or enemies are doing in another part of the world, and also sell travellers by sea any wind they want for the price of an article of clothing made from linen.

<div align="center"><b>Tartar Provinces' Incantations Against Enemies</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 28</div>
Alessandro Guagnini tells us that beyond the kingdom of Kasan there were three small Tar tar provinces which were unusually addicted to incantations, and that by these means they frequently stirred up fogs and other bad weather in the sky, and in this way overthrew their enemies.⁸⁷

<div align="center"><b>Zoroaster Drawing Sparks from the Stars</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 28</div>
Zoroaster used to draw things like sparks from the stars.

<div align="center"><b>The Marvels of Simon Magus and His Guard Dog</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 28</div>
Dositheus performed wonders long ago, but his pupil, Simon Magus, surpassed him-I dealt with his marvels earlier-and Glykas writes briefly in Part 2 [of his chronicle] as follows. 'He made statues walk, and when he threw himself into a fire, he was not burned. He would fly into the air, make bread from stones, and flit from one shape to another. Sometimes he would have two faces. He used to change himself into a pillar. He would make closed doors open of their own accord and domestic crockery seem to move of its own accord to perform its duties for humans. [He made] ghosts walk in front of him and said they were the souls of dead people. Once, when the Emperor was asking after him, he was struck down by terror, abandoned his own shape, and fled to another one'. In a later passage, Glykas writes, 'He had a dog chained at the threshold of his house, and this would devour anyone who tried to visit Simon without being invited. The dog told [St] Peter to come in and, in a human voice, announced that Peter had arrived'. You notice the Devil's power yielded to the power of God which was in Peter, and that Cerberus fawned upon [St Peter] whom he was unable to harm.

<div align="center"><b>Pythagoras's Golden Thigh and Tamed Eagle</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-29</div>
Pythagoras showed off his golden thigh, tamed an eagle with an incantation, and often used to have a conversation with it.

<div align="center"><b>Numa Pompilius's Illusory Banquet</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-29</div>
Numa Pompilius put on show the trappings of a sumptuous banquet, even though he had no food at all in the house.

<div align="center"><b>King Bajan's Shape-Changing</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-29</div>
Any time Bajan, King of Bulgaria, wanted to change himself into a wolf or some other animal, he was seen to change shape. Did he sometimes do this so that he would not be seen by anyone?⁸⁸

<div align="center"><b>Ollerus Sailing on an Enchanted Bone</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-29</div>
Ollerus used to sail over vast stretches of the sea on an enchanted bone as though it were a ship.

<div align="center"><b>King Eric's Wind-Drawing Hat</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-29</div>
Eric, King of the Goths, would draw a favourable wind from wherever he turned his hat, and Philostratus tells us wonderful things about Apollonius [of Tyana], and Suidas about Julian the Chaldaean.

<div align="center"><b>Julian the Chaldaean Checking the Plague in Rome</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-29</div>
Both Apuleius and Anastasius tell us that during the reign of Domitian this Julian surpassed Apollonius in this craft and when it came to checking a plague which had suddenly fallen on Rome. (There is a mention of Julian in Arnobius, Book 1, [52].) When two learned men have a rambling conversation while they are putting Vulcan back, Anastasius adds the same thing, and from him Cedrenus [repeats the information] that Manetho was so skilled in working magic that he was always openly contemptuous of Apollonius to his face.

<div align="center"><b>The Innkeeper Wise-Woman's Transformations</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 29-30</div>
Apuleius, himself a magician according to the evidence given by many people, writes about two women who were most deserving of astonishment. First of all he tells us about the landlady of an inn-a 'queen of wise women', to let you know that even in those days some of them were honoured with that title. 89 'A wise-woman', he says, 'has the power of a divinity to bring down the sky and lift up the earth, turn springs into stone and cause mountains to melt away, to raise the spirits of the dead to the surface of the earth and bring the gods down under it, to quench the stars and light up the underworld itself. Because her lover had dishonoured her with another woman, with a single word she turned him into a wild beaver, because when that animal is afraid of being caught, it escapes from those who are chasing it by biting off its genitals, [her idea being] that, because he had had sex with another woman, something similar would happen to him.

Since a neighbouring landlord was in competition with her, she changed him into a frog, and now the old man swims around in a barrel of his own wine and speaks to his former customers in polite croaks while he grovels abjectly in the lees. She changed someone else, an advocate who had spoken against her in court, into ram and now that is how he pleads his cases. This same woman condemned her lover's wife to perpetual pregnancy, (because she had said something satirical at her expense), by closing up the woman's womb and retarding the foetus's growth, and according to everyone's calculations the poor woman has now been distended for eight years, as though she were going to give birth to an elephant.90

<div align="center"><b>Pamphile the Magician's Powers</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 30-30</div>
Later on, he says of Pamphile, 'She is believed to be a magician [maga] with a very great reputation, and to be mistress of every kind of graveyard incantation. She knows how to plunge the entire light of the starry universe into the depths of the underworld and turn it into its ancient chaos, [simply] by breathing on twigs or pebbles or worthless things of that kind. As soon as she catches sight of a good looking young man, she is captivated by his beauty and immediately has no eyes or thoughts for anyone else. She instantly turns those who are of low rank and not complaisant enough into rocks and cattle and any other kind of creature. Others, however, she destroys completely.⁹¹

<div align="center"><b>Empedocles the Wind-Stopper</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 30-30</div>
According to [Diogenes] Laertius, Empedocles boasted considerably in the following vein:

'You will obtain the drugs with which to drive away illnesses and lighten old age,

And I am communicating them to you alone. By means of your rituals you will restrain the violence of winds Which suddenly spring up and devastate the fields with their raging blasts.

If you wish, you will soon rouse sluggish streams [to run] high And bring back fine weather from the midst of a storm. You will bring health-giving showers in the midst of summer, And the winds which blow over everything when the harvest is dry, And you will recall a dead person from the black underworld.

He also adds, from the histories of Timaeus, that whenever the etesian winds were blowing so strongly that they flattened the crops, Empedocles ordered that donkeys be flayed and after [their skins] had been turned into bags, he said they were to be moved to hills and the tops of mountains to collect the breezes.

Once the winds had stopped because of this, he was known as the xwhuoavéMas [wind-stopper].

<div align="center"><b>Circe's Love for Picus and Her Magical Transformations</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 30-31</div>
In his Metamorphoses, after Ovid has told the story of Ulysses's companions being turned into pigs, he expatiates on Circe's love and hatred for King Picus, among other things, and about Camens, or Canens, Picus's wife.

'She was remarkable for her beauty, but even more remarkable for the way she sang, Which is why she was called "Canens". She would make woods and rocks move, Soothe wild creatures, and interrupt the flow of long rivers With her voice and hold back roaming birds [from flying away]'.

A little later, he tells us about Circe herself, who had caught sight of Picus and had fallen violently in love with him. She says,

'But you will not escape, [even if] the wind is allowed to carry you away, If I know myself, if the power of my herbs has not vanished entirely, And my magical chants do not fail me'. She finished speaking and fashioned the likeness of a non-existent boar without a body, And told it to run across in front of the King's eyes And seem to go into a grove where the trees were thick.

While Picus came along with his horse in pursuit of the non-existent boar, she overtook him and, with words and a wicked spell, lured him into an act of passion.

'She took to prayer and spoke words of supplication, And entreated unknown gods with an unknown incantation, One with which she was also accustomed to disfigure the face of the snowwhite moon And affix to its ancient head clouds which suck up moisture. Then, too, by chanting an incantation she made the sky grow dense, And the earth breathed forth mists', etc.

But when the man chastely rejected the adulterous woman, she became mad with rage.

'Then she turned twice to the west and twice to the east. She touched the young man three times with her wand, and three times spoke her incantations'.

He was changed into a bird and saw feathers on his body. Finally, when his servants used threats to demand their king [be released] from the power of her harmful magic, She inflicted harm upon them by sprinkling them with poisonous slime and juices, And summoned Night and the gods of night from Erebus and Chaos, And prayed to Hecate with drawn-out wailing cries. The woods-marvellous to relate-leaped up from their place, The ground groaned, the neighbouring trees turned pale, She sprinkled the animals' fodder and made it wet with drops of blood. It seemed as though the stones were producing hoarse bellowings, That dogs were barking, that the earth was squalid with black snakes, And the thin souls of the silent [dead] were flitting about. These evil portents filled the astonished crowd with panic. She touched the amazed faces of the panic-stricken with her magically poisonous wand, And at its touch the monstrous appearances of various wild beasts Fell upon the young men. No one retained his own shape'.

<div align="center"><b>Medea's Magical Practices</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 31-31</div>
Of Medea he says in his foolish way, 'She strives to draw down the unwilling moon from its course And to hide the horses of the sun in darkness. She curbs waters and makes winding streams stand still. She moves woods and living rocks from their place. She wanders among [newly-dug] graves, noticeable because of her loose hair, And collects particular bones from pyres which are still hot. She curses people who are not there, fashions images out of wax, And drives thin needles into their wretched liver', [Heroides 6.85-92].

<div align="center"><b>The Truthful Wise-Woman's Promises</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 31-32</div>
Tibullus: 'As a truthful wise-woman [saga] Promised me with the aid of her magic. I have seen her bring down stars from the sky, And turn the course of a fast-flowing river with a spell. She has split open the ground with an incantation, drawn the spirits of the dead from their graves, And called down bones form a pyre which was still hot. Now she rouses the companies of the underworld with her magical hissing, Now she sprinkles them with milk and sends them back again. When she pleases, she drives clouds from a gloomy sky. When she pleases, she calls up snows from the summer sky. She is said to be in possession of Medea's herbs And to be the only one who has tamed Hecate's dogs. She has composed for me incantations with which you could deceive [people]. Intone them three times and spit when you have finished chanting. [Your husband] will not be able to believe anyone who [tells him] about us, Not even himself if he were to see us [together] on a soft bed.

You must, however, have nothing to do with other men, because he will see everything else. It is only in my case he will see nothing at all. What shall I believe? This woman has certainly said She can destroy my loves with her incantations or herbs', [Elegies 1.2.4363].

<div align="center"><b>The Old Woman's Night Curse</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-32</div>
Elsewhere this same poet [says],

'Surely some old woman has not cursed you in the silence of the night With her incantations and herbs that make people pale? A magical chant draws crops from your neighbours' fields. A magical chant stops an angry snake in its tracks. A magical chant tries to draw down the moon from her chariot, And would do so if trumpets did not sound from far away', [Elegies 1.8.1722].

<div align="center"><b>The Separating Herb from Caucasus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-32</div>
Propertius, Book 1, Elegy 12: 'Were we at risk of envy? Surely a god has not crushed me? Or is it a herb, picked on the heights of Causasus, which has separated [us]? I am not the man I was', [vv.9-n].

<div align="center"><b>The Boasting Wise-Woman of Petronius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-32</div>
Petronius Arbiter introduces a wise-woman [saga] who boasts in the following fashion:

'Whatever you see in the world obeys me. The earth, which abounds with flowers, Becomes listless when I wish it, and withers when its juices have dried up. It pours forth its riches when I wish it, and a crag with its bristling rocks Hurl out their native waters. The sea submits motionless Waves to me, and the west winds place their silent blasts At my feet. Rivers obey me. So do Hyrcanean tigresses, and snakes I have commanded to stand still. Why am I telling you about quite trivial things? The image of the moon comes down, Drawn by my incantations, and Phoebus in alarm is compelled To turn round his frenzied horses, once I have rolled back the vault [of the sky]. So great is the power of words. The fiery passion of bulls dies down, xtinguished by a virgin's rites, and Circe, Phoebus's daughter, too, Has changed Ulysses's companions with her magical incantations,' [Satyricon 134].

<div align="center"><b>The Incantation in Nemesianus's Eclogue</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-32</div>
Nemesianus, a writer of eclogues, [says]:

'She chanted the incantation which makes the moon swell up and a snake burst open, Crags to run, crops to leave their field, and a tree to be uprooted, [Eclogue 4.70-71].

<div align="center"><b>Folia's Thessalian Enchantments</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-32</div>
The prince of the Latin lyric [in his description] of three very beautiful wisewomen [sagae] says this about Folia:

'She snatches down the stars and the moon she has enchanted With her Thessalian voice'.

<div align="center"><b>Canidia's Love Magic and Incantations</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-32</div>
In the same poem, Canidia chants:

'Not because of the usual potions, Varus, (Believe me, you are going to be in floods of tears!) Will you come running back to me; and your right mind Will return, but not because it has been summoned by Marsian incantations. I shall prepare something stronger, since you disdain me. I shall pour you a stronger dose. And the sky will sink below the sea With the earth stretched out [over both] Before you fail to burn with love for me, Like pitch with its black fires'.

And presently [she says]:

'I can make waxen images move, As you know yourself, thanks to your curiosity; And with my incantations I can tear down the moon from the sky.

I can rouse the dead after they have been cremated, And make potions which excite desire.

<div align="center"><b>The Powers of the Thessalian Women and Erichtho</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 32-33</div>
More accurately than anyone else although, because of his overblown diction and lofty arrogance, somewhat obscurely, Marcus Lucan [says] of Erichtho:

'By means of Thessalian women's incantation love, which has not been prevailed on by the Fates, Has flooded into hearts hardened [against it], And grace old men have burned with illicit flames. Nor is it only their noxious Potions, or the pledges of future love for their young, which they remove, Swollen with juice, from [a foal's] forehead, which have an effect.⁹⁷ Even when it has not been defiled by the bloody matter of the poison someone has drunk, Once the mind has been enchanted, it perishes. Those whom the harmony of married sex And the power of a seductive shape does not bind together, [These women have influenced by means of the magic whirling of a twisted thread.⁹⁸ Nature's changes have ceased. Drawn out by a long night, Daylight sticks fast. The upper air has not obeyed the law. The headlong sky has become sluggish because it has listened to their incantation, And while he bears down on the heavens which are driven forward on their swift axles, Jupiter is amazed they are not loving. Now [the women fill everything With rain, and draw clouds in front of hot Phoebus, And the sky thunders without Jupiter's knowledge. With these same incantations
[The women], their hair flowing loose, have driven far away The damp mists and rainclouds. Since the winds are abating, The sea swells high. Forbidden once again to experience storms, It is silent while the south wind is throwing it into confusion, and the sails which carry the ship, Have swollen against the wind. A torrent has hung from a steep cliff, Stuck fast, and a deep-flowing stream has run In a quite different direction. Summer has not brought a rise in the [waters of the Nile; The Meander sends its waters in a straight line, and the Arar Has dashed headlong upon the sluggish Rhône. Mountains have lowered their summit and loosened their ridges. Olympus Has looked up at the clouds; and when winter was making things stiff [with ice], Even though the sun had not been shining, the snows in Scythia have melted away.

The Thessalian women's incantation has driven back from the shore they were defending The sea-goddess Tethys who was urged forward by the moon. The earth, too, has violently shaken the axes of her steady weight And, because of the exertion as she bends towards the centre of her sphere, has tottered. The cargo of such a great mass, struck by the incantation, Has fallen back and given a sight of the sky gliding round it. Every living creature with the power to kill, and which was born to do harm, Also dreads the Thessalian women and provides their practices with [the means of death. Greedy tigresses and the noble anger of lions Fawn upon these women. The snake unfolds his icy coils for them And stretches himself out upon the ploughed and frosty ground. Knotted vipers burst open their body and unite again, And a snake drops dead, breathed on by human poison,' [Pharsalia 6.452-491].

A few verses later, [he says]: 'By those women the stars were first Brought down from the sky as it rushed headlong, and the cloudless moon, Besieged by the dreadful poisons of their incantations, Looked pale and burned with dark light from the earth, Just as if the earth were stopping her brother's reflection, And inserting its shadows in the way of the flames from heaven. Pressed down [under the weight of the incantation, she suffers these great pains Until, having come nearer [the earth], she deposits foam on the herbs beneath her', [Pharsalia 6. 499-506].

<div align="center"><b>Medea's Rites in Seneca's Play</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 33-34</div>
No less learnedly, Lucan's uncle, famous for comedy and tragedy in what remains of [ancient] drama in Latin, my favourite, Seneca.

'Now, summoned by my rites, come, planet of the nights, Assume your most evil aspects, threatening in every one of your faces. For you, after the custom of my tribe, I have set free my hair from its bonds And have wandered your secret groves with naked feet. I have summoned water from clouds which have no rain, And driven the seas down to their depths; and Ocean Has sent his heavy waves further inland, since his tides have been overwhelmed. Because the law of the upper air has been thrown into confusion, The world has seen the sun and moon side by side and, 0 Bears, You have touched the sea which is forbidden to you. I have changed the sequence of the seasons. The summer earth has shuddered at my incantation And Ceres, under this compulsion, has seen harvest in winter. The Phasis has returned its violent streams to their source And the Ister, divided between so many mouths, has squeezed together Its savage waves and is sluggish on all its banks. The surge has resounded, the maddened sea thundered, Yet the wind is silent. The shelter of the ancient grove Has lost its shadows at the command of my incantation. Phoebus has abandoned the day and has come to rest in mid-sky; And the Hyades, moved by my incantations, are ready to fall', [Medea 750769].

<div align="center"><b>The Nurse's Command over Nature in Hercules Oetaeus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 34-34</div>
According to Seneca, the nurse claims no less for herself:

I have commanded a grove to bloom in the midst of winter's cold, And a discharged thunderbolt to halt in its tracks. I have stirred up a strait, Even though there was no wind, and I have calmed the wild sea; And the dry earth has opened up with fresh streams of water. Rocks have begun to move. I have burst open the doors Of the underworld and [scattered] its ghosts, and at my prayer of command The spirits of the dead talk, and the dog of the underworld has spoken. The sea, the land, the sky, and the underworld are my servants. The middle of the night has seen the sun, and day [has seen] night, And at my incantations, nothing keeps its laws', [Hercules Oetaeus 452463].

<div align="center"><b>The Vainglorious Magician's Transformations in Claudian</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 34-34</div>
Finally, the labour and study of my youth, Claudian:

'Because I have magical power, and there is in me An ardent desire to foresee the future. I know with which incantation A Thessalian woman tears away the radiance of the moon, what power The wise Egyptian's hieroglyphs have, and the technique whereby the Chaldean tribe Commands the gods it has summoned. The juices which flow From trees and the power of deadly [herbs] are not unknown to meWhatever flourishes on the Caucasus, rich with lethal plants, and blooms on the crags of Scythia[Herbs] which savage Medea and cunning Circe gathered. I have quite often propitiated the frightening spirits of the dead, and Hecate, With rituals done at night, and by means of my incantations Dragged corpses back to life after they have been buried. I have also destroyed many people with my chanting, Even though the Parcae were still spinning their threads. I have compelled oak-trees to wander about and a lightning-bolt to halt in its tracks.

I have bent rivers and turned them round to flow backwards And return to their sources. So that you may not think That perhaps I have told you lies, see how your own house has changed'. She finished speaking. The snow-white pillars, to his amazement, began To change themselves into gold, and the roof-beams suddenly Started to gleam with precious metal, [Against Rufinus 1.145-163].

<div align="center"><b>The Unburnt Hair of Iulus, Servius Tullius, Lucius Marcius, and Anagnini's Servant</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 37-37</div>
If by chance the Devil deceived pagans when he prevented a flame from burning the hair of Iulus, or Servius Tullius, or Lucius Marcius, or the tunic belonging to Anagnini’s servant, it is still possible for other things to be offered by way of explanation for such a well-known prodigy.

<div align="center"><b>The Weak Flame at the Waters of Scantia</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 37-37</div>
[The ancient writers] confirm this, because they tell us that a flame comes out of [a rock] at the waters of Scantia. 'It is weak when it comes out and cannot last long in a different material. Above this burning spring is an ash-tree which conceals it and is noticeably always green'. This would not be the case if the flame were real and complete.

<div align="center"><b>The White Fire of St Elmo on Ships</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 37-38</div>
Such, too, is that brilliant white fire which appears to sailors after a storm and, while resting confidently on top of the mast and tackle of ships, does not set fire to anything. (The pagans called this the white star of the Dioscuri or the Castors. To us it is St Elmo.)

<div align="center"><b>Roaming Balls of Flames Emitting Human Sound</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 38-38</div>
I have read about roaming balls of flames which emit a human sound

<div align="center"><b>The Amputated Corpse Arm Used as a Witch's Lamp</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 38-38</div>
and about a corpse’s right arm which was amputated, along with the shoulder blade and ribs, for use as a light during the night. The ends of its fingers burned with a sulphurous, violet-coloured flame so that witches [striges] could pour all their wicked venom upon someone who was sleeping and complete what they had decided to do. When at length the flame was put out, the fingers were as whole and uninjured as if they had not been a source of light earlier. Rémy claims that female magicians [magas] do this as often as they want, and has quite rightly attributed the entire thing to the cunning tricks of an evil spirit.

<div align="center"><b>The Harmless Flame Licking Snows on Etna</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 38-38</div>
On the other hand, when Claudius Claudian says that the snows on the summit of Etna remain unaffected because the immense heat 'under the snows protects them', and 'its harmless flame licks the frosts next to it', this is not a myth, because historians and natural philosophers vouch for it.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil Prevents Water from Spilling in Vestal Tuscia's Sieve</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 38-38</div>
just as the Devil did, too, so that the water Tuscia the Vestal was carrying in her sieve would not spill out.

<div align="center"><b>Malevolent Evil Spirit Stops Destructive Flame in Apamea Shrine</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 38-38</div>
This is also how the apparition of a malevolent evil spirit stopped the flame which had been thrown into the shrine of Jupiter at Apamea from destroying it until, by a miracle from God, a sprinkling of holy water drove the evil spirit away.

<div align="center"><b>Medea's Incantations Render Flame-Vomiting Bulls Harmless</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 38-38</div>
Likewise, poets write that Medea’s incantations made the breath of flame-vomiting bulls harmless, because the Devil always tries to imitate God’s miracles.

<div align="center"><b>The Miracle of Obedience: Brother Marcus Carries Burning Coals Barehanded</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 39-39</div>
I shall be content with a single example in which the merit of religious or blind obedience, along with faith, also shines forth. [Bernardino] Scardeone writes as follows. 'It happened once, perhaps by chance, that an Augustinian prior there was invited to lunch and sat down with these people', (the Jesuit followers of the Blessed [Giovanni] Colombini). 'After the food and drink had been removed and the spectators had withdrawn, and permission to speak had been given, he started to discourse, interminably and arrogantly, about the merits of the way Dominicans made their vows, tacitly reproving [the Jesuates] because, although they were counted as monks, they would not publicly and openly acknowledge their profession of the three vows along with all other monks in accordance with the usual form. To this the superior of the Jesuits, an uneducated individual, to be sure, but a clever man and one who was dear to God, craftily replied, "Father", (he said), "since we happen to be having conversation along these lines, do you want one of our brothers, who has offered himself to God in a straightforward way, or that companion of yours who has made his three vows in public, to show us who is the more genuinely obedient to his superior?" [The prior], not realising [what he was doing], willingly agreed. Then the Jesuit turned straight away to our Brother Marcus who happened to be serving those seated at table and said, "Brother Marcus, these friends of ours are cold.

By virtue of holy obedience, go quickly to the kitchen and bring them burning coals in your bare hands, so that they can warm up straight away". [Marcus] did not hesitate, (because he was quick to do as he was told), and believing there would be nothing he could not do if commanded under the rule of obedience, without any hesitation quickly carried from the kitchen to the table as many burning coals as he could in his cupped hands without any damage or harm to himself, and offered them to each man so that he could relieve his cold. Then, on the order of his superior, to everyone’s astonishment he took them back again whence he had brought them. The Dominican [i.e. Augustinian] prior I mentioned, immeasurably taken aback by this, looked at his companion as if he were about to tell him to do the same at once, but he, realising the situation, came to the conclusion he should not hesitate and immediately burst out as follows. "Reverend Father, for goodness’ sake don’t order me to tempt God. If you want, I shall bring fire in a sherd as I usually do, but not in my hands". But they, filled with astonishment on account of such a great miracle, were silent and stunned on the one hand, and on the other, pleased by their comrade’s unexpected reply, looked at each other and smiled.

<div align="center"><b>The Moon Swallowed by an Ass in Arcadia</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 40</div>
It no more agrees with reason than the more recent silly notion of a certain nation in our time, which thinks that the moon was swallowed by an ass because a herd of animals in Arcadia drank from a river in which, as does happen, the face of the moon was shining while [the herd] was drinking, and when [the animals] moved away the moon could not be seen because it was suddenly hidden by a cloud. Consequently, the people threw the unfortunate rider of Silenus into prison, fell on a colt, cut open its belly, and disembowelled it in a most savage fashion in order to restore the moon to the world.

<div align="center"><b>Apollonius and the Jars of Winds</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 40</div>
So does Philostratus when he writes that when he was among the Brahmins [of India], Apollonius saw two large jars, and once these were opened, storms and winds used to burst out, but when they were closed, fine weather and calm air would return, [Life of Apollonius 3.14].

<div align="center"><b>Sopater Fetters the Winds</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 40</div>
The natural philosopher Sopater, too, was put to death because he was accused of fettering the winds by means of a magical incantation so that the harvest could not be brought to Byzantium.

<div align="center"><b>Hail-Guards Avert Storms at Cleonae</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 41-42</div>
It is incredible that at Cleonae there were people publicly appointed to be халаÇopuráxas [hail-guards] to watch out for an approaching hail-storm. When these people had given the signal that such a hail-storm was near at hand, what do you think happened then? That people ran off to get their raincoats or get under cover? Not a bit of it! Each person would offer a sacrifice for his own benefit-one a lamb, another a chicken, and straight away, after they had tasted some of the blood, those clouds would start to veer off in another direction. Does this make you smile? Listen to something which will make you smile even more. If someone did not have a lamb or a chicken, he would lay hands on himself-but not to the extent of doing himself serious hurt, and I should not like to make you think that clouds are greedy or cruel. He would prick his finger with a really sharp pen and make his sacrifice with this blood. The hail would turn away from this man's little piece of ground just as much as it would [from the land] of someone who had persuaded it to do so by sacrificing bigger victims.

<div align="center"><b>Satan's Fire and Wind Afflict Job</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 42</div>
because there Satan makes fire fall from the sky and consume Job's slaves and flocks, and he also rouses a strong wind which overwhelms [Job's] sons and daughters while they are eating and drinking [Job 1.16 & 19].

<div align="center"><b>Wicked Angels Send Hail on Egyptians</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 42</div>
Holy Scripture specifically says that the hailstorm with which the Egyptians were punished was sent by wicked angels, [Exodus 9.18].

<div align="center"><b>Demonic Angels Offer to Stop the Winds</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 42</div>
Four angels standing at the corners of the earth offer to stop the winds from blowing and thus to inflict very great damage on the living, [Apocalypse 7.1].

<div align="center"><b>Magicians Calm Storm for Xerxes</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 43</div>
histories and exemplary stories about the winds and the storm calmed by magicians in the time of Xerxes, as Herodotus bears witness.

<div align="center"><b>Finns and Lapps Sell Winds with Knots</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 43</div>
Olaus [Magnus] writes as follows about the Finns and the Lapps. 'Once upon a time they used to offer to sell winds to merchants. They would use magic to consecrate three knots. When [the merchants] undid the first knot, they would have calm winds; when they undid the second, the winds would be stronger; and when they undid the third, the winds would be very strong', [History of Peoples in the North 3.15].

<div align="center"><b>Cyclades Inhabitants Attract Trade-Winds</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 43</div>
Aristaeus once said that the inhabitants of the Cyclades used to attract trade-winds during summer by means of certain rituals and sacrifices, and so, with their pleasant chill, keep the fields free from being squalid and barren.

<div align="center"><b>Indians Procure Rain in Cianita</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 43-44</div>
The Indians in Cianita procure rain by means of a ridiculous ceremony which is described in letters dated 1590 and 1591 from my Jesuit brethren in Peru. Gaspare Spitilli gives the following extract. 'When they are in need of rain, one of their magicians cries out in a loud, impassioned voice, speaking faster and faster, [saying] that they must all go to the mountain, and that when they get there everyone must abstain from food according to custom. (This "fast" of theirs, however, refers merely to salt, pepper, and cooked food.) When they have done this, they call earnestly upon the stars at the tops of their voices and pray for water. Then they return to their lands and pagan stronghold, taking a certain potion, which they drink when a young girl of a noble family offers it [to them]. It causes them to lose their senses and their reason, with the result that they look as though they are dead. After they have recovered, however, they mingle honey with water and maize and sprinkle the mixture in the direction of the clouds. Next day they place a well-born old man, or at any rate one of good reputation who is held in honour, on a couch and light a slow fire under it. When he starts to sweat, the magician catches the sweat in a jar, mixes it with goose-blood and the water from a crushed root, and again throws this liquid into the air as if to soak the clouds, asking, by the sweat, the blood, and the water, that the water they desire be bestowed upon them; and if by chance it rains on the day they carry out their superstitious ceremonies, they give thanks to the stars and shower the magician with many gifts'.

<div align="center"><b>Flame Produced by Incantation in Hypaepa</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 44</div>
This marvel involving water should not surprise anyone, because Pausanias writes that in Hypaepa it is common for a very bright flame to be produced from pieces of wood by means of an incantation in a foreign language, even though no fire has been applied to them, [Description of Greece 5.27.6].

<div align="center"><b>Witches Raise Vapors and Storms from the Fen</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 44</div>
what Rémy records in The Worship of Evil Spirits, Book 1, chapter 25 of the ritual used by witches [strigum] in our day is worthy of astonishment. 'I certainly remember this, which was testified and maintained by the free and voluntary assertion of two hundred people, more or less, whom I, in my capacity as a local magistrate, sentenced to be burned to atone for the crime of fortune-telling [sortilegii]. [They said] that it was their habit to meet on specific days and assemble on the bank of a fen or brook, especially if there was one which would be well hidden from the eyes of passers-by. There, with a wand they had been given by an evil spirit, they would keep on beating the water until they raised thick clouds of vapour and steam by which they were all borne aloft together. Then [they used] whatever they had stirred up in this fashion to clothe themselves in thick, dark clouds, and after they had enveloped themselves and their evil spirits in them, they would drive and thrust them wherever they wanted and at last shake out a lot of rain and hail on to the lands below. In this case I don't think there is any deception involved other than their thinking they are raising those vapours by thrashing [the water]. The evil spirit is doing it and carries the witches [striges] aloft through the air in those clouds and makes sure they do not suffocate. If those women are being carried along by the clouds, can it be denied that they could fall to earth from the clouds if the evil spirit abandons them? But more of this later. Rémy adds that before they beat the water like this, they would sometimes throw into it an earthenware jar in which the evil spirit had enclosed something secret, or even stones the same size they wanted the hail to be when it fell. Sometimes [they had] candles which they took down to the fen and held downwards while alight until drops [of wax] falling into it had sufficiently tainted the fen. In addition to this, they would sprinkle poisoned dust into the water. They would usually use black wands they had been given by an evil spirit to thrash the water and would beat the water with them without any kind of pause, at the same time repeating the words of dreadful imprecations. Certain women have also confessed that, with the help of an evil spirit, male and female witches drive big-mouthed jars through the clouds as far as a place they have in mind. [The jars] then hang upside down over it, are burst open with rocks and flames and, in a swift attack, destroy and pound to pieces anything in their path.

<div align="center"><b>Peasant's Daughter Summons Rain in the Garden</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 44-45</div>
Rémy gives many examples in chapters 25 and 29 of the same Book. Let me add two, one pleasantly amusing, the other ghastly. In the jurisdiction of Trier there was a peasant who, along with his eight-year old daughter, was planting cabbages in his garden. He happened to praise the little girl because she was carrying out this task well and she, a chatterbox because of her sex and age, said she knew how to do other, much more remarkable things. Her father asked her what she meant and she said, 'Stand aside a little and I shall make it rain all of a sudden on any part of the garden you like'. He was surprised and said, 'All right, I'll stand aside'. So he did so and the little girl dug a trench, poured water into it from her 'feet'-I'm using the Hebrew euphemism-stirred the water with a stick, muttered something or other under her breath, and lo and behold, suddenly rain [fell] from the clouds on to the designated spot. Her father was astounded. 'Who taught you this?' he asked. 'Mummy', she replied, 'and she's very good at this and other things like it, too'. After a few days the farmer, stirred by zeal, pretended that he, his wife, and his daughter had been invited to a wedding. Hastily they dressed up for the wedding and he put them into a wagon, drove them to the nearby town, and handed them over to a judge to pay the penalty for their crime of harmful magic [maleficium]. (I was told this story by men whom I find entirely worthy of being believed).

<div align="center"><b>Priests Summon Rain During Siege of Marcos</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 44-45</div>
The following exemplary story was recorded by [Giovanni] Gioviano Pontano. Ferdinand, King of Naples, was closely besieging the town of Marcos which lay near a fortress situated on Monte Dracone at the end of the Massican Hills, because it favoured the Angers party. A failure of its water-supply had almost forced it to surrender when a number of ungodly priests ventured to use magical practices to draw down rain. They were drawn from townspeople and the besieged who, under darkness of night, deceived the castle-guards and stealthily made their way along very uneven rocks to the shore, hauling with them an image of Christ nailed to the cross. First of all, they loaded it with abuse and frightful incantation, then they cursed it, plunged it into the sea, and invoked a storm from the sky upon the sea and the land. At the same time a number of priests, more villainous than anyone else, (seeing that they were busying themselves in carrying out unholy military manoeuvres to their satisfaction,) performed an abominable ritual whereby, it was said, they thought they could draw down the rain they needed. They stood an ass in front of the door of a church, chanted a deadly incantation, and then thrust a consecrated Host into the roof of [the animal's] mouth. They repeatedly addressed the ass in loud voices with deadly chants and then buried it alive on that spot in front of the church door. Scarcely had they completed the ritual than the air started to cloud over, winds began to stir up the sea, and darkness to pour over the brightness of midday. At one point the sky would flash with bolts of lightning, at another the light would be snatched away, and the sky and land would quake beneath the thunder. Trees ripped up by the winds were flying [through the air], and rocks split open by lightning were filling the air with their fragments. So much rain burst out of the clouds, and so heavy was the downpour, that not only were the cisterns incapable of collecting the water, but they poured out stones and rocks which had been burned and made thirsty by the sun, and torrents [of water] which spread out in every direction. Thus, the King, who had relied on taking the town by making it thirsty, was frustrated of his hope and returned to his former camp in Savo'.

<div align="center"><b>Statue of the Virgin Discharges Storm Against Saracens</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 45</div>
Vincent de Beauvais also tells us that the people of Constantinople were persuaded by a magician to immerse a statue of the Blessed Virgin in the sea and that it discharged a storm against the Saracens' fleet.

<div align="center"><b>Magician Invokes Lightning with Black Chicken Sacrifice</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 45-46</div>
Andrea Cesalpino, an Aristotelian, writes as follows in chapter 11 of his Inquiry into Evil Spirits. 'We have come across men who would stir up not only hailstorms, but lightning, too, but used to say they could not harm anyone they wanted, only those whom God had abandoned'-that is, in my opinion, who had fallen from God's grace through mortal sin. 'One of these men used to do this. He would invoke the prince of evil spirits in open fields and beg him to send one of his servants to strike with lightning someone he [the magician] had chosen. Later he would sacrifice a black chicken to him at a crossroad and throw it into the air, and once the evil spirit had received it, the magician would stir up a very violent hurricane, accompanied by lightning. This, however, rarely fell on the individual he had chosen'.

<div align="center"><b>Mongols Spread Darkness to Escape Robbers</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 46</div>
I have read in Marco Polo that the Mongols have such power because of their evil spirits' conjuring-tricks that they can spread darkness when and where they will, and that on one occasion [Polo] himself escaped with difficulty from robbers after they had surrounded him with this magic.

<div align="center"><b>Mongol Magician Spreads Darkness in Battle</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 46</div>
Hayton also tells us that a Mongol fighting-line was prepared for battle by their flag-bearer, a magician, who chanted a spell and spread deepest darkness over the enemy, and that they were victorious once light had been restored.

<div align="center"><b>Magi Raise Stench to Accuse Christians</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 46</div>
while Maruthes, the Bishop of Mesopotamia, was doing his official duties at the court of Yazdegerd, King of Persia, and Yazdegerd was showing the bishop favour because of his religious devotion, the Magi, fearing for their religion in case the King was going to convert, raised an intolerable stench and made up a false accusation that his Christian friends were responsible for it. But the Magi were arrested and, after a more thorough investigation of the matter, put to death, Nicephorus, History of the Church Book 14, chapter 18.

<div align="center"><b>Priestesses of Diana Walk Unharmed on Coals</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 46</div>
in olden times the priestesses of Diana at Castabala were not harmed by fire, (Strabo Book 5);

<div align="center"><b>Hirpi Walk Unharmed Through Fire</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 46</div>
among the Falisci, the Hirpi would walk through fire unharmed, as Strabo Book 12 and Pliny Book 7, chapter 2 testify.

<div align="center"><b>Rivers Cease Flowing by Magic</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 46</div>
They can make rivers temporarily cease to flow, dry up the channels of springs but still allow water to continue [bubbling up], wash away rocks and stones with springs they produce from the earth, and interrupt streams and drive them back to their source with the result that they seem to be flowing backwards-something Pliny says happened in his day.

<div align="center"><b>African Spellcasters' Deadly Praise</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 47-47</div>
In the land of Africa, there are certain groups of people who cast a spell \[effascinatium\] with their voice and what they say. If these people happen to bestow somewhat fulsome praise on beautiful trees, rather extensive cornfields, rather delightful children, very fine horses, or well-fed flocks in excellent condition, suddenly all these things die, punished for no reason other than this.

<div align="center"><b>Trier Wise-Woman's Milk Theft</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 47-47</div>
A few years ago, while I was staying in Mainz, a very notorious wise-woman \[saga\] was put to death in Trier. She had driven a stopcock in the wall of her house and would draw off all the milk from other people's cows-that is to say, an evil spirit would milk the cows and remove the milk very quickly by this means.

<div align="center"><b>Rémy's Insect and Animal Plagues</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 47-48</div>
According to Rémy, they have a habit of sprinkling a tiny bit of dust an evil spirit has given them, and this causes canker-worms, winged and wingless locusts, slugs, caterpillars, and this kind of noxious bane of fields and gardens, to make an immediate appearance. They lay waste to everything, pillage the standing corn, garden-vegetables, fruit, and sometimes the grass itself; and when they have done this, they sometimes raise a large number of mice which bury themselves in the earth straight away and eat the shoots and all the roots. Sometimes they attack draught-animals with a gadfly which, in the end, kills them cruelly with repeated stings. Sometimes they strike a particular herb against a tree and bring wolves out of what appears to be a hollow in the tree to the place where they want them to attack a sheepfold and its flock, and \[the wolves\] do not go away until they have caused great carnage. Rémy records the judicial confessions of all these people in Book 1, chapters 21 and 24, and thinks that the evil spirit does not produce these afflictions afresh for the occasion, but that he gathers things quickly from all over the place into a single spot. There is certainly no doubt at all that \[an evil spirit\] can do this.  
It seems likely that, because they harm and lie in ambush for precisely the particular flock and sheepfold to which they have been directed and which the person directing them wants them \[to attack\], those wolves are evil spirits who do this kind of damage under the guise of wolves. We may think that nothing stops an evil spirit from producing locusts and every other insect from material which has been placed here and there, by applying active things to passive, since they can be born from rottenness, although he does this at the time he needs and needs only a very short time \[to do it\].

<div align="center"><b>Sciltochium Town Fire</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
They can destroy houses with fire, as happened to the entire town of Sciltochium in Swabia which was reduced to ashes by a wise-woman \[saga\] in 1533.
