<div align="center"><b>Triscalinus's Priest Deception</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
An example is the well-known Triscalinus from Le Mans who pretended that a parish priest was carrying a pack of playing-cards under his arm instead of his breviary and even persuaded the priest himself \[that this was so\], and caused him, red with embarrassment, to throw the book away.

<div align="center"><b>Mainz Evil Spirit Slanderer</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
Such was the evil spirit of Mainz, an extraordinary slanderer, about whom \[Conrad\] Lycosthenes writes in his Extraordinary Events under the year 858, and another evil spirit who acknowledged that a monk in Santarém in Portugal was a saint.

<div align="center"><b>St Silvanus and St Kunegunda Slander</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
There is also the well-known story about a large number of evil spirits who were celebrating a feast at night and, even though they were deceiving demons, lied that they had come from a neighbouring lodging-house. St Germanus disclosed the deception.  
God may be more ready to permit them to take on the appearances of magicians and of those who have sometimes been ensnared by this crime, but it should not be denied that an evil spirit can fully adopt the appearances of innocent people if God allows \[him to do so\], although when and if He does allow it, He soon either uncovers the deception, as I have said, or allows it either \[as a contribution\] towards the remission of other sins \[the innocents have committed\], or for their greater merit and glory in enduring \[the experience\]. (See Bartolomeo della Spina, Investigation into Night-Flying Witches, chapter 14 and \[Three\] Defences \[against Giovanni Ponzinibio\], Book 3, chapter 3). ... something I read in connection with St Silvanus, Bishop of Nazareth, and St Kunegunda, (this I read in \[Albert\] Kranz), who was slandered by an evil spirit with behaving licentiously.

<div align="center"><b>Mammon's Possession of St Eustochia</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
I find that Mammon, an evil spirit who laid siege to the virgin St Eustochia of Padua, did this. God allowed her to be possessed this way for the whole of her life so that she could gain greater grace and glory, and when she was dead the name \'Jesus\' was found written in indelible writing on her little body. (Read Bernardino Scardoneo, The Antiquities of Padua, Book 2, class 6, folio 121).

<div align="center"><b>False Accusation Under Emperor Louis II</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
\[Magicians\] can likewise harm a reputation by revealing hidden sins, as demoniacs usually do, or by making a false accusation, as someone did in Germany during the reign of Emperor Louis II, (a story told by Sigebert \[of Gembloux\] and Vincent de Beauvais); and another evil spirit made a false accusation against \[St\] Dorotheus in relation to the same crime, as \[Simeon\] Metaphrastes tells us in \[Laurent\] Sauer, \'January\'

<div align="center"><b>Scoto's Illusory Banquets</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-48</div>
They can cause banquets or imaginary things to appear as, I think, did the Egyptians Celsus talks about according to Origen, the Brahmins according to Philostratus, and Pasetes according to Suidas, and some years ago \[Girolamo\] Scoto from Parma used to cause \[banquets\] to appear, but those who dined there, while seeming to be fully satisfied in Scoto's eyes, were tortured not long afterwards by genuine hunger.

<div align="center"><b>Magician's Failed Prison Escape for Duke Frederick</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 48-49</div>
\[Johannes\] Trithemius tells a story about Leopold, Duke of Austria, which is worth repeating. \'In 1533 Frederick, Duke of Austria, who had been elected Emperor instead of Ludwig of Bavaria, was beaten in a great battle between Ettingen and Moludorfum and handed over to Ludwig who sent him to a wellfortified tower to be kept under guard. Meanwhile, in Austria, a magician \[magus\] promised \[Frederick's\] brother Leopold that, if he were paid well, he would bring Frederick safely \[out of the tower\] within one hour by means of magic. The Duke made generous promises, and so an evil spirit was commissioned and flew to Bavaria. He assumed the appearance of a foreigner and came to the prison.  
\'If you want to be set free from captivity\', he said, \'mount this horse and I'll take you safe and sound to your brother Leopold in Austria\'.  
\'Who are you?\' asked the Duke.  
\'Don't ask me who I am\', replied the evil spirit, \'because that is beside the point. Just mount the horse I am showing you\'.  
At this point the Duke, who was in other circumstances a very courageous man, was seized with dread and protected himself with the sign of the cross. The spirit \[spiritus\], along with his black horse, disappeared and returned emptyhanded to the \[magician\] who had sent him. The magician loudly rebuked him for not bringing back the prisoner, and the spirit gave him an account of what had happened.

<div align="center"><b>Apollonius's Imprisonment Escape</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 49-49</div>
Philostratus writes that Apollonius \[of Tyana\] had escaped from his imprisonment, and the prayers of the Romans' Vestal Virgins were believed to halt runaway slaves who had not yet left the city.

<div align="center"><b>Tartars' Magical Smoke Victory Over Poles</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 50-50</div>
\[Martin\] Cromer says this about a memorable victory of the Tartars over the Poles. \'In 1240AD the Poles were fighting with the Tartars and were beginning to drive them forcibly in the direction of Legnica and press hard on the fugitives. In the Tartars' rear-guard there was, among other banners, a prominent flag with the letter X on its leading side. On the pointed end \[of its pole\], however, was the dreadful black figure of a bearded head. When the flag-bearer shook this flag with a degree of force, the head would breathe out smoke and a very dense, most disgusting vapour. This vapour would not only stop the Poles from seeing the barbarians but would also kill them with its utterly loathsome smell. The Tartars had made this happen by means of certain incantations which they use in their soothsayings and the rest of their divinations, (just as they do on other occasions, and especially in war), and they are also accustomed to guess the course of future events from human entrails. When the barbarians sensed that the Poles were in disarray, they encouraged each other, made an assault on \[the enemy\], threw their ranks into confusion, and caused great slaughter. The number of Christians killed in this battle was so great that the single ears the Tartars cut off. filled nine enormous sacks.\'

<div align="center"><b>Siderius's Wise-Woman Numbing Spell</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 50-50</div>
In the third Book of his history of \[the Kingdom of\] Bohemia, \[Jan\] Dubravius writes that in a battle in which Siderius was the victor, a wise-woman \[saga\] \[made sure\] that all the Luczani could neither go further forward nor retreat, and she fixed each man in his place by casting on him a marvellous numbness so that he could not run away.

<div align="center"><b>Noema's Magical Aid to Compus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 50-50</div>
The Chinese worship Noema, a virgin, for her sanctity. \[Juan\] González de Mendoza writes about her in Book 2, chapter 2 of his history of China. \[He says\] she had acquired the worship because of her practice of magic. When, by order of the King, a war-leader called Compus was leading his fleet towards an island, he could not pull up anchor, however hard he tried, so that he could sail away. At last, he noticed Noema sitting, quite unexpectedly, on his ship's figurehead. He approached her, full of reverence, and begged her for help and advice. She replied that unless she guided them, they would not be victorious over the Chinese, and so he set sail with her and hemmed in the enemy who were putting their trust in rather powerful conjuring-tricks. They poured oil on the sea and wrapped everything around in pretended flames, as though to destroy the fleet with fire. Noema put them out and dispelled similar stunts by the enemy with her more efficacious magic, the result being that when they saw that their attempts were unsuccessful, they acknowledged they were beaten and surrendered to Compus. (Mendoza adds several other things, too.)

<div align="center"><b>Ephesian Marks in Olympic Wrestling</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 50-50</div>
Suidas also testifies that a man from Miletus was taking part in a wrestling match with a man from Ephesus during the Olympics and was unable to win his bout because the Ephesian had \'Ephesian marks\' \[attached\] to his ankle, and it was noticed that as soon as these were removed by the referees the Ephesian, who had already exhausted thirty \[competitors\], was defeated.

<div align="center"><b>Chrysame's Poisoned Victory</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 50-50</div>
Polyaenus, too, tells us that Chrysame, a woman who used poisonous magic \[venefica\] procured a victory for Cnopus over the Erythraeans by means of her magic and drove the enemy mad, not with an incantation, but with poisoned pieces of bull's flesh, \[Strategems 8.43\].

<div align="center"><b>Huns' Apparitions Against Franks</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 50-50</div>
In Book 4, chapter 28 of his History \[of the Franks\], Gregory of Tours records that a Frankish army under King Sigebert was overwhelmed by the Huns because apparitions were cast in its path by an incantation.

<div align="center"><b>Willem van Gulik's Necromantic Defeat</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 51-51</div>
Next \[example\]: among the other causes of the great massacre of Flemings by the French in 1302, \[Jacob de\] Meyer in Book 10 of his chronicles records that the commander of the Flemish army, Willem van Gulik, personally employed diviners and necromancers \[sortilegos et necromanticos\], and conjured and consulted evil spirits. (\[Robert\] Gaguin and other French historians ascribe the same reason for the defeat and captivity of Ferdinand of Portugal, the Count of Flanders through his wife-\[namely\] the witchcraft \[sortilegium\] of his mother. The Flemings are silent on the subject). In connection with the year 1304, Jacob de Meyer also says this of the Willem I mentioned. \'Gossip has it that he was deceived by a necromancer from whom he had learned a \[magical\] technique which he promised would render him invisible whenever he wanted during danger, however great \[the danger might be\]. But the spell did him no good at all when it came to saving his life\'.

<div align="center"><b>Pergamum's Failed Magical Siege Relief</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 51-51</div>
The people of Pergamum once tried to free themselves from a siege by using magic, but their attempt came to nothing and their effort was wasted, as Paul the Deacon tells us in Book 20 of his history of Rome;

<div align="center"><b>Saracens' Capture of Pergamum</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 51-51</div>
and during the reign of Leo the Isaurian, Masalmas \[Maslama ibn Abd al-Malik\], the leader of the Saracens, seized Pergamum without any difficulty even though the citizens, who were relying on a certain magician, had made an appalling sacrifice to the Devil by cutting open a pregnant woman, removing the child alive, and boiling it in a cooking-pot while all the soldiers polluted the right-hand sleeve \[of their tunics\] with this wicked sacrifice. (For which, see Theophanes.)

<div align="center"><b>Bretislav's Battle and Stepmother's Curse</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 51-52</div>
I remember another noteworthy example of a \[magical\] stunt in connection with this topic, too. Bretislav, Duke of the Luczani-this is what Aeneas Silvius says-\'the founder of Bratislava, had started a war with his own nephew, Gremozislaus, Duke of Bohemia. The story is that when her stepson was about to go to war, there was a woman there who predicted that Bretislav would fall in battle and that the greater part of the populace would die with him, but that the young man \[her stepson\] could escape if he did as she told him. The youth replied that he would carry out her instructions, and so she told him he was to kill the first man he met, cut off both his ears, and stow them away in his wallet. Then he was to draw a cross on the ground with his sword between his horse's front legs, kiss it, and make a speedy retreat. Battle was joined on a plain called Thuscus, and Bretislav was killed. The young man who had done as his stepmother had told him escaped safely home, but he found that his wife, whom he loved dearly, had been killed. Her ears were missing and her breast had been pierced through. Aghast and unhappy, he realised that the ears of the enemy, which he had cut off, were those of his wife\'.

<div align="center"><b>Fridericus Stuphius's Magical War Defeat</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 52-52</div>
In Book 7 of the Annals of Bavaria, we read that the Fridericus Stuphius who falsely called himself Fridericus II and was preparing a war with the help of money \[he had got\] by magic, was overwhelmed, taken prisoner, and burned by Rudolf of Habsburg.

<div align="center"><b>Pope Innocent's Expulsion of Tuscan Magicians</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 52-52</div>
So, Pope Innocent acted no less judiciously than he did religiously when he took pains to expel from Rome magicians who had been invited there by pagans from Tuscany on the promise that they would free the city from a siege by Alaric, the same way they boasted they had rescued the town of Larina.

<div align="center"><b>Count of Allobroges Executes Magician</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 52-52</div>
Not unlike this was the good sense, (but more severe judicial punishment), in the case of the Count of the Allobroges. This man was a follower of the Duke of Anjou and had charge of the Castel dell'Ovo, a heavily fortified \[castle\] in Naples, washed on all sides by the sea. The Duke sent for him because he wanted his advice. Should a certain person's offer be accepted or not? The \'certain person\' was a magician who was boasting that he had surrendered this very fortress into the hands of Charles the Peacemaker who had withdrawn into it at the time, and that he was prepared to hand it over to the Angevins by using the same wily trick. He would create a thick cloud, \[he said\], build a bridge by using conjuring-tricks, and immediately extend it to the top of the fortress's walls; and once the defenders had been forced by fear to surrender, the soldiers who were going to come over the bridge would be so much on their guard that they would not be able to make any sign of the cross with their hand, their arms, their legs, or their weapons, and at that moment the bridge would be dispersed like smoke, and those who were crossing over it would die. The Count took note of this and argued that the Duke should send for the magician so that he could give him \[the Count\] more information about the whole business. The wretched man came and repeated his promise to the Count. The Count retorted that he would never commission him to convince such hard-working soldiers and experienced war-leaders that Charles the Peacemaker could not be vanquished without \[recourse to\] forbidden practices and such disgraceful behaviour. He then ordered that the magician be handed over to the public executioner and be beheaded. (These events took place in 1383.)

<div align="center"><b>Theophilus's Pact for Office</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 52-52</div>
Secondly, they adduce certain other examples such as that of Theophilus, the man who recovered the high office of Vidame with the help of an evil spirit, and similar \[stories\].

<div align="center"><b>Stuphius Pays Army with Magic Money</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 53-53</div>
Stuphius, (whom I have mentioned), paid an army with money \[he obtained\] by magic.

<div align="center"><b>Rémy's Deceptive Money Examples</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 53-54</div>
\[Nicholas\] Rémy confirms this in Book 1, chapter 4 of his Worship of Evil Spirits, with many examples from his personal experience \[as a judge\]. He says, \'In Dieuze on 30th September 1586, Sennel from Armentières received money-so she imagined-from an evil spirit and, in transports of delight, went home quickly to count it, but when she shook out her purse, she found nothing except bits of tile and pieces of charcoal. In Dieuze on 4th November 1586, Catharine from Metz found pig shit. Claudia Morèle in Serre, on 3rd December 1586: Benoît Drigie in Haraucourt, on 15th December 1585: Dominique Pétrone in Pagnysur-Moselle, on 20th October 1586, and very many others \[found\] tree leaves. In Masevaux, on 5th June, 1585, Jeanne le Ban found a gold coin wrapped in paper on the road, just as an evil spirit had predicted she would, and when she eagerly showed it to her husband she noticed, (not without embarrassment), that she had stumbled across a dark red pebble and not a gold coin after all; and what is more, at the very first touch it began to break up into little pieces. Catharina Ruffa from the Vallée de la Moselle, on 28th July 1586, was the only one to acknowledge she had once received three coins without being deceived by the \[evil spirit\]\'.

<div align="center"><b>Faustus and Agrippa's False Coins</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 54-54</div>
Rumour has it that when the magicians Faustus and Agrippa were on a journey, \[once they arrived at\] their lodging-houses they were in the habit of counting out coins which were real to look at, but after only one or two days those who had received them would find they were bits of horn or absolutely worthless scraps of rag.

<div align="center"><b>Moselle Sorceress's Scudi Deception</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 54-54</div>
\[An evil spirit\] had given an unfortunate, but well-born, married woman from Moselle, who was handed over to the flames at the confluence of the waters a few years ago because of the crime of sorcery \[crimen sortiarium \] a basket filled with what looked like scudi. She put them in a money box, but when she wanted to make use of them, she found horse shit in place of the coins.

<div align="center"><b>Basel Tailor's Snake-Woman Treasure Encounter</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 54-55</div>
\'In about 1520 in Basel, a simple-minded tailor with a stammer entered, (exactly how is not clear), the tunnel by which one can get into Augusta Raurica and, having gone further than anyone else had managed, he brought back a report of a number of extraordinary things he had seen. He lit a consecrated candle and went down into the tunnel. First, (he used to say), he had gone through an iron gate. Then he came out of one vault into another, and then into gardens which were in full, very beautiful bloom. In the middle he observed a hall, very richly decorated, and a young woman, very beautiful down to her private parts. Her head was circled with a golden crown, her hair was loose, and her lower half ended in \[the form of a frightening snake. She took him by the hand and led him to an iron chest. Two black hounds were lying on it and when they approached, \[the dogs\] set up a dreadful barking, but the young woman, with a menacing air, restrained them. Then she removed a bunch of keys she was in the habit of wearing tied round her neck, opened the chest, and brought out all kinds of gold, silver, and bronze coins, and he used to tell people that, as a result of the young woman's generosity, he had brought back not a few of these \[coins\] out of the tunnel itself.  
He added that the young woman told him more than once, with dreadful imprecations, that when she was born, she was already cursed by her royal descent, and had been changed into the kind of monster \[he saw before him\], and that she had no good reason to think she would recover her wholesomeness. But if she were kissed three times by a young man of undiminished propriety, her original form would be restored to her and the whole treasure which was lying hidden in that spot would be made over to her liberator as a dowry. He claimed he had indeed kissed the young woman twice, \[but\] that twice he observed her making such dreadful gestures in anticipation of her liberation that he was afraid she would tear him to pieces while he was still alive. Some of his grandchildren brought him to the theatre, but afterwards he could never find the entrance to the tunnel, let alone go into it\'.

<div align="center"><b>Basel Citizen's Deadly Tunnel Exploration</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 55-55</div>
After a few years another citizen from Basel went into the same tunnel with the intention of relieving his family's poverty but found nothing except the bones of human corpses. Seized by a sudden dread, he made a rapid exit and was affected by fits of madness until he died a wretched death on the third day afterwards.

<div align="center"><b>Margoulline Prior's Cave Death</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 55-55</div>
A few years ago, a prior from \[the abbey\] of Margoulline, along with two companions, went into a pit in the cave of King Sala near Puteoli in search of treasure, but died a wretched death and was not seen again, as \[Jacques de\] Villamont tells us in Book 1, chapter 23 of his Voyages;

<div align="center"><b>Pisa Treasure Hunters' Demonic Plagues</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 55-56</div>
and in his Inquiry into Evil Spirits, chapter 12, Andrea Cesalpino confirms these points when he writes as follows. \'I am certainly aware of many treasure hunters\', he says, \'because of their great skills, but I don't know any who have been successful in finding anything. In Pisa, during my lifetime, some of them tried to dig, but stopped because they were defeated by the difficulty of what they had undertaken. Finally, after a year, they started to dig in a spot where places built by Nero were said to have existed-the place is now occupied by a convent-and where there was an enormous pine tree. But they were so plagued by evil spirits that they were forced to give up the moment they began because they were afraid. (During this time, many of the sisters living there were obsessed by an evil spirit)\'.

<div align="center"><b>Saracen's Apulian Treasure Find</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 56-56</div>
One should also note in connection with this that certain people are said to have found treasures by the use of magic in which they are experts, and by clever guesswork-the Saracen, for example, in the time of Guiscard the Norman in Apulia, whose magical deed \[is described\] by \[Bartolomeo\] Platina and \[Pandolfo\] Collenuccio. But Petrarch ascribes it more to practical good sense, and as far as this is concerned one will refer to the maxim that everyone can weigh up the subject on a scale which is balanced true.

<div align="center"><b>Charles V Exiles Agrippa for Treasure Promises</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 57-57</div>
Let me close this chapter with the most powerful and most devout Emperor, Charles v, who made an excellent decision when he ordered Cornelius Agrippa and two other noblemen into exile from the court and his realms because they had offered people the hope of \[finding\] treasure with the help of magic.

<div align="center"><b>The Harmless Viper in the Peruvian Festival</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 57-58</div>
They also celebrate another festival no less superstitious than the one I mentioned earlier. In a small room they shut up a boy who has a beautiful face and is considered by the whole stronghold to be very good-looking, and order him to fast until his hair has grown long. Then magical conjurors, along with the elders, fetch him out and bring him to the house of one of his friends who has, for example, a very close connection with him. In the house, elderly women weep plaintively over the boy, and the common folk who are assembled and come together while this is happening, gradually cut off the boy\'s hair, starting at the front. Each person is offered a cooked goose. On the next day the magician dispatches \[some\] of the younger men to hunt wild animals, deputes others to make ready a suitable feast, orders girls to boil up some good wine and older folk to assemble musical instruments. From that moment, they start to play them, and from then on, those whom the magician sent away on his errands are free to pillage whatever they find out with anyone\'s house. They invite the neighbouring tribes to come to the feast wearing a variety of weapons.
The boy, who has been garlanded at the time of the feast, has an offensive weapon in one hand and a living viper hanging from the other. He sits in a chair which has been placed in splendidly decorated spot, and it is easy to deduce from what I have said that an evil spirit is at work, because the viper does not bite him. Several other dead vipers, too, are tied to the boy\'s body. These were caught by the young men when they went hunting and are placed on one side of the boy along with boiled and roasted geese. His other side is hemmed in by a large amount of wine. After this, soldiers invited to the feast come in according to their military rank and distinction, make a reverence to the viper, go up to the boy, drink the wine next to him, and take the food which has been prepared and placed next to the boy. Then they retrace their steps, make a reverence to the viper again, and ask for a second helping of food and drink. They spend three whole days in this fashion, after which they kill the viper. Then the women, (who have been shut up indoors for the whole period of the festival), come out shouting, \"Victory!\" During this kind of celebration an evil spirit frequently appears, urging \[people\] to drink, eat, and spend the festal days in cheerfulness and merriment. 

<div align="center"><b>The Magician Killed by a Snake in Salzburg</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 58</div>
The well-known example of the magician from Salzburg makes this point. When he was trying to force all the snakes-and this means one thousand of them!-into a furnace, he was finally killed by one huge old \[snake\] which struck out at him. An evil spirit, of course, had driven the rest of them into the furnace, but was unwilling to compel this one, preferring to rouse it against the magician. After all, if the rest of them had been driven into the furnace by the power of an incantation, that same power would have extended to this snake as well.

<div align="center"><b>The Greek Magician and the Ferocious Bull</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 59</div>
\[Paolo\] Grillando quite rightly says at the end of Question 8, \'On another occasion, during the time of Hadrian VI, before he arrived in the city as Pontiff, I saw a Greek magician who had a remarkable reputation because with words alone he had restrained the violence of a very ferocious bull which was part of a herd living in a wooded place. He rooted \[the bull\] to the spot like this, as I said, and, having humbled it, got hold of it by the horns and, with a quite fragile piece of rope, (which, however, had been made by magic), tethered it and led it where he wanted, in the middle of the night, for about four or five miles. This was seen by two hundred and more men\'.

<div align="center"><b>The Dogs Found in the Rock Quarry</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 60</div>
I absolutely believe this is what happened in the case of the dogs William of Newburgh spoke about as follows. \'While \[workmen\] were splitting open a huge rock in a particular quarry with their iron tools, two dogs appeared. They filled to capacity the space available to them in that rock which had no hole at all through which they could breathe\', (meaning \"perceptible hole\").

\'They seemed to belong to the breed of dogs known as \"greyhounds\", but they looked ferocious, smelled bad, and had no hair. It is said one of them died not long afterwards, but they say that Henry, Bishop of Winchester, played with the other one, which had a quite extraordinary appetite, for a good many days\', (History of the English, Book 1, chapter 28).

<div align="center"><b>The Toad in the Stone with a Gold Chain</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 60</div>
He follows this by saying that a toad was found in another stone with a small gold chain round its neck. I think an evil spirit added the chain and that the toad itself could have been born there, just as enormous flints have been found in cola-mines in Liège, inside which live toads have been formed and were lying hidden as though in their birth-canal.

<div align="center"><b>The Giant Monster from Brazil</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
Evil spirits can also produce a number of strange monsters, such as the one from Brazil which was seventeen palms\' tall, covered in lizard skin, with swollen breasts, lions\' limbs, and staring, flame-coloured, glittering eyes, and a similarly coloured tongue, (something like the monsters with half-human faces which were captured in the forests of Saxony in 1240).

<div align="center"><b>Alcippe's Birth of an Elephant</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
This is why Alcippe gave birth to an elephant at the time of the Marsic War \[91-88 

<div align="center"><b>The Swiss Woman's Birth of a Lion</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
This is why a Swiss woman bore a lion in 1278.

<div align="center"><b>The Woman in Pavia Birthing a Cat</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
This is why a woman produced a cat in Pavia in 1470,

<div align="center"><b>The Woman in Brescia Birthing a Dog</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
and another woman a dog in Brescia.

<div align="center"><b>The Augsburg Woman's Multiple Monstrous Birth</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
This is why, in 1531, a woman in Augsburg in one and the same birth first produced a human head wrapped in its membranes, then a snake with two feet, and thirdly an entire pig.

<div align="center"><b>The Woman and the Monkeys on the Uninhabited Island</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 61-62</div>
One \[story\] which caps the rest, extraordinary though they may be, is the one reported by Castanheda in his Annals of Portugal. Because of a crime \[she had committed\], a woman was taken away by ship to an uninhabited island, and when she had been put ashore there a troop of monkeys, in which the place abounded, stood round her, growling. Then one who was rather big, to whom the rest of them gave way, arrived on the scene, took her gently by the hand, and led her away into an enormous cave. There he and the rest of the troop set before her a large number of apples, nuts, and various roots and, with a nod, invited her to eat. When she had finished, the wild creature forced her to have sex with him, an outrage he continued without interruption for many days, the result being that she gave birth to two children. The unhappy woman how much more preferable would death have been!-lived like this for several years until God in His mercy sent a ship there from Portugal. The soldiers came ashore to fetch water from a spring which was next to the cave, and by good fortune the monkey was not there. The woman came running up to the first human beings she had seen for a long time, threw herself at their feet, and begged them to take her away from \[the monkey\'s\] villainy and \[her\] appalling slavery. They took pity on her misfortune and agreed to take her on board with them. But-and I\'d like you to notice this point-the monkey bore down on them and, with uncouth gestures and deep growls, called back his \'wife\' (nonwife). When he saw that the sails had been spread, he ran forward and held up one of the children to its mother, threatening that if she did not come back, he would hurl it into the sea; and he was not slow in carrying out his threat. Then he dashed back to the cave and just as quickly returned to the shore, holding up the other child, made his threat, and drowned it, after which he pursued the ship closely, raging all the time, and swimming after it as long as he could until the waves proved too strong for him. The whole of Portugal can bear witness to this very famous incident. The King condemned the woman to be burned in Lisbon, but because of the prayers of certain people who begged for her life, he commuted the death sentence to perpetual imprisonment.

<div align="center"><b>The Gothic Kings Descended from a Bear and a Virgin</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
Are you astonished, Reader? Look for yourself at Saxo Grammaticus and \[Johannes\] Magnus and \[Olaus\] Magnus, Bishops of Uppsala. They tell a story about Gothic kings who are descended from \[the union of\] a bear and a noble virgin,

<div align="center"><b>The Pegusian and Sianitan Tribes Descended from a Dog and a Woman</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 61</div>
and João de Barros \[who tells us\] about the Pegusian and Sianitan tribes of India who are descended from a dog which mated with a woman.

<div align="center"><b>The Hybrid Children from Men and Monkeys in the Andes</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 62</div>
A story told by the judicious historian Pedro Cieza \[de León\] confirms this opinion. He tells us that the men of the Andes are accustomed to have sex with huge monkeys, and that the children born from this union have a human head and private parts, while the rest of them resembles a monkey. They are wild and ugly, they do not speak but shriek in a terrifying manner, and the Indians themselves consider them to be monsters.

<div align="center"><b>The Indian Woman Birthing Monsters to a Dog</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 62</div>
Lastly, in the province of Aulaga, an Indian woman bore three monsters to a dog. (See his History of Peru, Part 1, chapter 95.)

<div align="center"><b>The Boy Born from a Man and a Cow in the Spanish Netherlands</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 62</div>
To these examples I add a story which is entirely true. Right here in the Spanish Netherlands there was an abominable individual who had sex with a cow. People later saw that the cow had become pregnant, and after a few months she gave birth to a male offspring-not a calf, but a boy. More than one person was present to see him falling from the womb of his mother, the cow.

They picked him up from the ground and handed him over to a wet nurse. The boy grew up, was baptised and instructed in the precepts of the Christian religion, dedicated himself to the practice of religion and, on his father\'s behalf, earnestly devoted himself to works of penitence. To be sure, he was a complete human being, but one whose mind had bovine leanings which made him want to graze in the fields and chew the cud.

<div align="center"><b>The Frieslanders Punished with Animal Tails</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 64</div>
It is certain that a monster is sometimes born as a punishment from God. The punishment meted out to the Frieslanders of Dokkum because they murdered the saintly bishop, Boniface, and his companions tells us as much. \'God,\' says Kempius, \'has avenged the shameful death of these saints upon the children \[of those people\'s\] children, with the result that to this day random members of the families whose ancestors carried out such a wicked crime have thick whitish hair on the back of their head, rather like a brute animal\'s tail.

<div align="center"><b>The Family Born with Tails for Insulting St Thomas of Canterbury</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 64</div>
In Book 13 of his English History, Polydore Vergil writes to this effect. King Henry II was beginning to reveal himself as an enemy of the holy man, St Thomas of Canterbury. \'\[St Thomas\] started to be so slighted, despised, and hated by the common people that on one occasion when he came to Strode, a village on the bank of the River Medway, a river which flows near Rochester, the inhabitants of that area, eager to inflict some dishonour on the good Father they so despised, did not hesitate to cut off the tail of the horse he was riding, thus bringing down a perpetual disgrace on themselves: because afterwards, in accordance with God\'s will, it turned out that all the members of the family which had committed that crime were born with tails, as though they were brute beasts. But that mark of infamy, together with the family of those individuals, has long since disappeared.\'

<div align="center"><b>The Nobleman's Child with Dog Features</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 64</div>
A further passage: I find in Book 2 Thomas of Cantimpré\'s work entitled Bees that a certain nobleman profaned feast days by going hunting, and his wife bore him a child with the distended jaws of a dog and the long floppy ears of \'hounds\', as they call them.

<div align="center"><b>The Evil Spirit Corcoton and Horned Children in Hispaniola</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 66-66</div>
In chapter 27, part 2 of his history of Peru, Cieza writes that in Hispaniola an evil spirit called Corcoton was in the habit of having sex with women, and the children born as a result of this had two horns.

<div align="center"><b>The Woman Punished in Brabant for Bearing a Child to an Evil Spirit</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 66-66</div>
Six years ago, in the leading town of Brabant, a woman was punished because she had borne a child to an evil spirit, and Luis Molina, a theologian of the Society of Jesus, has published that this has also happened during the present day. Several other writers from different nations, too, have confirmed it and reported examples of it.

<div align="center"><b>The Bavarian Nobleman Deceived by His Returned Wife</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 69-69</div>
What others say about a Bavarian nobleman is not dissimilar. He was bearing the death of his wife without much fortitude, [but] one night the woman came back to him and said she had returned to life. She convinced her husband [of this] and conceived children by him. At length, however-and this is something she had predicted-she vanished, leaving behind her woman\'s dress with her husband who did not refrain from uttering loud reproaches and blasphemies. An evil spirit thus cheated the nobleman and foisted upon him children it had stolen from someone else.

<div align="center"><b>The Beggar and the Devil Child in Galicia and Asturias</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 69-69</div>
Fifteen years ago, a beggar was carrying a child very painfully on his shoulders through Galicia and the Asturias in Spain, and I think [the child was one of these changelings. One of my Jesuit brethren found him en route near a river and, moved by pity, took a back seat on his mule so that he could take them across. The strong beast was scarcely able to land them on the other bank. A little later, he caught hold of the beggar who told him that the child was not a child but a devil who had promised him he would inveigle everyone into giving him alms, as long as he would carry [the devil] round in the guise of an ailing child swathed in bandages.

<div align="center"><b>The Young Girl Impregnated by Her Father's Nocturnal Emission</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 70-70</div>
St Thomas [Aquinas] writes-that perhaps a woman can conceive without a miracle and with her virginity in every way intact, \'as we are told happened\', (he says), \'to a young girl who was just reaching the age of puberty. In order to guard her modesty, her father used to keep her in his bed. [But] when he defiled himself while he was dreaming, his semen descended into his daughter\'s womb and therefore the girl became pregnant. This was the result, and in his commentary on Genesis 19, [Alonso Tostado of Ávila] claims that an evil spirit can make the same thing happen.

<div align="center"><b>Discoveries of Giant Corpses in Sicily</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 71-71</div>
The subject is clearly an extensive one. I shall write down brief extracts [from Fazello]. In 1342, a corpse was found in a cave on Mt Eryx. It was of a quite extraordinary size and part of the back of its skull had the capacity of several Sicilian pecks. (Boccaccio mentions it, too, in Book 4, chapter 28 of his Genealogy of the Gods). In 1516 in the territory of Mazzarino, masons dug up the corpse of someone about twenty cubits tall. Its head was the size of a large round cask, and each of its molars weighed five ounces. [Fazello] says he saw them, and that he had in possession not a few other teeth belonging to giants which were dug up in Melilli, [each] weighing four ounces, and that he also possessed the almost preternaturally wide shoulder-blade of another giant from Hyccara [Carini]. In 1547, Simeone Pigliono gave him two other molars, each weighing four ounces, from a corpse eighteen cubits tall, [which had been found] near Palermo. In the following year, another corpse was discovered there by Giorgio Adorno, twenty cubits tall; and in 1550 a corpse was found at Calatrasi about twenty-two cubits tall, whose head measured about ten feet round. [Fazello] adds many similar [examples], but there is no reason for me to spend time enumerating each one of them. He notes the fact that although the rest of the bones fell to dust at a touch, the teeth remained intact. They were like ours, but bigger, whitish in colour, and free from decay. One used to see this long ago in the tooth from Antwerp as well.

<div align="center"><b>St Augustine's Sighting of a Giant Molar in Utica</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 71-71</div>
St Augustine himself writes that he saw a molar on the shore of Utica so big it could match a hundred ordinary molars, (The City of God, Book 15, chapter 9).

<div align="center"><b>The Province of Dwarfs Found in Peru</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 71-71</div>
Fr. Ruiz writes in his letters, which I read in 1601, that a province of dwarfs was found in Peru in 1600.

<div align="center"><b>Nonnosus's Encounter with Short-Statured Human-Like Creatures</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 72-72</div>
In his Library, folio 3, Photius refers to what Nonnosus says about these dwarfs. \'While Nonnosus was sailing from Pharsa, he came to the furthest of the islands where a remarkable, unheard of thing happened. He came across some [creatures] which had the shape and appearance of human beings but were very short in stature and black in colour. Their whole body was covered in thick hair. Their women came along with them, and their children who were even shorter than the men. They were all naked, except that the older men and women concealed their shameful parts with a small piece of skin. They did not seem to be fierce or wild. They did have a human voice, but their language was unknown to everyone who lived round about them\'.

<div align="center"><b>The Peasant's Theft of the Enchanted Cup</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 76-76</div>
In Yorkshire, not far from where I was born, something extraordinary happened which I knew about from the time I was a child. Several miles away from the sea in the east there is a village, and next to it are the well-known waters commonly known as the "Vipse".

A peasant from this village, who had gone to see a friend in the next village, was returning, somewhat less than sober, late at night and (listen to this!) he heard the sounds of people singing and having a good time [which were coming] from a nearby hillock. (I have seen this hillock quite often. It is two or three furlongs away from the village.) Astonished that anyone would be rupturing the silence in the dead of night with cheerful celebrations, he wanted to investigate more closely and noticed an opening in the side of the hillock. He went up to it, looked inside, and saw a big house, brightly lit, full of men and women who were sitting down to what looked like a formal dinner. One of the waiters noticed him standing in the doorway and offered him a cup.

He took it, but deliberately refrained from drinking, poured the contents away, but kept the cup, and left quickly. The removal of the cup caused an uproar at the party and the guests chased after him, but he escaped because of the speed of the mule he was riding and retreated to his village along with his remarkable plunder. In the end, this vessel, which [was made] of an unknown material and had a strange colour and unusual shape, was offered as a gift to the King of England, Henry the Elder, and then, after he had refused it, to the Queen's brother David, (namely, the King of Scots) and was kept for very many years as one of the treasures of Scotland. A few years ago, I am reliably informed, William, King of Scots, surrendered it to Henry II who wanted to see it', [History] of English Affairs Book 1, chapter 38.

<div align="center"><b>Guillaume Edelin's Besom Ride to His Lover</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 76-76</div>
Robert Gaguin, General of the Trinitarian Order, writes as follows in Book 10 of his history of the French. 'At that time, Guillaume Edelin, a learned theologian and Prior of St Germain-enLaye, was sentenced to perpetual imprisonment in Évreux for the crime of breaking his monastic vows. This distinguished man was in love with a woman and was unable to enjoy her companionship. So he invited an evil spirit to be his patron, and worshipped him under the guise of a ram. Later [the spirit] taught him how to pick up a besom and put it between his thighs, as though it were a horse. It would then take him instantaneously where he wanted [to go]'.

<div align="center"><b>The Boy's Terror at the Avignon Synagogue</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 76-77</div>
Among the Avignon witches [striges], (as [Sébastien] Michaëlis tells us in his Discourse of Spirits, annotation 6), they arrested a boy who told the judges that his father has taken him to the 'synagogue', (which is what they call their meeting), and that while he was there he had seen them carry out many wicked, dreadful things. In consequence, [he said], he was terrified and crossed himself and said Jesus, what is the meaning of this?' As soon as he had said it, the entire mob disappeared, and he found he was alone in that place. Next day he came back to his home which was about three miles away from the 'synagogue', and denounced his father to the judges, and this is why the boy's neighbours gave him the name 'masquillon', that is, 'little sorcerer' [sortiarius]. This boy was still a prisoner in Avignon when Michaëlis was writing his account in 1582.

<div align="center"><b>The Witch's Ride on the Goat to Benevento</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 77-78</div>
Listen to another eye-witness [account] taken from [Paolo] Grillando whose words I am quite happy to write down. 'When this', (that is, of course, homage), 'has been done, he,' (the principal evil spirit), 'appoints an evil spirit to take custody of the woman, whom he must never leave, but serve her every wish; and whenever she happens to come to the theatricals, he will tell the woman this, bring her there, and give her instruction. Actually, this evil spirit keeps her company the way a husband does his wife. They often go to those assemblies, where a very large number of women get together, and they do not see the things I have mentioned mentally or intellectually or apparently.

They come to the foresaid places in their real, natural person in the following manner and ordernamely, first of all, one or two days before the assembly takes place, [the woman] is informed by the evil spirit who has been given custody of the woman how to prepare herself at such and such a time to come to the show. If the woman were to have a good reason which prevented [her attendance], he takes her and, provided her reason is genuine, excusable, and one she can legitimately offer as an excuse, she is given a hearing. Otherwise, if she were to pretend to have a reason which was not a reason for non-attendance, she would not be taken there against her will, but would stay at home. Nevertheless, as punishment for her lie, the evil spirit would torture her so vigorously and distress her so powerfully in mind and body with very great, continual pains and sickness internal and external, that she would have no peace inwardly day or night but would always be suffering affliction.

Everything she might do would have no effect and would come to nothing as she did it, to the point where, to make all her sufferings cease, she would confess her fault and promise, even to the extent of swearing an oath, that she would not refuse any more to go [to the meetings]. Now, when she promised to go, the moment the night and hour arrived, a voice like that of a human would summon her. [It came] from the evil spirit himself, although they do not call him an evil spirit, but 'little teacher'. (Some women [call] their teacher "Martinet" or "Little Martin".) she had been summoned this way, she would pick up a small box of ointment and smear certain parts and bits of her body with it. After doing this, she would leave the house and would always find her "little teacher" waiting for her at the door, in the form of a goat. The woman said she would mount on his back and cling hard with her hands on to his hair. The goat would immediately rise through the air and, in a very short time, carry her off to an almond-tree in Benevento where he would put her down gently', etc. [Question 7, no. 28]. It is worth noting that the rest [of the passage] agrees with what the other scholars I cited have written usually takes place in a meeting.

<div align="center"><b>Lucrezia Abandoned Naked in the Thorn Field</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 81-82</div>
In Book 2, question 7 of his Divinatory Magicians, Paolo Grillando relates that in 1524 a woman called Lucrezia was brought before him in his capacity as inquisitor. While she was being taken home from a witches' meeting [congregatione ludorum] immediately after dawn, she heard the bell being rung to call the people to morning prayer, and all of a sudden, the evil spirit who was carrying her abandoned her in a field full of thorns. By chance a young man she knew was passing through [the field] and the wretched woman call to him by his name. The young man, seeing she was completely naked, was afraid to approach unless she covered her private parts with her thighs and unbound hair. She kept on coaxing him gently and finally he came near her and asked how she was feeling and why she was there. At first, she hid all the other details and told him a lot of lies until the young man did not believe her and said he would not help her unless she told him the truth. She made him promise to keep it secret and [then] confessed what had happened to her and why it had happened. The young man took her home and she showered him with presents, but in the end, forgetting he had given his word [to keep quiet], he told the story to one person and then to another and so, little by little, the story leaked out. The woman was arrested, and the young man forced to provide evidence of the truth. (All this is what Grillando tells us.)

<div align="center"><b>The Husband's Discovery and Journey to the Benevento Sabbat</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 81-82</div>
He adds another [exemplary story] which is as follows. A woman from the diocese of Sabina used to profess this devilish craft, and because her husband had his suspicions about it, he questioned her on several occasions, but she always denied [any knowledge of it]. Her husband, however, continued to be suspicious and anxiously kept on pressing her for the truth. He acted in such a cunning way that one night he saw her smearing herself with an ointment. Once she had done this, he saw her leaving very quickly, like a bird, swooping down from the upper storey of the house to the lower. Her husband followed her in order to see where she was going, [but then] he saw her no longer, and when he came to the front door of the house, he found it closed. This caused him great astonishment. The next day, however, the husband questioned his wife again about what he was anxious to know, and she, (as one might expect), kept on saying she had no idea [what he was talking about]. But so that the woman would not be able to deny things any more, her husband told her plainly everything he had seen her do the previous night and then gave her a good beating with his cudgel, threatening to beat her harder if she did not tell the truth. But he did promise to forgive her if she told him everything.

So, the woman understood she could not keep her secret any longer, told her husband the truth frankly, and asked his forgiveness, which her husband granted on condition she take him to gatherings of this kind. In order to make sure of his forgiveness, she readily promised what he asked, and fulfilled her promise with Satan's permission. So, she took her husband to the place where the entertainments [ludi] were taking place. He had a good look at the sport, the dances, and everything else, and finally sat down at table with everyone else to eat. He thought the food was tasteless and asked for salt. There was none on the table and although he asked for it again and again, he was never given any. Finally, after he persisted in asking and had waited for a very long time, he was given salt, at which he said, 'Now praise be to God the salt has arrived!' When he said these words, the evil spirits, who run away when they hear God being praised, made an immediate retreat. Everyone else disappeared, the lamps were extinguished, and he stayed there, alone and naked, until dawn broke and he saw some shepherds.

He asked them the name of the district in which he was, and they replied, 'the countryside of Benevento in the Kingdom of Naples'. This was a hundred miles away from where the man lived and in consequence, even though he was rich, he was forced to beg along the road so that he could get back to his own home. The moment he arrived he accused his wife of being a vampire-witch [lamiae], and related everything that had happened, (the way it has been told here), to the judges and they, upon investigating the whole business thoroughly, as was proper, found that everything I have been saying was true, and the woman's confession confirmed it.

<div align="center"><b>The Daughter Transported to Her Cousin's Bedroom in Venice</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 82-83</div>
The Franciscan Alfonso a Castro, in his chapter 16, adds that he can give an account of many other pieces of evidence which he had come to know, from entirely trustworthy witnesses, [referred to events which] had taken place in Spain. Bartolomeo della Spina, Master of the Sacred Apostolic Palace, gives us examples which are no less reliable in chapter 17 ff. of his [Inquiry into] Night-Flying Witches, and I shall write down one of them. 'A young woman', he says, 'who was living with her mother in Bergamo, was found one night in Venice in the bedroom of a kinsman. She was discovered naked in the morning and was recognised because she was his cousin. When they asked her how she had come there, or what she was doing there and why, she started to wail, and after they got her dressed, she suddenly told them the following story. "Last night, I was lying awake in bed and saw my mother, who thought I was asleep, get out of bed, pull of her underwear, and smear her body with an ointment she took out of a little pot she removed from underneath some bricks.

Then she took a stick she had ready there, climbed on it the way one mounts a horse, and was carried away out of the window, and after that I did not see her at all. Then I got out of bed, too, and anointed my body the way I had seen my mother do and was carried away out of the window. I was immediately brought here where I saw my mother waiting in ambush for a boy who was lying in the bed. I was frightened when I saw that my mother was also startled by my arrival and was threatening to attack me, so I invoked the name of the Lord Jesus and the Blessed Virgin, and after that I did not see my mother and was left there, naked and alone". Once the girl had told her story, her cousin wrote down everything she had said for the Father Inquisitor in Bergamo.

[The Inquisitor] arrested the woman and when she was put to the torture, she confessed everything and added that she had been carried off by her evil spirit more than fifty times so that she could kill the foresaid cousin's child, but that she never been successful because his parents always protected him well with the sign of the cross and holy prayers'.

<div align="center"><b>The Coal-Miner's Pursuit to the Count's Wine-Cellar</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 83-83</div>
[Bartolomeo] also adds another example, and I shall write this one down, too. 'One Antonio Leo, a coal-miner from Ferrara [a Montagna] in Valtellina, told me this year that he had found out the things written below from someone in his home district, to whom they had actually happened. Because of what many people told him, this man became suspicious that his wife was going on a "voyage", (that is, to a meeting of witches [sagarum]), 'while he was asleep at night. One night he pretended to be fast asleep and observed his wife get out of bed, anoint herself with ointment from a small pot she had kept hidden, and straight away she was nowhere to be seen. Her husband was stunned and, drawn by curiosity, got out of bed and did the same as his wife. Immediately, (so it seemed to him), he was carried off up the chimney-which was how, (he thought), his wife had also risen upwards-and was brought to the wine-cellar of a noble Count.There he found his wife along with several other people. When she saw him, his wife immediately made a sign and she and the others left, leaving her husband in that place. He was found by the house-servants next morning and, amid uproar, arrested as a thief and brought before the Count. He was given time to speak and gave a vigorous account of what had happened, as a result of which his wife was arraigned before the inquisitor and at length confessed and paid the appropriate penalty for her crimes.'

<div align="center"><b>Claude Choteau's Encounter with the Morèle Cleft Dancers</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 83-84</div>
This is what Bartolomeo della Spina tells us. Still better witnesses are three examples of witch-transportation Nicholas Rémy adds to these three Italian ones in his Worship of Evil Spirits Book 1, chapter 14, of which I cannot deprive the reader. This is what he writes. 'In May 1589, villagers were celebrating a rural festival in Luz, which is a village not without a certain reputation in the foothills of the Vosges. Claude Choteau was coming back from this village at nightfall to a neighbouring village called Wisembach and had already climbed up a good part of the hill which rises between the two villages when he was battered by a sudden whirlwind. Astonished, he stood still and looked around [to see if there was any reason anywhere for such an uncommon occurrence, since the air was perfectly calm and still everywhere else. He noticed six masked women in a retired spot-the locals call it the Morèle cleft. They were dancing round a table set with a lot of gold and silver, throwing their heads all over the place as they did so and [acting like] people who are driven hither and thither by fits of madness.

There was also a man on a black bull, standing near them. He was looking at them as though he were someone in the street with nothing else to do. In the meantime, [Claude] stood where he was while he recovered himself and looked at the whole scene more closely. While he was doing this, the entire mob disappeared in an instant, as if it withdrew from his sight. So, his fear left him, and he resumed his journey. He had already got over the summit of the hill when would you believe it!-those women started following him closely behind, throwing their heads about as before and preserving a deep or agreed silence. A man with a coal-black face was walking ahead of them. His hands were curved, like grappling-hooks, and he was preparing to let them loose on [Claude's] face, had not [Claude] not drawn his sword and thwarted him. But because he did so, [the man] stopped taunting him and, as though in fear of his life, vanished.

Nevertheless, the women made another appearance and the man who, as I said, was seated on a bull and was watching their dances, was with them. Choteau, who had now become more confident, came closer to him and said, "Oh, there you are, Didier Gaxet!"-that being the man's name-Please, protect me if you can. Honestly, I give you my solemn word I'll not say anything at all about what I have seen". Hardly had he finished speaking when a whirlwind or cloud swept round him again, and when at last he spun himself round and freed himself from it, he found he was alone on the ground in a place a long way away from the path. But, by retracing his steps to where he had last been on it, he stumbled on to it and took himself back home as fast as he could.

Three days after giving this testimony, he was summoned before the investigating judge again and added the following to the very large number of details he had given earlier. He remembered, (he said), that when he had gone closer to the table to see what kind of a banquet has been set out on it, an evil spirit had immediately flown at his face with his nails, and while he was engaged in defending himself with his sword, he had been lifted up once again by a rather violent wind and carried off to the Combrimons Falls, a spot not less than two hundred paces away.

Lest anyone think this was perhaps the raving of a drunk frightened by the loneliness of the night and the place, in Sandeodat, on 22nd May, 1589, one of those women, Barbelline Caxet, had also said the same thing to the investigating [judge] a month earlier in almost the same words, adding that Desiré Gaxet and his wife had given Choteau two measures of wheat and two cows' milk cheeses to keep his mouth shut about what he had seen. Consequently, when the two of them were brought face to face, they agreed in every particular except one which was that Barbelline said the evil spirit had not tried to lay hands on Choteau because he had gone near the table-this was a lie he had told during his statement-but because he had been on the point of stealing a gold cup from it'.

<div align="center"><b>Johann von Hembach's Fall from the Meeting Tree</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 84-85</div>
Rémy gives another example of the same thing. 'Johann von Hembach had scarcely ceased being a youngster when his mother, a worker of poisonous magic [venefica], took him with her to nocturnal meetings of evil spirits and, on the grounds that he knew how to play the pipes, told him to play them and to climb a tree close by so that he could be heard more clearly. He did so and, being free to have a good look at their jumping and dancing, was astonished at their noveltyeverything there was so preposterous and ridiculous-and casually said, "Good God, where has this silly, stupid mob come from?" Scarcely had he said this than he fell to the ground and hurt one of his shoulders. He begged those who were standing near him to help him but found himself alone.

He talked about this quite openly, and people had different opinions about it-some maintained it was a vision, others that it had actually taken place. But them something happened which removed people's doubts, because one of the women who had taken part in the dancing, Catherine Prevost, was arrested not long afterwards in Freissen on 2nd September 1589 on suspicion of casting lots for divination [sortilegii]. She gave an account of the whole business as written above without being interrogated first, unaware of the version Von Hembach had already propagated. Otilla Kervers and Agnes Eisarbz, who were sentenced for [practising] poisonous magic [veneficii] the following year-Kervers in Werderst on 6th and 7th August 1590 and Eisarbz in Dieuze on 3rd November 1590-each in turn made the same declaration and in addition made it evidentially stronger by giving the name, (Mayebuch), of the place where it happened'.

<div align="center"><b>Nicole Lagbernhard's Encounter with Cloven-Footed Dancers</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 85-85</div>
The third [example] is not only a clear piece of testimony, but also makes as clear as daylight what had happened. It goes as follows. 'Nicole Lagbernhard was coming back from the mill in Guermange to Assenoncourt on 25th July 1590 and was making her way, at full noon, next to a hedge which was part of a wood, when she noticed men and women dancing in a circle and jumping about in the neighbouring ploughed field. But because they were doing so with their backs turned to each other, contrary to the usual way other people did it, she took a closer look and saw that, in addition to this, there were, mixed with those who were dancing and jumping in the circle, some who were deformed because they had the feet of goats and oxen. Almost dead with fear, she began to invoke the saving name of Jesus, (as people do when something unlucky and inauspicious threatens them), and to ask Him humbly that it might be possible for her to return home safe and sound. At that, all the dancers seemed to vanish, except for one called Petter Gross-Petter who was borne quickly into the air. But a small brush, such as bakers usually use to clean their oven before they put their bread in it, was seen to escape [his grasp] and fall; and while this was going on, Nichole, too, was swept up by a rather strong wind, started panting and puffing, and could scarcely draw breath, and when she got home she lay in bed, ill, for three whole days.

She and her neighbours talked about this, and the news spread through the whole district. At the first opportunity, the Petter I mentioned rather hot-headedly brought an action before a judge, so that he might not seem to be acknowledging and confessing to such a great crime by keeping quiet about it. In the end, however, he made up his mind to abandon [his action] because he was afraid that, since he was having no success with his case, he would be creating greater danger for himself. So, after due reflection, he abandoned it. This increased the suspicion against him even more, and many people considered it was because he had a bad conscience that he was [now] taking no notice of a wrong of which he had at first been so acutely aware.

So, the judge investigated his life and behaviour the more carefully, and when he discovered, by means of reliable tokens of proof, that the case [against Petter] was not without grounds, he arrested him and prevailed on him to co-operate and confess his crime and, in the end, to expose and convict the others who had taken part in the crime with him. Among them were Barbelline, wife of Jean Latome [who was tried] at Dieuze on 27th January 1591 and Mayette, wife of Laurent Grosmaire, likewise [tried] at Dieuze on 16th February 1591. They acknowledged, separately but in the same words, that the information their accomplice Petter had disclosed about the Sabbat, when they danced back-toback along with cloven-footed individuals in their midst, was true.

On 1st March, 1591, a third person, Jean Michel, a herdsman from Dieuze, agreed with [what] these women [had said in their] confession, and added that while all this was taking place he was doing the job of pipe-player by putting to his mouth a sheep-hook someone happened to offer him, and letting his fingers flutter over it, as though he were playing notes on real pipes. When Nicole, (as has been said), full of fear, called upon Jesus and made the sign of the cross, he fell headlong from the rather high branch of the oak-tree on which he was sitting. Then he was caught up in a whirlwind and carried off to a meadow called "Weiller" where, not long before, he had left his flock grazing.

But in the end, the weightiest and most powerful piece of evidence relating to this incident was that the day after Nichole had told [people about it], the place where they had been dancing in a circle appeared to have been trodden down in a ring-the kind horse-trainers have to exercise their horses in a circuit. It was also chequered with recent traces of hooves belong to goats and oxen, and these remained visible until the field was brought under the plough and furrowed the following winter. Verbal evidence was given by Nicel Klein, Didier Vernex, and Gaspar Schneider, and they were all brought quickly to court and questioned about it by the judge.'

<div align="center"><b>The Servant's Hay Trough Transportation to the Wyck Cavern</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 86-87</div>
No less confirmatory is another [example] from Baudouin van Ronss's Letters on Medicine, [chapter] 50 which tells us something which had happened in Holland in his own day.²⁴² 'In the village of Oostbroek, not far from Utrecht, there was a widow who had taken in a male servant because her situation demanded she get help with her household expenses. Quite often, in the dead of night, as soon as the house-servants had gone to bed, he would look through the lattice [over his window] in the usual nosy way servants have, and see his mistress come into the stable, make her way to a specific, pre-arranged spot, and then stretch out her hands and grasp hold of the hay trough next to the horse's stall. Wondering what on earth she meant by this behaviour, he decided to try doing the same thing at the same time, without his mistress's knowledge.

So, on the spur of the moment when his mistress came and did what she usually did and seemed to have gone away, he followed quickly, took a good look at the spot and then, imitating his mistress's example, threw his arms round the hay trough. Immediately he was snatched up into the air and carried off to the town of Wyck, to a cavern hidden underground, where he found a company of female workers of harmful magic [maleficarum] talking to each other about the acts of harmful magic [maleficiis] they had done. His mistress and her companions were astonished at her servant's unexpected appearance, and she asked him what trick he had used to get there in an instant, and why he had done so.

He told her, step by step, how he had done it and she immediately began to turn incandescent with rage, afraid that the result would be that those clandestine meetings at night would be brought to an end. So, she decided to talk to her partners in crime and [ask them] what they thought should be done in this awkward situation. Finally, they agreed among themselves to be friendly and make an exception of him, but that they would make him promise and swear an oath to remain silent and not communicate or reveal to anyone the secrets which he-beyond his merit or comprehension-might happen to see. He, for his part, made no such promise, but spoke to them in soothing tones and, with a view to getting them to behave with greater mildness, pretended that all his desires were fixed on one thing-that they would be gracious enough at some future date to let him attend their "witches' guild" [sortilegiarum collegio].

In the meantime, while they were discussing this, the minutes glided by, and it was time to go. Once again, his mistress stirred up uncertainty about whether he should be sent home, which would be dangerous for the whole assembly, or killed for the general good. In the end they all agreed to choose the more merciful of the two and decided to send him back once they had bound him by an oath [of silence]. His mistress undertook this task and promised that, once he had given his word, she would take him home on her shoulders. They got themselves ready and were [then] carried swiftly through the air by a wind from the east. But now, listen to this! They had covered a good part of their journey when there came into view a lake covered with a jungle of reeds. This provided the maleficent old woman with an opportunity and, fearful lest the youth repent of his (pretended) initiation into their ghastly festivals, she saw a way of getting rid of him. She shot forward suddenly and threw him off her back, hoping, one must suspect, that the violence of the fall would kill him and that he would be swallowed up on the depths of the lake. But how very merciful is God, who does not seek the death of a sinner, but rather that he be converted and live, because God put a stop to the madwoman's

intention and did not allow the innocent youth to be drowned. Indeed, He gave the young man the considerably fairer boon of living right up to the present day. You see, as chance and good luck would have it, he did not receive a fatal blow when he fell into the lake, and the violent impetus of his descent was somehow broken by the reeds. It was starting to become light. The poor wretch's body was tortured by a thousand agonising pains, and the only part of it which worked was his tongue. So, he kept on groaning and gasping for a long time until some huntsmen who were passing gathered round him, extremely surprised by the unexpected sound of wailing, and found a man who had dislocated both his hips. They asked where he had come from, and what kind of misfortune had happened to him, and he told them everything, just as it had happened. He was brought back in a carriage to Utrecht where the noble Johan van Culemborg, the city magistrate, struck with consternation and full of astonishment because of the strangeness of what had happened to the man, subjected the details [of his story] to close examination and then ordered his men to hunt down the lady with the evil eye [fascinatrix era], arrest her, and put her in chains; and the moment she came into the magistrate's hands, she confessed everything', etc.

<div align="center"><b>The Inquisitor's Curious Witnesses Beaten by Witches</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 88-89</div>
[Bernard da] Como tells us the same exemplary story when he makes clear the truth of a denunciation. Unbelievers may put it to the test. 'Almost fifty years ago now, in Mendrisio', he says, 'a town which was right next to Como, an inquisitor by the name of Master Bartolommeo de Homate, an official called Signior Lorenzo de Concorezio, and a notary, Giovanni de Fossato, were proceeding against these witches [striges]. One day the official, drawn by curiosity and wanting to test whether witches [strigae] would really go to their "play" physically, arranged a meeting [with one of them] and on a Wednesday came, with his notary and someone else, to a certain spot outwith the town, just as he had told the witch [strigae] in advance.

When the three men were almost there, they saw several people gathered together in the presence of someone-the Devil in the form of a goat-who was sitting in the manner of a great lord and, do you know, suddenly, at the Devil's command-and God permitted this because of the men's inquisitiveness-all the people in that gathering struck the official and his companions with sticks so hard that within fifteen days the official, the notary, and the third man died from the beating they had received. This is what he says.

<div align="center"><b>Saints Appearing Miraculously</b></div>
