<div align="center"><b>Cobbler Filippo and the Witch as Cat</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 95-96</div>
Bartolomeo della Spina tells an anecdote from his own time in his Inquiry into Witches, chapter 16. 'Another witness of this apparent transformation of night-flying witches [striges] into cats, a cobbler from Ferrara by the name of Filippo, made the following declaration to me under oath in court earlier this year. Three months previously, he said, a certain witch [strix] had undertaken to cure his child of a bewitchment for which, perhaps, she herself may have been responsible, on condition he would not stop her from coming near his child if he were to see any female cats fawning or behaving in a playful manner round him. Well now, that same day, an hour after she had left the house, Filippo and his wife notice a large female cat they had never seen before, coming towards the child at a fast pace. This frightened his parents, and they made several attempts to drive it away but were distressed to see that it kept coming back. Finally, they lost both their patience and their temper with it. So, they shut the door and, while the cat raced round looking all over the place for a way to escape, Filippo took his time and then threw a spear at it and at last landed it a very heavy blow which forced it to hurl itself out of a window high up in the wall. As a result, the whole of the cat's body looked as though it been squashed flat. Now, from that moment onwards the elderly witch [strix] was confined to her bed for a good long time, since every bone in her body had been broken, a fact which strengthened [the parents'] suspicion that the woman was indeed a witch [stiga] and had subjected their child to the enchantment which was causing him very serious distress. The marks and blows the cat had received, you see, looking like the marks one finds on the bodies of the sick, were found on the elderly woman'.

<div align="center"><b>Peasant's Boat Stuck by Toad-Witch in Flanders</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 96-97</div>
Not quite five years ago, in a village in the west of Flanders, not far from Diksmuide, very near the confluence of the Iperlee and the IJzer, while a plague caused by the evil eye [pestis fascinaria] was spreading in a remarkable fashion, I happened to hear the following story from a monk, a trustworthy individual, who was in the neighbourhood at the time. A peasant and his son were drinking in an ale-house, and out of the corner of his eye the man noticed that the serving-trollop was chalking up double the amount of ale when, as is their custom, he drank a toast to her. He pretended not to notice, but when he and his son had had enough, he called the landlady over and asked her for his bill. He was charged the sum which had been marked up in chalk, but refused to pay, and after the two of them had traded many words, he flung down on the table the amount he knew was correct and left. Boiling with indignation the woman called out, 'You won't be able to get back home today, or I'm not the woman I am!' But he paid her threats no heed and walked off. When he reached the stream where he had left his boat, however, neither he nor his son, (who was pretty strong), could shift it from the bank, even though they leaned on their poles with all their strength. You would think it had been nailed down in the mud. Now, it so happened that two or three soldiers were marching past, so the peasant hailed them. 'Hey, mates! Come and help us get this boat off the bank. I'll pay you for it, and you can have a good drink on me'. They answered his call, came along, and pushed as hard as they could, but they pushed for a long time to no purpose. Then one of them, sweating and gasping for breath, said, 'Why don't be unload all this stuff from the boat? Perhaps we'll find it easier to get it down to the water if it's empty'. So that's what they did and then-pay attention to this!-they saw a huge sallow toad in the bottom of the boat, looking at them with glittering eyes. One of the soldiers did not pick it up but drilled a hole in its swollen throat with the point of his sword and threw it into the water. There, as if dead, it rolled over on to its back, belly upwards. The rest of the soldiers inflicted further wounds on it while it was afloat, and suddenly the boat came free. The peasant was pleased and brought his helpers back to the same alehouse he had left earlier. He asked for a drink, and the waitress brought him one. He asked after the landlady and was told she had become very ill and was in bed, dying. 'Stupid woman!' he said. 'Do you think I'm drunk? I left her standing over there scarcely half an hour ago, and she was in good health and a nasty frame of mind, just like you. I'm going to go and see what's going on'. He went into the bedroom and found that the woman had died of wounds which had dug into and slashed open her neck and stomach. 'How did she get these wounds?' he exclaimed. The waitress insisted she did not know and was sure the woman had not set foot outwith the house. The case went before a magistrate who came to the conclusion that the wounds had been caused by both slashing and stabbing blows, and that they had appeared in the very same places in which the soldiers had struck the toad. The toad itself was nowhere to be found.

<div align="center"><b>Peter Stumpf the Werewolf of Bedburg</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 97</div>
It is about ten years ago that one Peter Stumpf was put to death in Bedburg, a small town in the territory of Köln, because for more than twenty years he had consorted with an evil spirit in the form of a succuba. She had given him a wide belt, and when he put it on, he and others thought he was turned into a wolf. In this form he had ripped out the throats of fifteen children and eaten their brains. He had tried to devour his two daughters-in-law, and he had treated his own daughter and his godmother as his wives. All this is well-known from court records and from engravings which are openly on sale.

<div align="center"><b>Nebuchadnezzar's Transformation into an Ox</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 97</div>
produce a single piece of evidence from Holy Scripture, which it is worth our while to explain-Nebuchadnezzar's [change] into an ox, and that of Lot's wife into a statue of salt. But since both these happened as the result of divine power, they can have no importance for changes brought about by evil spirits. In the case of Nebuchadnezzar, too, most Fathers [of the Church] deny that he assumed the form of an OX or the likeness of an ox's body. They interpret [the incident] simply [by saying] that his reason was so estranged that he believed he was an OX. Not to drag out the argument, it seems to me that an explanation more consonant with what Holy Scripture says, the explanation of St Gregory in his Moralia Book 5, chapter 8, and reason, is that of interpreters [who say] that the condition of the King's body changed into the constitution of an ox, that his human shape had degenerated to some extent into that of a wild animal, and that he had received internal senses which were like those of brute beasts. So as a result, he started to walk on all fours, as cattle do, to eat hay, avoid human beings, and spend his time in the open air.

<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 97</div>
and that of Lot's wife into a statue of salt.

<div align="center"><b>The Speaking Head at Nicopolis</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 99-99</div>
Let me add one \[example\] from Antonio Bonfini. \'About three years after the Emperor Sigismund was disastrously defeated by the Turks at Nicopolis, many people were going down into the plain and heard a voice coming repeatedly from among the bones, crying out the names of Jesus Christ, our Saviour, and the Virgin Mary. They found a speaking head among the corpses, and when it realised there were people in its vicinity, it said, \"Why are you standing there so amazed, gentlemen?

I am a Christian. I fell, unconfessed and unshriven, in this war. Mother Mary is not allowing me to be condemned to eternal torture. She has kept me still in control of my tongue so that I can confess my wrong-doings and purify my mind with Apostolic rites. So please, send for a priest to hear my confession and give me absolution\". Asked why he had deserved such a great favour from the Virgin, he replied that during life he had had a particular devotion to her, celebrated her seven feast-days every year with very great reverence, and honoured her on religious fast-days \[by taking\] only bread and water. So, they sent for a priest from the nearest village, and he duly heard \[the man\'s\] confession, who received remission of all his sins and, after receiving this final cleansing, suddenly fell silent and remained quiet ever after\'.

<div align="center"><b>Polycritus's Ghost and Speaking Head</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 99-100</div>
Sometimes he makes up any old oracle, such as the one Phlegon of Tralles tells us about. He took it from Heron, and its tenor is as follows. Polycritus married Locrides and slept with her for three nights. On the third day he died, leaving her pregnant, and she gave birth to a monster of both sexes. It was brought into the midst of a meeting of the people so that they could be consulted about what this presaged, or what should be done about it. Polycritus appeared as a ghost in the middle of the people and predicted that a disaster would come to the Aetolians and Locrians, and that the people would have no power to resist it. He removed his monstrous son\'s head, swallowed \[the rest of\] him, and vanished, and not long afterwards the head spoke of the same disasters.

<div align="center"><b>Publius's Head Warns of Roman Disaster</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 100-100</div>

King of Asia, the Romans had been terrified by oracles \[which said they should not invade Asia any more, and the head of Publius, the Roman commander, which had been left behind by a wolf after it had devoured the body, as he himself had predicted when moved by a divine frenzy, (so it is thought), chanted in a long speech that disaster would befall the Romans.

<div align="center"><b>The Insensitive Pagan Diviners</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 104-104</div>
In his book on the Egyptian mysteries, just before the middle, Iamblichus, speaking about pagan diviners, testifies that many of these soothsayers were not burned when fire was applied to them or, if they were burned, they did not feel the burning or any of the other tortures; and he adds that this happened because there was a god blowing from within \[them\], driving away the fire and quenching everything else. Who was this god of the magician Iamblichus other than an evil spirit, some kind of Eurycles?

<div align="center"><b>The Invulnerable Law Student Quirinus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 104-105</div>
I knew a lawstudent called Quirinus, a distinguished Bachelor \[of Laws\], who, putting his trust in one of these parchments, joined in quarrels and fights, often received blows without ever being wounded, and in the end was killed by a very slight wound at a party in Rome in 1572 or 1573.

<div align="center"><b>The Shape-Shifting Magician Scafius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 104-105</div>
In this connection I should refer to what the author of The Ant Hill writes about Scafius. \'The principal author of acts of harmful magic \[maleficiorum\] in the territory of Bern was someone called Scafius. He was bold enough to boast about this in public, \[saying\] that he could make himself look like a mouse in the eyes of his rivals whenever he wanted and slip away out of the hands of his deadly enemies, and this, it is said, is how he escaped from the hands of his mortal enemies. But divine justice wanted to put an end to his roguery, and at length his enemies discovered him sitting gingerly on a stove near a window; and since he had no fear of them while they were laying siege to him, they ran him through with their swords and spears without warning via the window, and he died a miserable death in that place because of his crimes\'. Thus \[Johannes\] Nider, \[Formicarius 5.4.\]

<div align="center"><b>The Torture-Resistant Witch and Her Death</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 105-105</div>
An astonishing story belonging to this genre is told by \[Claude\] Caron. A fifty-year old women whom he knew made light of it, in the way \[I have described\], when melted lard was poured all over her body and every part of her body was pulled apart. When she was taken off the rack she felt no pain, and she was unhurt and uninjured except that her big toe had been pulled off during the torture and had not been restored, although the damage did not hurt or hinder her at all. But even as she made sport of all her torments and obstinately denied everything, Satan, who had accused of her harmful magic via the mouth of a possessed woman, killed her in prison by tearing out her throat,

<div align="center"><b>The Insensitive Little Witch Near Amiens</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 105-105</div>
(Antichrist Revealed, Fr. Bernard Olivier, who was Provincial of the Society of Jesus for the Spanish Netherlands at the time, told me that in 1599 a little witch-girl \[puellulam strigatricem\] was arrested near Amiens.

She used not to feel it, he said, when her feet were very savagely scorched and she was severely whipped until, on the advice of a particular priest, they placed a wax image of the Blessed Lamb against her neck. Then her evil spirit\'s conjuring-tricks were forcibly removed by the power of the sacred medicament, and she began to be fully aware of the force of the pain.

<div align="center"><b>The Insensitive Mark of Isabelle Pardée</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 106-106</div>
\'Isabelle Pardée was arrested in Épinal on 7th May 1588 on a charge of fortunetelling \[sortilegii\]. She revealed to the mayor of the city the part of her body which had been marked in this fashion by her evil spirit, and he had the idea of carrying out a test \[to see\] whether what people used to say about its insensitivity was true. So, he ordered a small pin to be thrust into it and rammed down as far as it would go. This was done in front of quite a large crowd of people. No blood at all appeared from the wound and the wise-woman \[saga\] gave those who were watching not the slightest sign that she was feeling pain. Thus Rémy, Book 1, chapter 5.

<div align="center"><b>The Insensitive Mark of Claude Bogart</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 106-106</div>
On 30th October 1590, Claude Bogart in Porrentruy was about to be put to the torture again.

Her skin had been shaved in accordance with the usual practice, and because of this a scar on top of her bare forehead became visible. The investigating judge therefore suspected the truth-namely, that this was an imprint of her evil spirit\'s fingernail, which used to be hidden by her hair before \[she was shaved\]. He ordered a small pin to be thrust very deeply into it, and when this had been done it was seen that she had no sense of being hurt, and that no blood, or very little, flowed from the wound. But she persisted in denying the truth and kept on saying that the numbness stemmed from an old blow from a stone. Not long afterwards, however, when she had been put to the torture, she not only admitted that \[the mark\] had been inflicted on her by an evil spirit, but she also gave a detailed account of the very many other things she had suffered most undeservedly at his hands. Thus Rémy, Book 1, chapter 5.

<div align="center"><b>The Insensitive Mark of Mugette</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 106-106</div>
Very recently, on 14th June 1591, at Essey, which is a village a mile away from the city, the local landowner was present and ordered the recorder to put Mugette, who was charged with divination \[sortilegii\] to the test. So, this man stripped off all her clothes in order to see if he could find the identifying mark anywhere. This finally made its appearance on her left thigh, looking very much like a wart. He probed it with a knife as deeply as possible, \[but\] could not draw or force a word from Mugette, or even a drop of blood from the wound, although when he lightly stabbed the place next to the blemish, she emitted a great cry of pain and blood flowed out profusely\'. Thus Rémy, Book 1, chapter 5.

<div align="center"><b>The Insensitive Mark of Bayaronne</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 107-107</div>
In the book by Claude Caron I have already recommended, he writes that the mark of the notorious wise-woman \[sagae\], Bayaronne, which was well hidden on her left thigh, very indistinctly outlined, the size of a penny-piece with, as it were, fine threads running out from its centre to the periphery, was entirely and completely insensitive.

<div align="center"><b>The Coal Mark on the Woman from Narbonne</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 107-107</div>
I also suspect that the sign of a double piece of coal on the feet of a woman from Narbonne was \[such a\] mark. (I recount this story, taken from \[Jean\] Gerson, Book 3 or 5, later.)

<div align="center"><b>The Reactive Mark of Jean Del Vaulx</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 107-107</div>
Just such a mark was seen on the back of Jean Del Vaulx, and Pierre Dheure, the investigating judge in the affair, noticed something remarkable. If you ran a needle or a pen-nib quite deeply into it, the accused did not feel any pain and did not ask what was going on. But if you threatened his chain-mark from behind at a distance with a finger held out stiff, in such a way that he could not see you, the accused man would suddenly be convulsed as though he were being tortured severely. I find no physical cause for this. I suspect a moral one-that \[the man\'s\] utterly arrogant evil spirit was smarting at being despised, and that this made him angry and furious at the torture of his slave.

<div align="center"><b>The Prolonged Sleep of Epimenides</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 107-107</div>
While looking for a sheep, he went into a cave during the midday heat, was overcome by deep sleep, and slept, according to Pausanias, for 77 years, (67, according to Eudemus, and slightly fewer, according to others). Later on he woke up, thought he had had forty winks, and began to look for his little sheep. Perhaps there is a natural explanation for why these people slept, although in the case of Epimenides I should rather attribute it to an evil spirit.

<div align="center"><b>The Conjuring Sleep of the Demi-Gods of Sardis</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 107-107</div>
The sleep of the demi-gods of Sardis was a fairy-story, because these people were actually dead. They seemed to be sleeping and to be woken up because of a conjuring-trick by evil spirits.

<div align="center"><b>The Twenty-Year Fast of Roger Bacon's English Girl</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 108-108</div>
I should think this was the reason for the abstinence from food and \'nonimbibing, (as Plautus puts it), of Roger Bacon\'s English girl who, he said, ate nothing for twenty years.

<div align="center"><b>The Two-Year Fast of the French Priest</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 108-108</div>
\[It also explains\] the French priest who abstained from all food and drink for two years in the time of Pope Nicholas v,

<div align="center"><b>The Four-Year Fast of the Girl from Speyer</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 108-108</div>
and the girl from Speyer who, on the orders of Emperor Ferdinand, was closely watched and observed by many physicians in 1540 to see if it was true that, after a serious illness, she had already persisted in abstaining from food for more than four years, but was still physically strong and healthy and had not lost weight, and her body was merely covered in phlegmatic scurf. (She would, however, frequently burst into tears). She had the usual dirt in her nostrils and ears, and she used to sleep and stay quiet for quite a long time. But she did not discharge faeces or urine either from her anus or from her female parts. This gave rise to a great deal of argument-among the Italians, by Simone Porzio, and among the Germans by an eye-witness, one of the physicians who was observing the girl, Gerhard Buchholtz.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Phaetusa</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
In Book 6, Part 8 of his Epidemics, Hippocrates writes that in Abdera, Pitheus\'s wife, Phaetusa, was very near giving birth when her husband was sent into exile. For several months her menstrual flow ceased, she endured great pains in every part of her body, and her physical appearance changed into that of a man. Her body became manlike, (\'owµa ávδpwIn\'), she turned hairy all over, grew a beard, and her voice became quite rough.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Anamisia</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
The same thing happened in Thrace to Gorgippus\'s wife, Anamisia,

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of the Woman from Spoleto</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
and in Book 42 Livy says the same thing happened to a woman from Spoleto during the second Punic war.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of the Girl in 171 BC</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
Pliny (Book 7, chapter 4) says, \'Transformation of females into males is not a fairy-story. I have discovered in the annals that during the consulship of Publius Licinius Crassus and Gaius Cassius Longinus \[171BC\], a girl was changed into a boy under \[the eyes\] of her parents and, at the augurs\' order, was taken away to an uninhabited island.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Arecusa to Arescon</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
Licinius Mucianus has recorded that he himself saw a man called Arescon in Argos, who used to be called Arecusa. She had actually married a man, but then grew a beard, produced a man\'s organs of generation, and married a woman.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of the Boy in Smyrna</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
\[Mucianus\] also says he saw a boy in Smyrna who had suffered the same fate.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Lucius Cossitius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
I myself saw a person in Africa who had turned into a man on the day of her marriage-Lucius Cossitius, a citizen of Thysdrus.

He was still alive while I was writing this\'. That is what Pliny writes.

<div align="center"><b>The Woman from Gaeta's Sex Change</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
In his History of Naples, Gioviano Pontano tells the story of a woman in Gaeta who was married to a fisherman for fourteen years and changed into a man:

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Emilia from Eboli</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
and another woman, called Emilia, who had been married to Antonio Spensa, a citizen of Eboli, changed into a man after twelve years of marriage, dissolved their union, married a woman, and procreated children.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change After Bearing a Son</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-109</div>
\[Pontano\] adds that another woman, having borne one son to her husband, suddenly grew male organs of generation and, not being a woman any longer, left her husband, married another woman, and got children by her.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Maria Pacheco to Manoel</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 109-110</div>
In the town of Esgueira in Portugal, nine leagues from Coimbra, there lived a man of good family who had a daughter called Maria Pacecho. When she was of the age at which she would begin to menstruate, a male member burst from her places instead of a fluid humour-it had either lain hidden inside her or came out of her in some other fashion-and so a post-pubertal young man suddenly appeared out of the girl. When she became a man, she also took the name Manoel Pacecho. He went on board ship, set out for India, performed brave deeds, acquired the reputation of being an active soldier, as well as great wealth, and on his return to his own country, took as his wife a woman of good family. Amato makes no mention of children, but says he remained beardless, and that his face had a feminine look to it-signs that his manliness was not altogether complete.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of the Peasant's Barren Wife</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
Secondly, Torquemada tells us he had heard from a friend, a man with a considerable reputation and who was entirely trustworthy, that not far from the city of Beneventana in Spain, the wife of a peasant who was moderately well-off was treated in less than kindly fashion by her husband because she was barren. Sick of being treated badly, she ran away one night, dressed as a male house-servant and, thus accoutred, she passed herself off as a man in various places and earned her living by working as a servant. After some time, either because the natural heat \[she had\] inside her would have this power, or because her imagination, produced and strengthened by her continually wearing men\'s clothes and doing men\'s work, acquired this power, the fact is she discovered she had turned into a man. So, having been a wife for a long time, she decided to act as a husband and joined herself in matrimony to a woman. Her secret remained hidden for a long time because \'he\' did not dare to tell it to anyone, until somebody who had known her very well before noticed that her face was very similar to that of the peasant\'s wife who had run away. He asked whether, by chance, she was the woman\'s brother, and she told him the whole story, just as it had happened, and this is how it came to light.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Changes of Francesca and Carola</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
\[Battista\] Fregoso writes the following. \'During the reign of Ferdinand I of Naples, Ludovico Guarna, a citizen of Salerno, brought up five daughters. The two oldest were called Francesca and Carola, and when they reached the age of fifteen, both girls\' genitalia burst out into what one expects of men. They changed the way they dressed and were considered to be men and took the names Francesco and Carolo.

<div align="center"><b>The Wedding Night Sex Change in Eboli</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
Likewise, in Eboli, during the same king\'s reign, there was a girl who had been betrothed to a man for four years already. On the first night of her marriage, when she went to bed with her husband and, either because of the vigorous friction or for some other unknown reason, the membranes which made her look like a woman were broken and men\'s parts came out. She went home, sued for a return of her dowry, and thereafter was considered to be a man\'.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Philotis</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
I have set these \[anecdotes\] in front of you so that Phlegon\'s narrative may seem less incredible. He says, \'A young woman called Philotis, who was born in Smyrna, was given in marriage by her parents to a man once she had reached \[the appropriate\] age, but produced a male member and turned into a man.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Aeteta</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
In Laodicea, which is in Syria, too, there was a woman called Aeteta, and while she was actually having sex with her husband she turned into a man and called herself Aetetus. \[This happened\] when Macrinus was Governor of Athens and Lucius Lamia and Aelianus the Elder were consuls in Rome\'. (Phlegon, one of Hadrian\'s freedmen, adds that he has seen this person).

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change of Marie to Germain</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
In Book 1, chapter 21 of his Essays, Michel de Montaigne testifies that in his own time there was a girl in Vitry le François called Marie. She was dancing rather energetically when, all of a sudden, male organs burst out of her, and she was changed into a man. During the sacrament of confirmation, the Bishop of Soissons gave her the name \'Germain\', and this is what people started to call her. Later on, a beard grew on her chin. She lived a long time-into very old age-but never married. \[Montaigne\] attributes the change to her vivid imagination, but I add that her physical constitution was tending in that direction.

<div align="center"><b>The Sex Change in Auch</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 110-110</div>
The author of Antimaeologium writes that \'in Auch in Gascony he had seen a man of sixty years and more, grey-headed, strong, with a full head of hair, who had been a girl up to the age of fifteen. But in the end, it so happened that when her thin ligaments burst and her pudenda emerged, she changed her sex, since she had never menstruated before\'.

<div align="center"><b>The Misbaptized Child in Paris</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 111-111</div>
\[Séverin\] Pineau writes that in Paris in 1577 in the Rue Saint-Denis a woman gave birth at night to a son who was hastily baptised because he was weak, and was called Jeanne, as though he were a girl. After a few days, first his mother, then \[other people\] realised, not without great astonishment, that she was a son and gave him the name Jean. It is therefore easy to deceive the common people, who are rather ignorant.

<div align="center"><b>The Failed Sex Change of Sporus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 111-112</div>
Second conclusion: I have to believe that it is impossible for Nature and for an evil spirit to make a woman out of a man. This attempt was made in the case of Sporus by Nero who was utterly devoted to magic, but without any success. It was done by extreme withdrawal of his completed parts into female places, and I do not understand how Nature would degrade \[someone\] this way.

<div align="center"><b>The Boy to Girl Change in Benevento</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 112-112</div>
Nor is \[the story\] particularly ancient which \[says that\] in Campanian Benevento One of their young men suddenly became a young woman\', \[69.3-4, 13-14\].

<div align="center"><b>The Rejuvenation of Aeson</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 112</div>
\'His beard and hair Laid aside their greyness and seized hold of the colour black. His thinness was routed and fled; his pallor and mustiness went away; His hollow wrinkles were filled with increased flesh; His body became wanton. Aeson was astonished and remembered That he was like this forty years ago\', \[Metamorphoses 7.288-293\].

<div align="center"><b>The Rejuvenation of the Centenarian in Tarento</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 113</div>
In Tarento in 1531, there was a centenarian ready for the grave, whose hair was falling out, whose skin was peeling off, and whose nails were dropping out in the wasting away and foulness of old age. But everything changed for the better and from being an old man, he became young \[again\] and lived a further fifty years.

<div align="center"><b>The Rejuvenation in Rioja</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 113</div>
In the Castilian part of Rioja, too, a similar thing happened to someone and was very well known because everyone was talking about it, and it was carefully investigated by the chief magistrates of Castile.

<div align="center"><b>The Rejuvenation of the Abbess in Monvendro</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 113</div>
Monvendro is a town, (once called Sagunto), in the Kingdom of Valencia. Valesco de Tarenta writes that during his lifetime there was an abbess of a convent here who, already a dry-bones, suddenly began to menstruate again. Her teeth grew back, her hair turned dark, the wrinkles and folds in her skin filled out, her pendulous, shrivelled breasts swelled up like those of a young girl, and in the end her features, face, and entire bodily carriage made her look like a young woman. As a result, shocked by the novelty of her situation, out of modesty she began to withdraw from everyone's gaze.

<div align="center"><b>The Long-Lived Indian</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 113</div>
More recent, very trustworthy, writers on Portuguese history give us a long, well-established narrative about an Indian of good family who lived for three hundred and forty years during which the flower of his youth withered three times and blossomed again three times;

<div align="center"><b>The Youth-Restoring Spring on Bonica</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 113</div>
and there is no lack of people who testify that in the New World there is an island called Bonica on which there flows a spring whose water, more precious than wine, restores youth to old people when they drink it.

<div align="center"><b>The Youth-Restoring Spring on Lucaya</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 113</div>
Pedro Cieza, however, not without good reason, includes a narrative, which originated in a healthier location in the region, about a similar spring on Lucaya: History of Peru Part 2, chapter 41.

<div align="center"><b>Simon Thurway's Forgetfulness</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 116-117</div>
Another lengthy period of forgetfulness happened to the Englishman, Simon Thurway. \[His memory\] drained away because of God\'s just punishment.

He was a very clever logician and had a great reputation as Professor of the Liberal Arts in Paris, \[but\] he was filled more with knowledge which puffs a man up than with charity which edifies and was not afraid to boast in the Schools that he knew the whole of Christ\'s law and could demolish it by overruling it. But he was struck so suddenly by forgetfulness that he could not remember his basic letters, could not recite the Lord\'s Prayer, and did not know how to read. While Nicholas of Durham was still a young man, he saw him being taught his letters by his own son, and \'\[Nicholas\]\', says Matthew Paris in Book 7 of his History of the English, \'told me about it while I was writing this\'. It happened in about 1201. (Trithemius says that Simon came from Tournai, but he is wrong because \[Simon\] was an Englishman from Cornwall. He is also mentioned in Book 15 of Polydore \[Vergil\'s\] History of England).

<div align="center"><b>The Initiation of Vampire-Witches</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 118</div>
Johannes Nider has left us a passage about the initiation of vampire-witches \[lamiarum\], and in this \[passage\] there is a confession by one of them. \'We lay siege to little children who have not yet been baptised, or even to those who have been baptised, especially if they are not protected by the sign of the cross and prayers. We kill them with our rituals while there are in their cradles or lying beside their parents, and afterwards it is thought they have been smothered or have died in some other way. We take them secretly from their graves, boil them down in a cauldron until the flesh has almost entirely come away from the bones and one can swallow it and drink it. From the more solid part of this matter, we make an ointment which is suitable for what we want to do-our \[magical\] practices and our shape-changing. We fill a flask or a bottle with the runnier humour, and once anyone has drunk any of this, to the accompaniment of a few rituals, he immediately becomes a participant in and a master of our sect\', \[The Ant-Hill 5.3\] This is the confession of a woman. It is followed by another which seems to be that of a man. It adds the other ceremonies used in their abominable initiation and advancement to the hellish office of \'Master\'. This is the order in which I was led astray. First, on Sunday, before the holy water is blessed, the person who is about to become an apprentice enters the church with the Masters \[of the craft\] and there, in front of them, he denies Christ, his faith, his baptism, and the Catholic Church. Next, he pays homage to the \"Magisterulus\", that is, \"the Little Master\", which is the only title they use for the evil spirit, and finally he drinks from the bottle I mentioned earlier. Once he has done this, he immediately feels himself receiving and retaining a mental image of our craft and of the principal rituals of this sect. This is how I was led astray, as was my wife\', etc.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil's Temptation of Ignatius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 119</div>
This was undoubtedly the stratagem whereby the ancient serpent tried unsuccessfully to deceive Ignatius, the founder of my Society. Let me write down the whole thing in the words of \[Pedro de\] Ribadaneira. \'But visions, which he saw in the midst of his studies\'-he is talking about grammatical studies\'began to disturb him very frequently and in large numbers. He wanted to commit to memory the inflections of nouns and verbs, but such a great abundance of divine thoughts started to pour into his mind that he was unable to learn anything new at all, and if anything had stuck in his memory before this, it started to flow away utterly and completely. Nor was he able either to prevent thoughts of this kind from coming, or to drive them away when they started, even though he zealously made every effort \[to do so\]. Shocked by the strangeness of the situation, he asked, why does this happen when I am praying, when I am making my confession and receiving the bread which transcends its substance, when I am awake, and when I scourge myself? When I survey all the spiritual gifts I receive from God, such frequent, such great, and such powerful \[moments of enlightenment do not present themselves to me. So, when I am engaged in doing unimportant things and am not thinking about God when I am close to God, why do such beautiful images and heavenly illuminations come to my mind? I recognise, I do recognise how clever this most troublesome Enemy is. I recognise the Old Fox\'s tricks and cunning\', etc. The \[Enemy\] got hold of Ignatius\'s mind, which had already long been actively engaged in receiving intimations of heavenly things, because he knew in advance that if Ignatius\'s teaching were to be consonant with his religious devotion it would threaten him \[the Enemy\] with a greater harm. So he tried, with this display of better doctrine, to make \[Ignatius\'s\] task of the learning the rudiments of letters ineffectual, \'at this time especially\', (as \[Giovanni\] Mattei says), \'offering his understanding frequent illuminations, and throwing light on the abstruse mysteries of Holy Scripture\'.

<div align="center"><b>The Prophetic Son of Chrysanthius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 119</div>
Chrysanthius of Sardis, who was a magician and High Priest \[of Lydia\] at the time of Julian the Apostate, had a son and although \[his son\] had no skill in the art of versification or any part of \[the art\] of grammar, with a single glance at the sun he would pour out prophecies against the Christians.

<div align="center"><b>The Inspired Shepherd Poets</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 119</div>
Breathed on in the same way by the divinity who lights up the night, various shepherds suddenly became poets-Evages of Bithynia, for example, and others.

<div align="center"><b>Amphiaraus Becoming a Poet</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 119</div>
Amphiaraus also became a poet this way,

<div align="center"><b>The Old Woman Taught by Spirits</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 119</div>
and the old woman who was taught Pindar\'s hymn, and evil spirits in the guise of old men clad in skins, who lived in her house and fulfilled the role of incubi, told Sosipatra everything which was done elsewhere behind her back, and taught her everything which had been written by poets, philosophers, and orators.

<div align="center"><b>The Magical Exploits of Román Ramírez</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 119-122</div>
These examples, however, are superior to teaching received from the Devil. In March in the year, 1600, in accordance with the sentence of the Inquisitors of the Faith, the bones of one Román Ramírez, together with a statue, were burned to ashes in Toledo. Ramírez had died in prison while awaiting trial. He was accused, among other things, of having received skill in medicine from an evil spirit, and since this criminal united in his person many aspects of magic, I shall not regard it as a chore to translate the \[procurator\] fiscal\'s accusation faithfully from Spanish into Latin.

\(a\) He said \[Ramírez\] had already been accused of the Muslim error and had abjured it, but added that he had relapsed-which is what that kind of muck usually does-and returned to his vomit. For many years he had kept the great fast they call \'Ramadan\', and observed the rites and ceremonies of Muhammad by first saying \'guadoc\' and \'zála, washing his whole body, putting on a clean outer garment, reciting the \'azoras\', (i.e. prayers), of that sect with his face turned to \'al-quibla\', (i.e. the rising sun), raising and lowering his head and bowing himself to the ground, extending his palms upwards to the sky while he was saying \'Alaqueur\', (i.e. God is great), and rising before daybreak to celebrate the fast \'zahor. Thus absolved \[of his sins\], he solemnly celebrated his Easter, \[called\] \'Ramadan\', for three days. This he did by abstaining from all work and manual labour, especially on the first day, by putting on a clean outer garment, and by making the declaration \'guadoc\' and \'zála\', along with many other Muslim rites and ceremonies. Then, adding crime to crime and sin to sin, he revived the overt pact and treaty he had made with an evil spirit many years before with the intention of doing something wicked. He worshipped this evil spirit, did him homage, and dedicated his soul to him on condition he assist him with his favour and advice as often as he might be needed or asked in connection with anything at all. So Ramírez asked and obtained from the evil spirit knowledge of and expertise in how to cure many secret concealed illnesses with herbs, fumigations, and superstitious incantations: and, as a matter of fact, he cured not a few people this way.

\(b\) He persuaded people he had a wide and detailed knowledge of literature, both sacred and profane, and so they would give him the titles of stories and he would-quite openly for money-recite them, more or less word for word, as though he were reading them from a book open in front of him, something he did with the help of his evil spirit. In this way he amassed not a little power because of the money he was given by his audiences, and when someone asked him how he had acquired such a reliable memory, he replied that someone very close to him, a person very knowledgeable about the properties of herbs, who had an evil spirit as a close friend, had given him some kind of drink. He also said that on one occasion, when this same person wanted to go to Saragossa, which was fifteen leagues away, he made an invocation-de bon varón, as they say-and a horse suddenly appeared. They both mounted it and very soon afterwards arrived at the city gates where they left the horse after removing its bridle. They then did the business which was the reason for their journey, returned to the gate, threw the bridle on the horse again, and were brought back home almost in an instant.

\(c\) There was an incident which took place in the town of Deza. A woman went to bed angry, (as it happened), and put herself in the hands of an evil spirit. Suddenly someone-she had no idea who-removed her from that place and because she had disappeared completely, her husband came to Ramírez and asked him for his help. Ramírez replied that the man should not be downcast, and that he would restore his wife to him. He gave him a letter and told him to go to a particular place among the vineyards, make a circle there, and stand in the middle of it, and when he heard a crowd of people passing by, even if he saw no one he was to ask, \'Where is the King going to be?\' If the answer was that the King was crossing by on that side, he should throw the letter to the ground in front of him \[self\]. The man did as he was told and got his wife back, although he did not see who had brought her back or how he had done it.

\(d\) The procurator fiscal added that \[Ramírez\] said a good many very obscure things, and things which had happened in places very far away, about which he could not have known anything at the time he was revealing them, except through his pact with an evil spirit. On one occasion, (said the fiscal), while Ramírez was leaving the house he said a traveller was about to come and ask and entreat him to come and free his daughter from an evil spirit which was obsessing her, and so they should summon him at once. The other man came and Ramírez was summoned. While he was curing the illness, Ramírez behaved in such a way that it seemed perfectly clear that an evil spirit had been dispatched against the sick woman beforehand through an act of harmful magic \[maleficio\] by Ramírez himself. \[People thought this\] because, first of all, on the day of her marriage the unfortunate girl had suffered a flux of blood together with a mental blackout and pressure round her heart; and not long afterwards she had frequent, long-lasting fainting fits, and began to hate her parents and her husband, and could not endure the sight of him or sleeping with him.

They came to the conclusion she was obsessed. A priest was sent for and for eighteen days he tried to drive away her wicked guest with exorcisms and the usual remedies of the Catholic Church. Finally, one evil spirit spoke up and said that a hundred and one evil spirits had been dispatched against the woman\'s body, but that now he was the only one because all the others had gone away. So when the priest began to repeat the usual exorcisms from the Church\'s servicebook and to urge him to say where the rest had gone, he replied they had left for the town of Deza to visit the foresaid Román Ramírez and ask him what he wanted them to do, and that they would not leave until he had given them permission to do so. At this point, the unfortunate girl was cruelly beaten to the floor and stayed there the whole day in a swoon. When the priest began to order him to stop harassing the woman and to let her live with her husband as a wife should, and to give her back a precious jewel he had stolen, \[the evil spirit\] replied that he was under Román\'s control and that he \[himself\] had the jewel but could not give it back. It was after this that the parents, affected by what the evil spirit said, had sent for Román Ramírez to cure their daughter.

The moment he entered the house, the woman began to say and do extraordinary things, crying out that she knew he had come, but did not know why he had done so. Then, as he entered her room and she saw him, she fainted, her mouth became contorted, and she remained like this for three days, unable to eat or drink, having no power to swallow anything at all. So the magician drenched her with a strong suffumigation composed of \[smoke from\] burning shoe-leather, juniper oil, and other things, summoned her back from her fainting fit and then, addressing the spirit which was obsessing her, said, \'Leave her alone. Leave her alone and go away. Flee, unless you want me to repeat the suffumigation\'.

The evil spirit, however, was unwilling to reply, so Ramírez told everyone to leave the room and, thinking no one could hear him, started to question the spirit and demand, \'Why don\'t you answer me? The spirit which attended my grandfather had more humility! What have I done that you do not deign to speak to me? Isn\'t it enough that I have devoted myself to you entirely for such a long time? So now make this woman sleep so that she doesn\'t hear anything we say\'.

Meanwhile, another evil spirit arrived, and the accused said to him, \'You\'ve come at the right moment. What did you do while you were in Deza? How are my wife and children?\'

\'Your son\', said \[the evil spirit\], \'was not well, but he\'s now out of danger. Your wife was worse. She has now recovered. Your grandson is still sick, and there is a danger he may die.

Then Ramírez replied that since they knew he had served them faithfully every day, and how much he was bound to them, they were not unaware he was being pressed for a debt of two hundred reals as payment for a garden he had rented. It was fair, \[he said\], that they do him a favour and leave the obsessed woman\'s body, at least for the space of fifteen days, and leave her healthy and sound in the meantime, so that he could receive payment of a large fee and increase his future reputation and good character, which would otherwise receive a notable blow. After that, they could attack her again, and take possession of her as far as he was concerned. The evil spirit then promised he would do this and obey him. But when Ramírez noticed his conversation had been overheard by other people, and that he had been found out as someone who had power, so to speak, over an evil spirit, he became angry and threatened he would throw \[the evil spirit\] into chains, and the memory of this would make sure he would not dare do anything like that again.

The evil spirit was expelled, but because the woman\'s parents had not paid a large enough fee for what he had done, Ramírez threatened that they would soon need his help again. Immediately, he transferred the condition \[caused by\] his harmful magic \[maleficii\] into the husband of the woman who was now free and told his evil spirits to go into him and molest him. They did so and afflicted him very cruelly for the entire day, inspiring him with a thousand frightening mental images and temptations to despair. So, they sent for \[Ramírez\] again and when he came to the man he said, \'You have been struggling valiantly with an evil spirit, my friend\', then, employing suffumigations, he drove the evil spirits out of him. He left the town, discontented with the tiny fee \[he had received\]. After he had gone, the parents discovered that not only had their daughter not been cured, but that she was growing worse day by day. So, they wrote to the magician and sent no small a sum of money along with the letter, promising a larger sum if he would cure the woman completely. He deigned to come, gave the courier the material for another fumigation, and wrote down the ritual he should use, adding that when he got back to the house, he would find her somewhat better and dancing with female friends: and that is how it turned out. But since the wife was still not willing to sleep with her husband, he suffumigated her and added the words he had been told \[to use\], namely, \'Evil spirits, leave this woman, because Román tells you \[to do so\]. Otherwise, I shall send for him, and he will come and drive you out by force, because this is what he has promised me\'. At this invocation, the woman gave a loud cry, was cured, and remained free and lived with her husband the way married couples usually do. (This comes from the court records I mentioned).

<div align="center"><b>Blessed Angela da Foligno's Torments by Evil Spirits</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 123-125</div>
But in order to make clear by means of a particular example how far an evil spirit is sometimes accustomed to go in trying to constrain the will-not with complete success, but close to it, and not in actuality, but in appearance-for the consolation of those who have been tempted I shall record the words of Blessed Angela da Foligno. In chapter 19 of her Life, she describes one of her experiences.

\'I suffer more or less continually at the hands of evil spirits torments and sufferings of the soul and, I am telling you, there are more of them, and they are and incomparably more grievous than those suffered by the body. I don\'t know how to tell you what they are like, other than to say, think of somebody suspended by a rope upon a beam, with his hands tied behind his back and his eyes blindfolded, left on the gibbet still alive, without any help, any remedy, or any support. I am saying I am tortured in a greater state of hopelessness and more cruelly by evil spirits, because I see that the evil spirits are hanging my soul in such a way that, just as the hanged man has nothing to support him, so my soul does not appear to have any support left, and all my soul\'s courage is overthrown while my soul knows \[what is happening\] and looks on. When my soul sees that all its courage is being overthrown and is leaving, and that it cannot set oppose this, there is such great pain to my soul that sometimes I can scarcely weep because of my hopeless grief and anger. Sometimes, on the other hand, I can\'t stop weeping, and sometimes such great anger comes over me that I can scarcely hold myself back from ripping myself to pieces. Sometimes I can\'t stop myself from hitting myself dreadfully, and when I do that, I have sometimes made my head and other parts of my body swell up. When my soul sees all my courage vanishing and leaving, my soul starts to lament and I cry out to my God.

I also suffer another torment-that all my defects have come alive in me again and are now beginning to gather strength, \[and I suffer\], not because during my life-time they bring my reason under their control, but because they cause and bring me great punishment. Even vices which never existed in my body enter into me and are set on fire and bring me great punishment. But they do not last, and when they die again, they bring me great consolation because I see that I have been handed over to many evil spirits who cause the vices I abhor, and which were dead, to come alive again; and \[it is\] they who add the vices which never existed. When I remember that God was afflicted and despised here \[on earth\], I should like, (in as much as it is proper), all the evil things happening to be to be doubled. Whenever I am in the utterly dreadful darkness caused by the evil spirits, when it seems that all hope of good is entirely absent, the darkness is dreadful and the vices in my body, (which I realise inwardly are dead in my soul), are being revived. Outwith my soul, however, the evil spirits are reviving them and even those vices which did not exist are being revived. I also suffer physically in at least three places, because in my shameful places there is such a great fire that it was my habit to put physical fire to them to put out the fire of lust, until my confessor told me not to.

When I am in that darkness, I believe I should more readily choose to be roasted than to suffer the things I have just been talking about. At that point, indeed, I cry aloud and call for death, \[begging\] God to allow it to come to me in any way at all. On those occasions I say to God, \'O Lord, if you must send me to Hell, please do it quickly and don\'t put it off and, since you have abandoned me, finish the job and plunge me into the deep\'. Then I realise it is the work of evil spirits and that those vices are not alive in my soul, because my soul never consents to them. My body, however, suffers violence, and there is such great pain and disgust that if it were to last, my body could not bear it. But my soul also sees that all power has been taken away from it, even though it does not consent to the vices. In spite of the fact that it does not have the power to resist the vices entirely, it also sees that it is opposed to God, and so falls and is tortured in them. There is also a particular sin which God has allowed to come into me, which never existed in me \[before\]. I recognise clearly this sin which God has allowed to come into me, and this sin is so great that it surpasses all my other sins. There is also a particular strength which God has clearly given me to counter that sin, and by this God sets me free by His special supernatural power. If I did not have a sure faith in God, the responsibility would lie with me and not with anyone else; and in that case a sure, untroubled hope in which I could have no doubt would be my responsibility. But the strength always prevails and the sin \[always\] fails, and the strength is in control of me and does not allow me to fall into sin.

The strength is so great that not only is it in control of me, it actually gives me so much of its strength that I really do recognise God in this \[situation\] and am illumined and strengthened to such a degree that I could not be moved to the smallest sin by anyone in the world, or any of the demons in Hell, or anything else; and my faith in God rests in me, along with this power. The sin, however, is so great that I am ashamed to mention it and \[to say\] that whenever the foresaid courage has been taken away from me, I also have the impression that what has abandoned me is not something which could protect me, either because of my feelings of shame or \[because it is\] a torment \[intended\] to stop me from rushing into sin straight away. But then the courage I mentioned comes over me and sets me free with its special supernatural power in such a way that I could not sin for all the good or evil in the world. These hardships I have borne for two years and more\'.

Thus far St Angela\'s translator, with insufficient Latin.

<div align="center"><b>Lapp Magician's Trance for Distant Knowledge</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 126-126</div>
This is the kind of thing Olaus Magnus tells us about Lapp magicians. If any traveller wants to find out exactly what is going on back home, they undertake that within twenty-four hours he will find out what is happening there, even if home is three hundred miles away. This is how they do it. A shaman \[incantator\] ceremonially summons his gods and then suddenly collapses and becomes unconscious as though his soul had suddenly left his body, because either he has no breath left in him, nor does any sensation, movement, or life seem to remain with him. But to make sure they are \[actually\] still there, there must always be someone with him to guard the body while it is abandoned and lying unconscious, because if this is not done evil spirits make off with it. Once twenty-four hours have elapsed, his breath comes back and the unconscious body wakes up with a groan, as from a deep sleep, as though the man who had fallen down were being called back to life from death. After he has been restored this way, he replies to questions and, to create trust in his questioner, tells him something his questioner recognises and knows for certain has taken place in his own home or in those of his relatives.

<div align="center"><b>Virgin from Zaragoza's Deceptive Raptures and Stigmata</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 126-126</div>
In the case of the virgin from Zaragoza-this is what I call her because her deception was finally uncovered in that city by the Archbishop in 1585-who pretended she had the stigmata of Christ, her \'raptures\'-and every one of them stemmed from a pact she had made with an evil spirit while she was feeding her sheep-were as frequent as those of Magdalena de la Cruz from Cordoba who was a notorious wise-woman \[saga\].

<div align="center"><b>Magdalena de la Cruz's Frequent Raptures</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 126-126</div>
her \'raptures\'-and every one of them stemmed from a pact she had made with an evil spirit while she was feeding her sheep-were as frequent as those of Magdalena de la Cruz from Cordoba who was a notorious wise-woman \[saga\].

<div align="center"><b>Er Son of Armenius's Revival from Death</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 126-126</div>
This was an error of the Kabbalists, and I suspect the Platonists were not averse from it, because of a well-known story told by Plato himself. Er, the son of Armenius, a Pamphylian by birth, had lain dead for ten days among the corpses of those slain in battle. He was brought home and afterwards, on the second day, when he should have been put on his funeral pyre, he came back to life and gave an account of some of the extraordinary things he had seen while he was among the dead.

<div align="center"><b>Clazomenae Man's Apparent Soul Journey</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 126-126</div>
There was a similar story about someone from Clazomenae in Book 3 of Origen\'s Against Celsus,

<div align="center"><b>Aristeas of Proconnesus's Repeated Revivals and Soul as Crow</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 126-127</div>
and one about Aristeas of Proconnesus which Pliny tells in \[Inquiry into Nature\] Book 7, chapter 52, and in his Life of Romulus, Plutarch, relying on Herodotus, tells us that the dead man came back to life many times and could recover his soul wherever he wanted, and that those who were standing nearby could see his soul in the shape of a crow, flying out of his mouth.

<div align="center"><b>White Weasel Emerging from Soldier's Mouth</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 127-127</div>
This man, however, was not dead, but in a magical trance, and it was not his soul which was flying out of his mouth, but a devilish conjuring-trick. The same has to be said about the white weasel which emerged from the sleeping soldier\'s mouth and went back in again, and then went back and forth across a small brook over a sword which someone had laid down. This story, told by Hélinand \[de Froidmont\], was a joke by an evil spirit who did it in order to impress on \[people\'s\] minds this error about the soul\'s migration, and to convince old women\'s fantasies and children still wet behind the ears, rather than natural philosophers.

<div align="center"><b>St Giles of Assisi on Soul Separation in Ecstasy</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 127-128</div>
I think this is how one must understand what is said about St Giles \[of Assisi\] in The Deeds of Franciscans. When he was asked whether during this life the soul sometimes left its body during an ecstasy or during contemplation, he replied that it did and that he knew a man to whom it happened; and he said it \[happened\] either because once every bodily function was removed, \[the soul\] forgot it completely, or because divine power was removed from the body and was then brought back into it. (Juan Salvador Trados tells the story in volume 1 of The Deeds and Sayings of the Illustrious Order of St Francis, p. 205).

<div align="center"><b>Burgos Virgin's Daily Raptures from Eucharist</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 128-129</div>
I know, and a thousand people still living know as well, that there was a virgin dedicated to God who was still alive in Burgos in Castile, in 1585-one of those they call \'beatas\' there. The moment she caught sight of the Eucharist and, scarcely had she received it in her mouth, than she was lifted into a rapture. This happened every day and to such an extent that she could not feel small pins applied to and driven into her feet, and because she was someone who was averse from human glory, she was in the habit of begging God, very ardently, (since she preferred to be holy rather than talked about or seen), not to let anything of this kind happen to her in front of other people; and when she came back to herself, she would hurry home as fast as possible, full of embarrassment and shamefacedness, and lock the door.

<div align="center"><b>Damme Crucifix Image Growing Hair and Beard</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 131-132</div>
So that I can pass over everything else, \[let me tell you this\]. In Damme, a town near Bruges, I once saw a very large wooden image of the crucified Saviour which had grown hair and a beard. These were shaved off every year but would grow back again. The devout considered this to be a miracle because of the frequent cures God used to confer upon the sick there, and other records of God\'s munificence were noted on a large number of tablets there until Calvinists, in their irreligious, obstinate folly, destroyed the image with fire and, as is their custom, razed the church to the ground.

<div align="center"><b>Linz Child Born with Post-Death Stigmata</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 132-132</div>
I can also quite happily class the following \[anecdote\] as a portent from God. \'While Charles of Austria was holding courts of justice in Linz\', says Pietro Bizzarri, \'a child was born to a peasant. There was nothing healthy about him at all. He lived for fourteen days, sweated for three days before his death, and sweated until he died. Immediately the stigmata began to appear on the dead child\'s hands and feet and in his side, just the way we see Christ\'s wounds depicted in paintings. A crown like a crown of thorns also made its appearance on his head, on the skull itself. When the Emperor heard this, he sent some of his people. They looked very carefully at the portent, and then gave him a detailed account of the whole thing, which made him realise that the situation was exactly as people had said it was.\' (See Bizzarri\'s Epitome of Remarkable Things in Europe, p. 302).

<div align="center"><b>The Shade of Creusa Appears to Aeneas</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 133-133</div>
Why, (unless it was because what the common people believed was also the opinion of those in the know as well), would the Phoebus of poets say of Anchises's son, 'The unhappy figure and shade of Creusa herself Appeared in front of my eyes, an image larger than the one I knew, [Aeneid 2.722-723].

<div align="center"><b>Cynthia's Post-Death Recounting</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 133-133</div>
Why, too, would the Latin Callimachus introduce Cynthia recounting such things as this after her death?

'The spirits of the dead do have a kind of existence. Death is not the end of everything, And the wan shade overcomes and escapes its pyre At night we are borne away and wander. Night sets free the shades which have been imprisoned, And Cerberus himself casts aside his padlock and goes wandering. Laws bid us return to Lethe’s fens at dawn. We are borne along, and the ferryman surveys the freight he is carrying', [Propertius, Elegies, 4.7.1-2, 89-92].

<div align="center"><b>Apuleius's Curse Involving Apparitions of the Dead</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 134-134</div>
Apuleius also says, in the first part of his Defence, Aemilianus, may the god Mercury who goes between the gods of the upper world and those of the lower grant you the ill favour of the gods of both, and always keep on casting in your way apparitions [species] of the dead, any shades [umbrarum] at any time, any spectres [lemurum], any spirits of the dead [Manium], and any goblin creatures [larvarum], and may he thrust upon your eyes all the terrors of the night, all the nightmares of the sepulchre, and all the horrors of the grave'.

<div align="center"><b>Moses Appears on Mount Tabor</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 138-139</div>
There is the suggestion that this is the way the law-giver Moses appeared along with Christ, our Lord, on the summit of Mt Tabor. I do not think [the idea] that Moses appeared in a body made of air for that occasion, as [Walafrid] Strabo and St Thomas [Aquinas] thought, is objectionable. One will be able to say that this was a genuine apparition in which Moses's real soul appeared, and one will also be accurate in saying that Moses 'appeared', because that is the preferable way of describing it. In his Care for the Dead [18], St Augustine, too, hints that he thought this apparition of Moses was not unlike that of Samuel, and no one says of Samuel that he appeared in his own body.

All the arguments brought against St Thomas [Aquinas], too, are irrelevant with the result that one cannot take the authority of the Fathers [of the Church], who use the word 'resurrection' in its broad sense, and apply it to Moses, saying that he is like someone 'rising up again' in a body he has assumed, which is an opinion to which [Cornelius] Jansen inclined in chapter 67 of his Harmony. But because in fact St Jerome, Epiphanius, and most of the Fathers use the word of a real resurrection-you have their words in [Francisco] Suárez-I shall not drag out the argument further.

I say, rather, that real resurrections have nothing to do with the subject I am discussing here, because I am talking about the appearance of the dead and Moses's [appearance] was no more going to be that of a dead man than [the appearance] of Elias, since Moses would have been alive at the time, and so would Elias. But what did a certain [author] say in The Transgressor Bound?³⁷¹ When Moses answers the evil spirit's question, 'Where have you come from, Moses?' he says, From the upper air, of course. Where I, fortunate man, was carried up with Enoch and Elias, And am awaiting God's final Day [of Judgement]'.

This must be read with caution, because if it means that Moses was carried up while he was still alive with Enoch and Elias who were not yet dead, that is undoubtedly a rash thing to say and contrary to Holy Scripture. If [it means he was carried up] with Elias and Enoch and will come at the Final Time, that is equally rash. If [it means] Enoch and Elias were carried up into the highest fiery heaven, this is clearly novel and unheard of, because anyone who has a share in that glory before death is a saint, a pilgrim who has attained his goal. More dangerous by far in the same work are Michael's and the good angels' bewailing the punishment of Lucifer and even accusing God of cruelty. Plainly that was about as valuable a thing to do as transforming Aeschylus's Prometheus into Lucifer and the Nereids into good angels! This was how daring the poet was prepared to be.

But to return to what I said about Moses: one needs to make the same answer about those who rose and appeared with the rising Lord. They, too, had returned to life and perhaps followed the Lord as He ascended into Heaven, and had no more part in death, if they merely appeared in their own body and informed it, as is very likely. Therefore, I deny that spirits [spiritus] assume their own bodies, (in order to appear in them, at any rate), and that they do so in order to inform them and give them life, because under those circumstances they are not returning in order to be what they are seen to be. They are doing so in order to furnish, by means of these [bodies] which are well-known [to others], a way in which they can be recognised, for which purpose a feigned body is perfectly adequate.

<div align="center"><b>Samuel's Apparition</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 138-138</div>
In his Care for the Dead [18], St Augustine, too, hints that he thought this apparition of Moses was not unlike that of Samuel, and no one says of Samuel that he appeared in his own body.

<div align="center"><b>The Resurrection Appearances with the Lord</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 139-139</div>
But to return to what I said about Moses: one needs to make the same answer about those who rose and appeared with the rising Lord. They, too, had returned to life and perhaps followed the Lord as He ascended into Heaven, and had no more part in death, if they merely appeared in their own body and informed it, as is very likely.

<div align="center"><b>Andreas of Rome's Journey to Hell</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 142-142</div>
So, what, (someone may say), do you say about those people whose souls, we read, have been told to go into their body again and, indeed, some [souls] which have been sentenced to the tortures of Hell and have been released therefrom? (An example is Andreas of Rome in 1070, whose story is well worth reading and can be found in Book 1, chapter 33 of the life of St Hanno of Köln in [Laurent] Sauer, Volume 6, for 4th December.)

<div align="center"><b>Angel Appears as Old Man to Hermit</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 143-143</div>
We find it written in the lives of the [Church] Fathers that an angel with the appearance and face of an old man appeared to a hermit who wanted to understand the inscrutable depth of God's judgements.

<div align="center"><b>Child Appears to St Augustine Pouring the Sea</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 143-143</div>
There is a very wellknown story about St Augustine. While he was giving thought to his commentaries on the Trinity, a child appeared to him. The child was making a vain, useless attempt to pour the sea, regardless of how big it is, into a small ditch with a spoon.

<div align="center"><b>Child Appears to Theophilus with Roses and Apples</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 143-143</div>
He could not be mistaken for an angel, any more than the other [apparition] in the very old story about St Dorothea's struggle. This one was like a very pretty, delicate four-year old child and had brought delightfully blushing roses and fresh apples to Theophilus in the middle of winter, just as the virgin, boldly trusting in God, had promised, even though everything in the area was bristling with frost and shrivelled up because it was so cold.

<div align="center"><b>Wisdom and Chastity Appear to St Gregory of Nazianzus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 143-143</div>
Rufinus [of Aquileia] and St Bonaventure testify that Wisdom and Chastity presented themselves to St Gregory of Naziazenus's sight and, unless this had happened to him while he was asleep, one might suspect they were angels in female form.

<div align="center"><b>Virgin Appears to St Lorenzo Giustiniani</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 143-143</div>
St Lorenzo Giustiniani claims that a virgin, brighter than the sun, appeared to him to tell him she was God's Wisdom who had taken human form for the sake of reforming humankind, and that God had given her to him [St Lorenzo as his bride. She then gave him the kiss of peace and withdrew. His biographers say this was our Lord Jesus appearing to him in that form. I should think it was an angel rather than [Jesus], because if Christ Himself appeared in female form, why do we not believe that angels sometimes make themselves seen in that same guise?

<div align="center"><b>Angel Appears as Dove to St Gommar</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 143-144</div>
There is a life of St Gommar who is venerated by my fellow Brabantines, recorded either by Theobald or some other ancient eloquent writer, in which you can find that an angel, sent by God, presented itself to be seen in the guise of a dove glowing with a remarkable brilliance on top of his sleeve. It advised him against a pilgrimage he intended to make, and suggested he change to loftier studies and labours. The holy man obeyed, and in nothing which took place afterwards can one detect the mocking deception of an evil spirit. On the contrary, several things [happened] from which it appears that this took place on God's instructions.

(I am not unmindful that evil spirits have, in the past, misused the image of a dove which has been set apart as [a symbol of] the Holy Spirit). So I am persuaded that this apparition was that of a good angel.

<div align="center"><b>Angel Appears as Eagle to Wambert</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 144-144</div>
