<div align="center"><b>Tyrannus Pretending to be Saturn</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 181</div>
or the one invented about a heathen priest, Tyrannus, who lied about being Saturn so that he could be free to commit adultery with well-born married women. (Rufinus [of Aquileia] tells us about this, and St Cyril of Alexandria mentions this same villain in Against Julian Book 7).

<div align="center"><b>Nectanebo's Deception as Jupiter Amun</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 181</div>
Nectanebo, an Egyptian magician, transformed himself into the likeness of Jupiter Amun and deceived Olympias, Philip [of Macedon\'s] wife, and from this adulterous union was born Alexander the Great-which is how he got the idea that Jupiter was his father. Joseph ben Gorion tells the story in Book 2, chapter 6 of his History of the Jews, and praises the shrewd historians of Egyptian affairs, Titus [Livy] of Rome, Strabo of Cappadocia, and Nicolas of Damascus.

<div align="center"><b>Visions of Drunks</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 181-182</div>
In [Book 1], chapter 8, Le Loyer tells us that change or defect in the senses is not an infrequent cause of people\'s seeing apparitions because their sight, or one of their other senses, is deceiving them. He gives the example of drunks about whom St Ambrose gives a very good account. \'This is why meaningless images come to drunks, whose sight is untrustworthy and unsteady. They often jump over shadows as though these were little pits; the ground shakes, along with their face, and seems to rear itself up and lean away from them, and thinking it is whirling around them, they rush straight ahead in fear and clutch at the earth with their hand-or they think mountains are racing together and imprisoning them. They suffer from a roaring in their ears like the crashing of sea waves and the thunder of the surf on the sea-shore. If they see dogs, they think they are lions and take to their heels. Some dissolve into confused laughter; others wail in inconsolable grief, and others see things which make them irrationally afraid; and they dream while they are awake and quarrel while they are asleep.

<div align="center"><b>The Man in Argos Who Applauded an Empty Theater</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 182</div>
As Horace puts it in his Letter to Florus: \'At Argos there was once a man, not undistinguished, Who used to believe he was listening to wonderful tragic actors, And sat happily and applauded in an empty theatreSomeone who would perform all the other duties of life in a perfectly straightforward manner, Undoubtedly a good neighbour, an amiable host, Courteous to his wife: someone who could forgive his slaves And not go mad if the neck of a bottle had been damaged: Someone who could avoid a rock or an open well. This man was restored to health by his kinsfolk\'s help and care. But when his sickness and its bile had been driven out of him by a strong dose of hellebore And he came to himself again, he said, \"Oh dear, oh dear, my friends, you have killed me, Not saved me, because you have taken away a pleasure, And removed by force a delusion of my mind which gave me much delight,\" [Epistles 2.2.vv.129-140].

<div align="center"><b>Thrasyllus and the Ships in Piraeus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 182</div>
In his book on the extraordinary things one hears, Aristotle tells us that a similar thing happened in Abydos and, in connection with the pleasure a mad false notion [can give], in Book 4 of his Motley History, Aelian gives evidence at length about someone called Thrasyllus who thought that every ship which came to Piraeus belonged to him.

<div align="center"><b>The Trireme House in Agrigentum</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 182-183</div>
But let us return to imaginary apparitions. I don\'t know of anything which has happened less frequently than an incident related by Timaeus of Locri. \'There was a house in Agrigentum which was called \"The Trireme\" because of the following incident. Some young men\', (from Tauromenium), \'were attending a drinks party there. They started to become somewhat merry and, as usually happens not long afterwards, when they had become well and truly flushed, they were at such mental cross-purposes and so beside themselves that they thought they had been deposited in a trireme, and that a dreadful storm was tossing them on a sea full of swelling and plunging waves. So, as though they were afraid that the ship would be wrecked because the things in the house were being thrown around, in order to lessen [the effects of that shipwreck, they started to throw all the plates and anything else they came across in the fuddled state, into the road, or \"sea\", and people ran from all over the place to plunder what had been thrown away. The next day, when they came before magistrates who charged them with what they had done, they replied, (because they had not had enough sleep), that the fear of death had made them prefer to destroy what was in the ship rather than be devoured, themselves, by the waves. Everyone was amazed at their stupidity, and one of their number who seemed to be somewhat older, said, \"Sons of Neptune, I was so afraid, I threw myself under the beds and lay down in the bottom [of the boat]\". Since they were seen to be even more out of their wits, they were dismissed by the examining magistrate, who quite seriously impressed upon them that they should be wary of any other brutish invitation of this kind. They, however, pretending to thank him, said, \"If we are preserved from such stormy waves and make our way back to harbour, we shall raise statues to you in our own country as our saviours, along with [those] of other gods of the sea, Sons of Neptune\". (Thus Caelius Rhodiginus, deriving [the story] from Athenaeus.)

<div align="center"><b>Hydrophobia Visions of the Biting Dog</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 183</div>
People suffering from hydrophobia run away from water because in it they see the dog which bit them.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil's Influence Through the Senses</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 183</div>
\'This evil, says St Augustine, \'which comes from the Devil, creeps through every entrance of the senses, gives itself to shapes, adapts itself to colours, adheres to sounds, underlies smells, pours itself in by means of flavours, and fills all the pathways of understanding with little clouds. Sometimes it tortures cruelly with grief and fear; sometimes it distracts by means of jokes, or mocks by means of stageplays,\' [Book of Eighty-Three Questions].

<div align="center"><b>The Evil Spirits Disturbing the Loreto College</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 184-185</div>
I can confirm this, because it actually happened at the start of my own Society, when a college was founded by Fr. Ignatius [Loyola] in Loreto. Its rector-in charge was Fr. Olivier Manare, who was still with us at the time, and who gave me an absolutely accurate account of it, which I shall add now. In 1555 malicious spirits [spiritus] were disturbing first one place, then another with loud crashing and banging, causing a remarkable amount of trouble, and depriving a very large number [of brethren] of their rest, among whom several were novices in the religious life. They also used to appear in broad daylight in different shapes. An evil spirit appeared to one brother in the form of an 'Aithiopian' with many enticing and persuasive words to make him leave the religious life. But when he saw that his effort was in vain, he went away, accompanied by very heavy breathing and a foul stench which lasted the whole day.

He manifested himself to another brother in the likeness of St Paul and began to urge him to a devout reading and study of his Epistles so that, on this specious pretext, he could draw him away from his attention to his liberal studies and thus push him into disobedience and out of the religious community-which he was successful in doing. He met Olivier [Manare] himself, in the shape of a very large black Molossian hound, jumped up at him and nearly threw him to the ground. He would thrust and push in the form of a mewing cat against the bench on which brethren were resting while they prayed, and rub up against them more than once, in order to divert or destroy their concentration.

There was no peace while the brethren were asleep, and meal-times were not allowed to pass without disturbance. Those who were resting would repeatedly feel little cats climbing on to the bed and getting under the covers, and this filled many brethren with fear and would deprive them of sleep and make them get out from under their blankets. While he was having breakfast, one brother was struck so hard on his side by the fist of an unseen assailant that he turned very pale at once, and almost fell backwards off his bench.

This molestation of his flock caused the rector a lot of annoyance. He tried [those of] the Church's remedies which would meet the case-Masses, prayers, exorcisms, holy water, holy relics, and other such things. But God did not want the evil to be removed entirely by these. Therefore, Olivier had recourse to the father they all had in common, St Ignatius, and asked for his prayers and help in this molestation. Because he was clearly depressed, Ignatius wrote him a letter, exhorting him to patience and urging him to use those remedies the Church usually employs in similar situations. 'We have already looked to these and tried them all', said Olivier in a letter, 'but the evil is still with us and is becoming worse'.

When he received this letter, Fr. Ignatius replied, 'Put your trust in the Lord, and in His goodness. He will set you free, and I shall pray earnestly for you.' When he received this letter, the rector read it to a circle of Fathers and brethren who by chance had gathered together at the time and, (marvellous to relate), that infestation of apparitions and phantasms instantly stopped, with the result that nothing of the kind was heard or seen again afterwards in Loreto. From a letter written later, we are left in no doubt that the best of Fathers began to pray for his sons and kept on praying as long as letters were carried from Rome to Loreto.

<div align="center"><b>The Deacon Gerontius and She with Asses' Legs</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 187</div>
Sozomen tells us in his [History of the Church] Book 8, chapter 6, where he mentions Gerontius, the deacon, who was punished by St Ambrose and discharged from his service by Nectarius because he kept on insisting he had got hold of 'She with asses' legs' at night by her shaved head and thrown her into a mill. The holy men thought, not without good reason, that this meant he had been trafficking with the demon 'She with asses' legs', although they did not condemn him for maintaining his belief in evil spirits of this name. This is how one should interpret what Nectarius and Ambrose did.

<div align="center"><b>Watery Spirits Informing Shepherds of Syracuse's Capture</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 188</div>
There were watery evil spirits who, on the very day Syracuse was captured by the Saracens, gave the news to Peloponnesian shepherds who were near their swamp. (See [Tomasso] Fazello, Two Decades on Sicilian Affairs Book 6).

<div align="center"><b>The Perjurer Plunged into the Palisci Lakes</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 188</div>
Because of the attentions of this kind of evil spirit, a person who perjured himself was plunged into the lakes of the Palisci, (a place which is now called Naphthia), as Aristotle, Diodorus, and Macrobius tell us.

<div align="center"><b>The Spring of Diana and Unmixable Wine</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 188</div>
Near the town of Ihomisum [Comiso in Sicily], there is the 'spring of Diana' which flows into Camarina. In ancient times, if any with dirty hands diluted wine with this water, the wine and water would not mix.

<div align="center"><b>The Overflowing Spring in Alesinum</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 188</div>
In the territory of Alesinum, today known as Caronia, there was once a spring which was usually calm, but when pipes were played harmoniously, it would start to swell and overflow its banks. (See Fazello, first decade, Book 1, chapter 5).

<div align="center"><b>The Dancing Troops of Subterranean Spirits</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 189</div>
\'Sometimes at night, adds \[Trithemius\], \'troops of them come out from the mountains and lead wonderful, amazing dances on the plains \[below\] and, as though they are in fear of their single leader, at his signal they suddenly vanish and return to their lurking places.

Occasionally one hears the sound of little bells in the midst of them, and sometimes they pretend they are the spirits of dead human beings.\' Pliny tells a memorable story about their dances and the different sound \[they make\] on Mt Atlas, (Inquiry into Nature Book 5, chapter 1.) So does Solinus, The Wonders of the World, chapter 37. He calls these evil spirits \'Aegipanes\', and Saxo the Grammarian describes the dance and gives the names of every kind of evil spirit in Book 1 of his history of Denmark.

\'The savage troop of dancing ghosts is carried forward and runs to and fro along the wind, And makes harsh noises in the direction of the stars. Fauns come together with satyrs, and a body of Pans Mingled with spirits of the dead fights with a wild look on their faces. Swarthy \[spirits\]⁴⁷⁸ come together with those who dwell in forests, and hobgoblins who do harm Are eager to share the stony footpath with vampire witches. Furies leap forward and the hobgoblins crowd together with them; And a prophetess, together with sub-nosed \[spirits\], jostles them\'.

<div align="center"><b>The Necromancer's Encounter with the Sybil of Nursus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 190-191</div>
In his book, The Hatred of Satan, discourse 6, \[Crespet\] refers to a Domenico Mirabelli from Arpino. He, along with his step-mother, Marguerite Garnier and other accomplices, was arrested in Mantes la Jolie in France and brought to Paris, along with books on magic which they were taking to be consecrated by the presiding sybils of magic.

This Domenico confessed in court that an associate of his, known as \'the Scotsman\', who had lived in France for a long time, had the reputation of being a necromancer and had carried out remarkable trials of his skill in front of various princes. Quite a few of them had become his students, \[although\] he had taught them nothing worthwhile. This Scotsman had made his way to and met the famous Sybil who, people said, lived in the Cave of Nursus in Italy. He reported that she was a tiny woman who sat on a low stool, and that her hair was loose and hung down to the ground. She gave him a consecrated book and an evil spirit enclosed in a ring to wear on his finger. The book and the evil spirit could be made to work anywhere he might wish, as long as an adverse wind was not blowing on him as he was about to go there. (Do laugh, Reader! The wind is stronger than the evil spirit? Do laugh, too, at the following pieces of fiction regarding magic).

He also said that the supreme Pontiff had placed guards who guard the cave with the utmost care so that no one can take himself off, go there, and consult the sibyl, and that no one can reach her except those who have been initiated into magic and know how to make themselves invisible. This is because when anyone is speaking with her, whether he is a magician or not, dreadful storms and lightning-flashes disturb the whole surrounding countryside.

This is what \[Marco\] Stellato meant to imply in these verses from Book 2:

\'Either the gods of the air or the stars send these winds. Indeed, often when a magician wants to remove Treasure which is lying hidden in the earth, Or to consecrate a book, Or to bring any deity under his control by means of a magic ritual, I have been told he raises a wind and a sudden storm\'.

(Crespet adds that these Sibyls willingly guard flocks of sheep and live among cattle. This is not the reason. It is because shepherds and herdsmen are usually rather simple-minded and somewhat dull-witted, and have plenty of time on their hands, and so are more exposed to being ambushed by these evil spirits.)

<div align="center"><b>The Avenging Spirit Temesius of Temesa</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 193</div>
The radapnaios \[murderer, avenging spirit\] Temesius which used to trouble Temesa was one of these. He pretended he was the soul of Polita who had been murdered by the Bruttii. The Greek have a great deal to say about this one. He would ape the angel who wrestled with the patriarch Jacob.

<div align="center"><b>St Antony's Encounter with a Satyr in the Desert</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 193</div>
In his biography of St Antony, St Athanasius says that St Antony met one of these in the desert.

<div align="center"><b>Pan's Killing of Peasants in the Forested Mountains</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 195</div>
According to Eusebius Preparation of the Gospel Book 5, \[chapter 6\], the oracle of Dodonian Apollo of Branchidae replied that, with the high-pitched sound of his incantation and the dreadful appearance of his body which was contorted in frenzy, Pan killed nine peasants who were cutting wood in the \'forested mountains\', údnevta xαì ойреа, and that he would have killed more had not \"Apteuis åypotépn \[Artemis of the countryside\] restrained him, (that is, a stronger evil spirit holding back a weaker).

<div align="center"><b>The Noonday Evil Spirit of Eastern Russia</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 196</div>
In eastern Russia, an evil spirit had the habit of roaming the countryside at noon during harvest time, dressed like a grieving widow, and would break the arms and legs of labourers unless they immediately fell flat on the ground at the sight of the apparition and worshipped it.

<div align="center"><b>The Disemboweled Apparition in the Salt-Pits of Peru</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 196</div>
In the salt-pits of Peru, behind the River Consata, an apparition of gigantic size appeared to the Indians. Its stomach had been cut open and it had been disembowelled. It was carrying two small children in its arms, made dire threats, and then vanished. The ignorant people made light of it because it appeared while the sun was shining, but a dreadful, fatal plague followed its threats, as \[Pedro\] Cieza confirms in Part 1, chapter 24 of his history of Peru.

<div align="center"><b>The Plague Demons of Chosroe and Justinian</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Page 196</div>
He writes that it was recorded that during the widespread, extraordinary plague which depopulated the whole world at the time of Chosroe and Justinian, evil spirits looking like human beings were seen busying themselves in public and private places. They struck down those they met, and those whom they had struck down were immediately wasted by disease, (\'a particularly virulent, burning blast which is why the Jews call these evil spirits Mererim and Rhessaphim, \'deadly\' and \'burning\' or \'fiery\'\'). \[Procopius says\] this happened to some people while they were awake in broad daylight, (there you have the evil spirit at midday), and to some while they were asleep at night, (there you have the evil spirit of the night).

<div align="center"><b>The Ghost of Gello Bringing Death to Children</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 198-198</div>
Another was \'Gelo\' about whom Nicephorus tells us the following while, among other things, he is talking about the things which foretold Maurice\'s becoming Emperor. \'When his mother was giving birth, she said that a strange, constantly changing, sweet smell was given off by the earth. She added-and this sounds like an old wives\' tale, and I have grave doubts about it-that very often a female called \"Empusa\", (but someone else said was \"Gilo\"), took the child out of its cradle as if she intended to eat him, but that she was unable to do him any harm at all\'.

<div align="center"><b>Women Accused of Infant Murder Through Closed Doors</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 198-199</div>
This was partly a bit of old women\'s nonsense, partly not nonsense but actually true. It was nonsense and out of step with reason to think that these women\'s bodies became so small that they could come through closed doors and push the bodies they had been born with through them. (See what I said in question 17.) \[But\] it was true that, with the help of an evil spirit, these wicked females were sometimes in the habit of entering the bedrooms-regardless of the fact that the size of their bodies could not change and penetrate solid objects-and once there making every effort to destroy the children. Consequently, the judge who is unwilling to be ensnared by credulity or incredulity when it comes to these matters must distinguish carefully between them, and he must examine very carefully indeed all the circumstances relating to the judgements he is going to make, because it can happen that a judge acquits the accused of one of the charges of deception which have been brought against him, although he would have been found guilty had the charge been brought in a different way. For example, suppose some vampire witch \[strix\] is accused of having entered \[somewhere\] through closed doors, having changed her body into spirit form, and thus, no longer in physical form, has killed the children. No judge will be stupid enough to believe this accusation, and if the accused denies it, there is no agreed evidence from someone other than \[the accuser\] that the children have been killed or harmed, and the accuser cannot prove his case other than by referring to the story in \[Ovid\'s\] Metamorphoses, she will undoubtedly receive a verdict of acquittal. If you read carefully the biography of Tarasius, written by the monk Ignatius, you will have no difficulty in understanding that this was the situation in the case of the women acquitted by George, (not Philip, as some people call him), the city governor, and that it was the cruelty and ignorance of Isaurus Leo which had put them in that position because, in his incredible stupidity, he believed that the women had changed themselves into spirit form. Listen to what Ignatius says. \'Some poverty-stricken women had been accused of murdering infants at the breast by coming through a hole in the house \[walls\]⁵ or through closed doors, and killing the children before anyone realised it was happening. They were hauled into court by those who believed fairy stories\'-he is talking about the story of Gillo which had been made up by pagans in ancient times-\'and were unwilling to follow the teaching of Christ, our God, which is not in the least deceived by ghosts \[spectris\] and mental apparitions \[phantasmatibus\]. In the fairy stories told by the Greeks, you see, there is a woman called \"Gello\" who is said to have died before her time and to have come in various spectral forms to newly-born infants and killed them\'. (There\'s a fairy story for you! A dead woman came back and committed infanticide!) \'Having been enticed and deceived by the wicked spirit \[improbo spiritu\] of this story, those who were making a plausible job of saying these things tried to transfer this cursed crime to the women by saying that they had changed themselves into spirit form and that they were the reason \[the children\] were dying before their time\'. You see the basis of the charge, Reader. They single out \[the notion\] that the dead Gello had killed the little children; it is discovered that many of them have been carried off suddenly; therefore, it is probable it was done by these women who got rid of their body\'s bulk, changed themselves into spirit form, and in this fashion inserted themselves through solid objects or thin cracks and murdered \[the children\]. Isn\'t the writer justified in exclaiming against such accusers in the following terms? \'What stupidity! What blindness of the eyes of the heart! If the body, which has been put together and moulded by length, depth, and breadth were dissolved into spirit form, and allowed to do this, then Christ, who is truth itself, says, \"A spirit does not have flesh and bones\", \[Luke 24.39\]. It is those who make this assertion who think it is a phantom, namely, \[those who have said\] that these women were not physical beings, but spirits, or had been endowed with this spiritual power of penetrating \[solid objects\]. Moreover Christ, who took real flesh and rightly told His disciples that a spirit does not have flesh and bones, will not be limited by any object He meets to being a figment of the \[passive\] imagination. Thinking along these lines, therefore, and making a judgement without much hesitation, George acquitted these women of the crimes.\'

<div align="center"><b>Adam the Lay-Brother's Encounter with a Shape-Shifting Evil Spirit</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 200-200</div>
A more recent example, (but you will scarcely find one like it), was recorded for posterity by \[Robert\] Gaguin in his History of the French. During the reign of Philippe le Bel, a lay-brother, (or \'convert\' as the French call them), from the Monastery of Vallis Sarnarii, by the name of Adam, was riding along the road very early in the morning with a serf-attendant a little before Christmas on monastery business when an enormous tree, covered in frost and rime, presented itself to him on the way, hurrying towards him as though to join its pace and bulk to his. His terrified horse jerked its head back and tried to go back the way it had come. With an immense effort Adam restrained the animal and spurred it forward. When it was quite close to the tree, the tree suddenly vanished from sight, leaving behind a dreadful smell of sulphur. From this Adam deduced that the apparition was an evil spirit, and fell to prayer, commending himself to God. But he did not interrupt his journey. A little while later he looked back and saw that a black man, sitting on a chestnut-brown horse, was following him. \'How is it\', he said, \'wretched, damned \[creature\], that you dare attack me with these tricks of yours? Don\'t you know that my brethren back in the monastery are pouring out prayers and supplications to God for my safety? Go away and be quick about it. I have nothing in which you can be interested and possess nothing which belongs to you\'. The wicked \[spirit\] went away but, lo and behold, returned a moment or two later in the shape of a man with a long, slender neck. Adam, sick of being bothered so often, drew a sword which had been fastened to his saddle, and waved it in the air two or three times. It split the air so quickly that it made a whistling sound. He tried to drive the apparition away, but it suddenly changed its appearance and stood where it was in the form of a man of middling height, wearing a hood on its head and a monk\'s scapular. Its eyes sparkled like steel or bronze caught in the sun\'s rays. Adam tried to stab his enemy several times, but his blows had no effect or force and merely parted the air. Then, in greater contempt and derision, the evil spirit took the form of an ass. The servant was terrified and persuaded his master to draw a circle on the ground with his sword and to make a cross with arms of equal length in the middle of it. Adam followed his advice, but the old fox did not stop. It changed its ass\'s ears into horns and in this shape, and in a most threatening manner, pretended it wanted to burst into the circle. Finally, it had had enough of the game and turned itself into a wheel which then rolled forward and attacked the monk. Carried by its continuous rolling, it crossed over the circle, leaving the monk completely unharmed, and disappeared from sight. The long sickness which followed his servant\'s terror, the long-lasting stench of Adam\'s clothes, the hoarseness of his voice and his difficulty in breathing from which he found it difficult to recover, assured people of the truth of what he and his servant had been through. So did the sudden change in his horse which, after this vision, \[ceased to be\] tame and tractable and started to be fierce, to throw its rider to the ground, and to kick out.

<div align="center"><b>Evil Spirits as Armed Soldiers Attacking Monks</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 201-201</div>
In the Life of St Norbert, chapter 26, (and also in the life of St Guthlac the Hermit, in Sauer, under the month of April), one reads that evil spirits under the appearance of armed soldiers attacked a number of monks.

<div align="center"><b>Monk Limpugius Encounters Phantom Army of Dead Soldiers Near Worms</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 201-201</div>
In Trithemius\'s Chronicle of Hirsau, we read, under the year 1168, that a very large number of cavalry and infantry, drawn up in battle-lines as though going to fight, appeared near Worms to a monk \[called\] Limpugius. When the monk was dating enough to ask who they were, they replied they were not real, living soldiers, but the souls of soldiers, and that long since they had died in this very spot in the service of a certain prince. Their uniforms, weapons, and horses, \[they said\], which had been tools of sin while they were alive were evidence of their punishment, now they were dead. Everything he saw round them was merely the fire in which they were being burned and he could help them with his prayers and supplications.

<div align="center"><b>Phantom Tournament After Battle Near Montenaken</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 201-201</div>
Johannes Caesarius tells us that the night after the Duke of Brabant\'s army was slaughtered by the men of Liège, round about the beginning of the night, one of the Count of Loon\'s servants saw a very big tournament held by evil spirits near Montenaken, which was where the slaughter had taken place. A Spanish priest, too, while walking from one village to another at dusk near the Count\'s castle, saw an immense tournament of dead people screaming and shouting, \'Sir Walter of Milene! Sir Walter of Milene!\', this Walter being a well-known knight who had recently died. \[Caesarius\] adds that this vision quite often vanished that night and then appeared again.

<div align="center"><b>Knight Beheaded by Phantom Horseman in Bohemia</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 201-202</div>
Johannes Nider tells the following story. \'When the kingdom of Bohemia was being shaken by insurrection and slaughter on account of heresies, not far from the borders of the kingdom, next to a particular valley, people heard shouting at night and quite often saw presses of horsemen who were dressed in different colours. On the occasion I am describing, there were two rather bold knights in a castle not very far from the spot where these prodigies were happening. The knights wanted to know if there was any truth at all in these visions and hastily went at night to a boundary wall from which people had been accustomed to see them. But before they dared come near \[those visions\], one \[knight\] had pangs of doubt and said to the other man, \"We\'ve seen them. Let that be enough. I\'m not going anywhere near them. What\'s the old saying? \'One shouldn\'t make a joke of this kind of thing\". The other \[knight\] rebuked him for being timid, spurred his horse on, and man and horse drew near all the foresaid armed men. One \[horseman\] came out \[from the crowd\], cut off \[the knight\'s\] head, and rejoined his companions, and when the timid knight saw this, he fled and told everyone the dreadful things which had just happened. The headless body was discovered the next day, along with its head, in the valley where the armed men had been seen, but no trace of any human being or horse was found. However, certain signs and traces of something ill-omened were seen in the mud\'.

<div align="center"><b>Soldier Rides with Dead Cook and Phantom Army to Jerusalem</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 202-203</div>
\[Nider\] adds that he was told this story, and another one, by the Archbishop of Mainz, a very hard-working man and a person worthy of being believed. \'He told me\', (says \[Nider\]), \'the name of a soldier from the Rhine whom he knew very well, and whose son was alive at the time. This soldier had always been a prominent figure among almost all the nobles of Lower Germany because in warlike situations he was completely fearless. Because of his high-spiritedness and courage, he used to carry out serious raids and sometimes suffered them in his turn at the hands of other people. In pursuit of these \[raids\] he was in the habit of riding out-not always during the day, but also at night-to places where he would have the advantage. So on one particular night, with some hand-picked servants, he wanted to ride through a forest near the Rhine and entered it where it started. But before he came to the end of the forest, beyond which lay a broad open space, just as people do when they fear an ambush, he sent a servant ahead to where the forest ended to see whether there was any ambush \[waiting for him\] on the open space, because \[such an ambush\] could be seen under the light of the moon and the twinkling of the stars. So when the servant peered through the branches of the trees and saw a quite extraordinary army strung together the length of the open space and approaching on horseback. When he had seen this, he reported the results of his inspection to the soldier who said, \"Let\'s stay where we are for the moment, because it\'s likely that others are following close behind as a rear-guard for this army. We\'ll make a dash towards them, and we\'ll not be afraid of \[their\] small numbers, whether the advance troops turn out to be hostile or friendly\". Then, after a brief delay, the soldier and his men left the forest, came into the open space, and saw no one except a man sitting on a horse, following his people at a distance, and leading another horse. The soldier came towards him and asked him who he was, saying, \"Aren\'t you my cook?\" (because, from a distance, that is who he thought it was. The soldier\'s cook had died not long previously.) \[The man\] answered, \"Yes, my lord.\" The soldier said, \"What are you doing here, and who are the men who came before you did?\" The dead man replied, \"My lord, those who preceded me are the nobles and knights such and such.\" (He gave many of them their own names, you see.) \"They and I have to be in Jerusalem tonight, because this is our punishment.\" The soldier asked again, \"Why do you need the horse you are leading when there\'s no one sitting on it?\" \"It will be at your service\", came the reply, \"if you want to come to the Holy Land with me. Don\'t be alarmed. I shall bring you there and back alive, on my word as a Christian, if you heed my warnings.\" Then the soldier said, \"I have attempted many extraordinary things in my time. I\'ll add this strange \[experience\] to them.\" While his servants tried to persuade him not to, the soldier jumped down from his own horse, mounted the dead man\'s horse, and both riders were carried away from the servants\' eyes. The next day, however, the servants waited in accordance with their instructions, and the soldier and the dead man returned to the place where they had first met. Then the dead man said to the soldier, \"So that you don\'t think this vision was an entire fiction, I am giving you two unusual things. Keep them in memory of me.\" Then he produced a small piece of cloth made from asbestos and a little knife in a sheath. \"When the \[cloth\] is dirty\", he said, \"clean it with fire. When you draw the knife, do so carefully because if it cuts you, it will poison you.\"

<div align="center"><b>Ghost Soldiers Assault Polish Camp During Siege of Naclum</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 203-204</div>
\[Marcin\] Kromer, a careful writer, says, \'The King of Poland, Vladislaus I, was besieging Naclum, a very well-fortified castle in Pomerania. The sentries there quite often saw lines, as it were of soldiers, in the moonlight, galloping towards the Polish camp and assaulting it from the open fields. This happened fairly frequently and so the Poles became angry and restless, and since the enemy nowhere got as far as open fighting, one night, when they were told the enemy was at hand at that very moment, they gave a great roar and charged out of their camp in a great mass and, in a fruitless attempt, pursued them as they fled in different directions. The besieged \[Pomeranians\] heard the noise the Poles were making and suddenly burst out from the fortress in a hostile charge. They worked quickly to spread \[fire\] round in several places while the few who had stayed behind in the fortress defended themselves, and easily set the siege-engines and a large part of the camp on fire. They claimed that the perpetrators were ghosts of the night who, under the appearance of a hostile army, attacked the Poles this way with God\'s permission, the particular reason being that they had violated the Lenten fast which should be observed by all Christians, and had rashly and heedlessly eaten flesh and food containing milk in defiance of the usages and ordinances of the Catholic Church.\'

<div align="center"><b>Frightening Woman Lashing Air Before Antioch Rebellion</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 204-204</div>
On the night before a rebellion by the people of Antioch, an enormous woman with a frightening expression on her face was seen lashing the air so forcefully with a whip that the whole city could hear the noise.

<div align="center"><b>Ghastly Apparition Warns Patriarch Gennadius of Coming Peril</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 204-204</div>
A ghastly apparition appeared to Gennadius, the Patriarch of Constantinople, while he was approaching an altar in the church in order to beg God to keep the people safe, and when he addressed it in holy fashion it replied that as long as Gennadius was alive, it was not permitted to inflict the damage which was threatening the Church, but that once he was dead, it would attack it with great savagery.

<div align="center"><b>Tall Man Cries Warning on Mt Ziquenus Before Persecution</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 204-204</div>
Before \[King\] Hunnericus initiated his persecution of Catholics in Africa, an immensely tall man was seen on the summit of Mt Ziquenus and heard to cry out, \'Go away! Go away!\'

<div align="center"><b>Aithiopians Chase Saints from Faustus's Church in Carthage</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 204-204</div>
In Faustus\'s church, which is in Carthage, a large number of \'Aithiopians\' was seen chasing away the church\'s saints and driving them in front of them.

<div align="center"><b>Aunaberg Mine Spirit Kills Miners with Breath</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 205-206</div>
Such was the Aunaberg evil spirit which killed more than twelve miners with its breath in a pit which is called \"Rosenkrantz\", and for this reason \[the pit\] was abandoned, even though it was extremely rich in silver. When \[the spirit\] appeared in the likeness of a horse with a long neck and savage eyes, it would discharge its breath through a mouth gaping wide.

<div align="center"><b>Schneeberg Mine Spirit Lifts Miner in Georgenthal</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 206-206</div>
The \"Schneeberg\" was the same kind of \[evil spirit\] and wore a black cowl. It once lifted a miner off the ground in the Georgenthal mine and placed him, not without scraping his body raw, in a higher spot in that immense cavern which was rich in silver.

<div align="center"><b>She-Goat Spirit Haunts Jew's Mine in Turkey</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 206-206</div>
A Jew in Turkey was forced to abandon a very profitable mine because of an evil spirit of the mine, which would frequently appear to people in the form of a she-goat with golden horns.

<div align="center"><b>King Kavadh Confronts Treasure-Guarding Spirits</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 207-207</div>
When Kavadh \[I\], King of Persia, accepted a very large treasure consisting of very precious stones, gold, and silver in the very well-fortified stronghold of Zudader which is on the boundary between India and Persia, it was being guarded by evil spirits in order to prevent all mortals from gaining access to it. When \[the King\] came with an army and tried to seize the castle by force, the evil spirits rush out and fought bravely and \[the King\] was driven back. So, he had recourse to the magic of the Jews and the Magi, but this too turned out to be fruitless. \'So\', says Kedrenos, \'he was persuaded that he could get hold of it with the help of Christians\' prayers to God, and asked the bishop of the Christians living in Persia for help. The bishop ordered the Christians to fast, pray, and take communion. He celebrated the divine mysteries, came to \[the fortress\], invoked the evil spirits who were dwelling there, drove them out, and handed the castle over to Kavadh without any problem\'.

<div align="center"><b>Magician Germanicus Buried by Cave Guardian</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 207-207</div>
Note, too, the story of Germanicus, a magician who went into a cave where a treasure had been hidden, while his companion stayed just out with the entrance. \[Germanicus\] saw a dog, which was entirely black, lying on the chest. Cupidity, however, drove away fear and he stepped forward a little. The earth gave way, and he was buried in the cave.

<div align="center"><b>The Soldier Deceived by a Beast's Corpse</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 210-210</div>
on one occasion [an evil spirit] assumed the corpse of a brute beast and deceived a soldier who thought he had possessed a beautiful young woman, only to find he was lying in the embrace of an animal's putrefying, stinking corpse

<div align="center"><b>The Ghost of Polycritus Eating His Son</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 210-210</div>
and I have written in more than one place about the apparition of Polycritus as a ghost which ate his son.

<div align="center"><b>The Apparition of Philinnion</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 210-210</div>
There is an example similar to this which Phlegon of Tralles related, but he says he relied on the testimony of an eye-witness. His story runs as follows, [although] I shall give it in abbreviated form. Philinnion, the daughter of Damostratos and Charito, fell in love with Machates, her father's guest. Her parents did not take kindly to this. She died of grief and had a public funeral. Philinnion came to [Machates] and, after they had talked for a long time, she had sex with him more than once that evening. They had dinner together and Machates gave her an iron ring and a gilded cup.

She, in her turn, gave him a gold ring and an enchanted pectoral. Her nurse went solicitously to make sure the guest did not lack anything and saw the girl she had nursed lying in bed with him. Delighted, she told [Philinnion's] parents and they, full of astonishment, ran [to the room] and, on the second day, took the two of them by surprised. With a wail, they embraced their daughter who was sleeping alongside their guest. But Philinnion looked at them grimly and said, "Father, Mother, you were cruel to your daughter, and because of your prejudice against me I have been enjoying my Machates in my father's house for fully three days. Whether it was over-anxiousness on your part or lack of feeling, it will stay with you and cause you great sorrow. You will grieve all over again and bury your daughter a second time".

She finished speaking, turned very pale, and her corpse collapsed like a dead person again-or rather, it actually was dead-and her parents, overwhelmed with grief, collapsed at the same time. The people and magistrates found out about this incident and ordered that [Philinnion's] tomb be opened. They found no body there, only the iron ring and gilded cup Machates had given her. Philinnion's corpse was found in the bedroom, on the bed, and because it was a monstrous portent, on the advice of Hyllos, an augur, it was thrown outwith the city bounds to be devoured by birds and wild animals. Machates himself, realising he had been deceived by an apparition and unable to bear the shame, killed himself not long afterwards.

<div align="center"><b>Beelzebub Assumes Hanged Man's Body in Nicole d'Obry's Possession</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 210-210</div>
In the story of Nicole d'Obry, the possessed woman of Laon, too, we read that the devil Beelzebub assumed the body of a hanged man and tried to drive the exorcist away from her.

<div align="center"><b>The Animated Corpse of the German Prince</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 210-211</div>
what Caesarius, the monk of Heisterbach, writes about the same subject in Book 12, chapter 3 of his [Discussion of Miracles. After the body of a dead German prince had been buried for a whole year, an evil spirit got hold of it and, as though he were giving it life, exercised control over it. (This is how one should interpret [Caesarius] when he writes, 'the spirit quickened the [body] in place of its soul'.)

<div align="center"><b>Devil Inhabiting a Man's Body for Years</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 210-211</div>
Caesarius adds similar things, (which one can read in his biography of St Patrick, the Apostle of the Irish), about someone whose body a devil inhabited for many years in the place of the man's soul.

At length, [the devil] came out of him at the command of a holy man and the body fell down and was reduced to dust.

<div align="center"><b>The Clerk with a Demonic Voice</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 211-211</div>
In chapter 4, too, he writes that a clerk had such a pleasant voice that [people] thought it was a pleasure to listen to him, until a monk came along and warned them that this was not the voice of a human being but of a devil. The monk spoke earnestly and solemnly to the evil spirit, and when it went away, the corpse fell down and stank most dreadfully.

<div align="center"><b>Evil Spirit Emerging as Black Vapour from Women</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 211-211</div>
In The Hammer of Women who Work Harmful Magic, Jakob Sprenger tells us about vapour. He says that women have been seen in the fields with their dress pulled up, moving their body about and thrusting with their haunches, and that not long afterwards an evil spirit came into view, withdrawing himself from them in the form of a black vapour or exhalation.

<div align="center"><b>The Magicians of Mans and Elemental Bodies</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 211-211</div>
Magicians from Mans, Mirabellius and his associates, who were burned in Paris, said in their judicial confession that two things were necessary for an evil spirit to assume bodies-a favourable wind and a full moon, because if the wind is against him he cannot get the body to coalesce, and if there is a waning or a new moon he cannot form a body because he has only enough material for one which is very small indeed. The bigger the moon, the bigger [the body he can make].

<div align="center"><b>Apparitions to the Tarquinii</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 211-211</div>
Dionysius of Halicarnassus tells us that the Tarquinii often saw an apparition of an evil spirit in a body made of the elements

<div align="center"><b>The Ghost Appearing to Athenodorus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 211-211</div>
and the ghost which appeared to Athenodorus in Pliny [the Younger] was of this kind

<div align="center"><b>The Incestuous Persian's Restless Corpse</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 212-212</div>
Undoubtedly the apparition of the incestuous Persian, whose corpse kept on being thrown out by the earth, was of the kind we are discussing. There is a lengthy account of it by Agathias at the end of Book 2 of his Persian War.

<div align="center"><b>The Statue Illusion in Gunerchena</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 212-213</div>
I find that a third kind of apparitions of evil spirits takes place, very infrequently, through certain images or statuettes, and I shall tell you what Thomas of Cantimpré said about these in Book 2 of his Universal Good, chapter 57, paragraph 21.

'Gunerchena is a remarkable and well-known village in Brabant. Here a young man fell in love with a young girl, but when he spoke to her parents about marrying her, they refused. It was not long before the girl fell into an acute fever, and when she grew worse everyone thought she was dead. There was much lamentation, and the bells were rung as though for someone who had died. Without delay the girl's young lover left the village at twilight and was making his way to another [village] when, as he was passing some thickets, he heard a noise like that of a woman weeping. He ran to and fro, anxiously trying to find [the source] of the sound and discovered the girl he thought was dead.

"Your family is mourning you because you're dead", he said to her. "Where have you come from?"

"Oh, a man went ahead of me and showed me the way", she said. The young man was astonished at this, since he could see no one else except the girl, so boldly he hurried her away and immediately hid her in a house outwith the town. From here he came back to the village and spoke to his friends, then he and his friends went to the girl's father who was sitting by the corpse. [The young man] asked him if he would let him have his daughter whom he was mourning as though she were dead.

Her father was amazed and said, "Surely to God you are not saying to me that you will raise her from the dead and take her as your wife?"

The young man said, "Only if you willingly swear I can have your daughter as my wife if I give her back [to you] alive and cured".

Her father agreed and confirmed his promise in front of everyone. A moment later, the young man pulled aside a linen cloth which they thought was covering [the girl's body], and found a remarkable statue, the like of which could not have been made by human hands. Those who have taken a close look at devilish statues of this kind, however, say that inside the wood appears to be rotten and that the outside is wrapped in thin skin. After this the girl was brought back and restored to her father, and a few days later, now fully recovered, she took the young man as her husband and lived, safe and sound, right up to the present day.

<div align="center"><b>The Statue Deception in Flanders</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 213-213</div>
An almost similar instance is that of a man in the territory of Flanders. His sister fell ill and people thought she had died. On the day before she was to be buried, [her brother] found a statue in a thicket of reeds next to the sea-shore. He took [his sister] back to his own house, then entered the house where his friends were mourning her as if she were dead. He drew his sword, uncovered the statue, and cut it to pieces. Everyone was frightened and cried out, "Why would you behave with such savage barbarity towards your sister's corpse?" He smiled [and said], "Do I look as though I am being cruel to my sister? That is not my sister's body, but an image [of her], an illusion [created] by evil spirits". So saying, he took them all with him, brought them to his own house, and showed them his sister whom he had brought back. She, too, lived right up to the present day'.

<div align="center"><b>The Apparition to St Albert of Trapani</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 213-213</div>
Such [a form], perhaps, showed itself to St Albert [of Trapani], the Carmelite, who caused it to run away by making the sign of the cross at it. (See the narrative by Giovanni Maria [de Poluciis] of Compostela at the start of his biography of St Albert).

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Horse to Hadingus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 214-214</div>
If someone needs to be transported somewhere, [the spirit] clothes himself as a horse, as Olaus Magnus tells us in the case of Hadingus

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Horse to the Comte de Mâcon</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 214-214</div>
and Peter of Cluny and Hugh de Fleury in that of the Comte de Mâcon.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Cockerel to Pachomius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
He appeared to Pachomius as a large, raging cockerel, ([Simeon] Metaphrastes in Sauer, under the month of May)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Raven or Vulture to St Romuald</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
to St Romuald as a raven or vulture, (see Damian's biography of him)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Fox to Hilarion</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
but to Hilarion as a little fox.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Dog to Dunstan</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
[He appeared] to Dunstan as a dog, (see his biography in Sauer, under the month of May)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Snake to Léonard de Corbiac</b></div>
