to Léonard de Corbiac as a snake

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Serpent to St Margaret</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
to St Margaret and to Fernández Gonzalvo, Count of Castile and his army, as a serpent.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as Christ to St Martin</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
If he wants to lead someone to evil under the appearance of good, he appears in the form of Christ, as he did to St Martin, (see [Sulpicius] Severus's biography)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as God to the Widow Theodora</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
or in the form of God, as he did to the widow Theodora

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as an Angel to St Juliana</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
or in the form of an angel, as he did to St Juliana.

<div align="center"><b>The Peasant Demon and the Wayward Novice</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
An old author writes [as follows] in his life of Abbot St Robert. 'One night the holy man saw that something malign was standing at the entrance to the choir and was, in fact, making attempts to enter, although these attempts came to nothing. It had the form of a peasant with his tunic pulled up over long bare legs. He was carrying a little basket on his back and had a piece of wood fastened to his chest with cords. He started to walk round the choir with his neck thrust forward, casting his eyes intently on the brothers to see if he could perhaps notice something in one of them which would be pleasing to him. The [holy] man, however, started praying more earnestly to God and rousing the brothers from their listlessness, and the impudent spy was kept waiting for a long time to no avail. When he saw he was achieving nothing, he grew angry and took himself off to the lay-brothers' choir. There he also observed everyone inquisitively.

If he saw they were drowsy, he laughed and rejected them; if they were occupied with harmful thoughts, he broke into applause and started dancing in an extraordinary fashion. At length, he found a young man among the novices, whose mind was wandering off towards forbidden things. He was present only in body and, indeed, having secret thoughts about running away. Noting, therefore, that this man had captured his interest, the peasant] seized hold of him with a small three-pronged fork, thrust him into his little basket, and quickly rushed away from there. The holy man noticed this and was very anxious and concerned for the young man's salvation. In the morning he made diligent inquiry after him, but the youth with his wandering thoughts had fled before dawn, shaken off Christ's gentle yoke, and followed His enemy.

He joined a band of criminals, took to thieving, and not long afterwards was arrested and executed, ending his life in a most wretched fashion'.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil with Curled Hair at a Wedding</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
An evil spirit with crisply curled hair appeared to St Robert during a wedding procession, busying about with an insolent expression on its face. (See Sauer, Volume 3, under 7th June.)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Ship to Maximus of Rhegium</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
It appeared to Maximus of Rhegium as a ship recently landed, with its crew

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Heretical Clerk to Evagrius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
to Evagrius as an heretical clerk

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Reaper or Druggist to Macarius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
and to Macarius on one occasion as a reaper, and on another as a druggist

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as a Driver or Servant to Nathanael</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-215</div>
to Nathanael on one occasion as a driver riding a donkey, and on another as his servant, Jorarius

<div align="center"><b>Apelles and the Seductive Demon Woman</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 215-216</div>
It appeared to Apelles as a beautiful, long-haired, seductive woman, but he was sensible enough to drive it away with a glittering sword which he turned on it.

<div align="center"><b>The Devil's Frightening Appearance to St Martin</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
[The evil spirit] is certainly in the habit of appearing to the dying under an extremely frightening aspect as, according to Severus Sulpicius, he did to St Martin

<div align="center"><b>The Devil's Fiery Appearance to a Florentine</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
and to a well-known Florentine to whom he appeared, pouring forth a huge flame from his mouth and sulphurous smoke from his nostrils, (see Blaise Melanesius's life of St Giovanni Gualberto,)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil's Appearance to St Odilo</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
and similarly to St Odilo, (according to Sauer in the biography of the saint, under ist January.)

<div align="center"><b>The Devil as an Ethiopian to St Opportuna</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
To St Opportuna he appeared raging in the likeness of a very black 'Aithiopian', with his hair and beard dripping as though [made] of hot, flowing pitch, and with eyes like glowing red-hot iron, [promising] tortures, (Bishop Adelelinus in his biography of her in Sauer, under the month of April).

<div align="center"><b>The Angel's Explanation to St Aichadrus</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
An angel gave an explanation of this to St Aichadrus. It is permitted by God, [he said], so that the very terror of such a frightening vision can purge saintly people [of their sins], if there is anything left in need of purging in them. (See what Fullelus writes in his biography of Aichadrus in Sauer, under the month of September).

<div align="center"><b>The Speaking Head of Polycritus's Son</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
To this type also belong the skulls or heads of dead pagans in which evil spirits spoke, such as the head of Polycritus's son, about which Phlegon [wrote], taking his story from Hieron of Alexandria

<div align="center"><b>The Speaking Head of Publius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
the head of Publius-Phlegon again, from Antisthenes

<div align="center"><b>The Speaking Head of Gabinius</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
the head of Gabinius, described by Pliny the Elder

<div align="center"><b>The Speaking Skull of a Magician</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 216-216</div>
the skull of a magician, about which [Gianfrancesco] Pico [della Mirandola] tells us, and that of another magician, on which see Melchior Flavinus, the Minorite.

<div align="center"><b>Agrippa's Walking Corpse in Leuven</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 219-219</div>
Magicians, too, with the help of evil spirits of a higher rank, (because of their mutual pact), can compel evil spirits of lower rank to enter a corpse, wear it, move it, and do everything else for the time being which make it seem to be alive. Cornelius Agrippa did this in Leuven. He used to have an inquisitive member of his household and on one occasion, when Agrippa went on a journey and left the keys of his library with his wife, (whom he later divorced), with the instruction that she was not to let anyone enter it, this ill-considered young man kept badgering the poor woman in and out of season until he succeeded in getting from her the means of entering \[the library\]. He went in, came across a small book of invocations, and read it.

Well now, lo and behold he was disturbed by a knocking at the door! He continued reading. Someone knocked again. The young man, who was inexperienced in these things, did not answer at all and an evil spirit came in. \'Why have you summoned me?\' it asked. \'What are your instructions?\' Fear stopped the young man from speaking. The evil spirit blocked his throat, and thus the wretched \[youth\] paid the price of his wicked curiosity. Meanwhile the arch-magician returned to the house and saw evil spirits jumping about all over it. He made use of his customary magic, and they answered his summons and told him what had happened. He ordered them to enter the corpse of a murderer and go for a walk every so often in the market-place, (where the rest of his students were accustomed to meet quite frequently), and leave the corpse there. The body walked there three or four times and then fell down as the \[spirit\] which was directing it left it. For a long time, people believed the corpse fell down because \[the man\] had died all of a sudden, but the signs of suffocation started to give rise to suspicion, and later on time revealed everything and Agrippa fled to Lotharingia and began to spew out the heresies had had been concealing.

<div align="center"><b>Enarchus's Return from the Dead</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 220-220</div>
There is an extraordinary story in Plutarch who tells us that Enarchus was left for dead by his doctors and was seen not long afterwards to come back to life. He said he really had risen again from the dead and that the evil spirits which had taken him away from life had been severely reprimanded by their prince because they had carried him off instead of Nicandas, a tanner, who had been seized by fever the same day and hour while lying in bed and had died of his illness.

Not content with this, in order to prove he had come back from the dead, he predicted to Plutarch, who was not well at the time, that he would soon be fully recovered-and that is how things turned out.

<div align="center"><b>Earl Richard and the Attacking Corpse</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 220-220</div>
Ranulf \[Higden\], the author of Polychronicon, tells us that one night Earl Richard went into a church all by himself to offer a prayer, and there found a bier on which a corpse had been laid out. \'All of a sudden, while the Earl was praying, the dead man jumped off the bier with a loud cry and came towards the Earl with his arms outstretched, as though to knock him down in his embrace. The Earl made the sign of the cross on his own forehead and somewhat forcefully adjured \[the dead man\] to go away. This he did not do, so \[the Earl\] drew his sword and cut him down, and thereafter gave instructions that watch was to be kept over corpses throughout the whole region\'.

<div align="center"><b>Seasonal Deaths in Lucomorye</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 220-220</div>
More extraordinary than either of these \[stories\] is one told by \[Robert\] Gaguin in his description of Muscovy, and I shall now add it \[to my account\]. \'Certain people living in Lucomorye, a province of Russia\', he writes, \'die every year on 27th November, as swallows and frogs are in the habit of doing, before the cold of winter sets in. Then, when spring returns, on 24th April, they come back to life again\'. He adds, \'When these people sense that death is near, they put goods they want to sell in certain places and their neighbours, the Grustintzi and Serponovtzi, take them away, leaving behind in the same spot payment of equivalent value. When the people of Lucomorye come back to life, they take this payment if they think it fair. If they do not, they ask for their goods back. It is said that this is why quarrels and wars very frequently arise between them\'.

<div align="center"><b>Voice from Ajax's Tomb</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 221-221</div>
Finally, also relevant here is the story about a German youth which I shall tell in the next question, which would be remarkable but for the fact that he pretended to be killed, even though he had not been killed, but had simply fainted away and then thought he was decapitated. Everyone else thought the same, since their eyes had been blinded, but his head had not been cut off, nor had it had been restored. It was all a conjuring-trick.

At this point, let me add one example of a fictitious resurrection. Antiquity had produced no story more convincing or more wonderful than this one. It took place in Dalheim in Lotharingia in 1581, and involved Petronius, a herdsman, and Abrahel, a succuba. In his summary of Book 2, that remarkable man, Nicolas Rémy, had shown most picturesquely and in very elegant verse, how Abrahel seduced Petronius into criminal embraces, then into the murder of his own small son, and finally, because of his grief and despair and a false resurrection of his child, into idolatry: and how the evil spirit presented him with a likeness of the living \[child\] for a whole year and then abandoned it as a corpse. I shall transcribe this with greater pleasure than equanimity because, as the result of the insupportably bad practice of its recent printer, the verses in \[Rémy\'s\] Worship of Evil Spirits have been removed and the work published in mutilated form. \[Thus\] they will be preserved unharmed, and in consequence will be read in more places.

\'There is a country in the middle of the bounds of an enormous kingdom Between the winding streams of the sandy Moselle And the straight courses of the Saar with its undisturbed river-bank. There is a village here called Delheim-not a well-known nameAnd while a herdsman was grazing his flock in a distant valley there And pondering the various cares which were rolling around in his heart, Perhaps sick of the unfairness attendant on his straitened circumstances, Or contemplating, with a degree of passion, revenge and anger Because of a loss he had suffered, or high words exchanged in a quarrel, A succuba presented herself quite unexpectedly.

She was most seductive. Her face had a maidenly expression, her gestures were charming, Her head was decked with false sapphires, And \[her hair\] was twisted into glistening curls on each side \[of her face\]. She comforted the unhappy man and gave him hope of becoming master of his situation, And promised him the wealth which the needy have so often prayed for in vain, Or which the long-eared king \[Midas\] was willing to demand of Iacchus \[Dionysus\] As long as he did not object to what he was saying. Just as when a fire which is quickened by a pair of bellows Licks the iron while the puffing renders \[the metal\] ductile, So that the hard \[substance\] is persuaded to be compliant And is prepared \[to receive\] all its instructions, And does not disregard the arts of persuasion: \[The herdsman\] is happy to submit to \[the succuba\'s\] alluring command, And on both sides trust is strengthened by a \[mutual\] pact. He made his way into her dreadful embraces. Their unequal love was cemented by senseless coition. Irreparable, freedom Died at once amid those unspeakable passions. And, just as the parts of a body wounded by the dangerous bite of snakes Spread the consequent poison throughout the whole body, So that health itself cannot provide what health requires, Even if it spreads itself as far as it can, So Abrahel took over her lover entirely.

<div align="center"><b>Petronius and the Succuba Abrahel</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 221-223</div>
When their cattle were being killed by murrain, they gathered at the tomb of Ajax, son of Telamon, in Sigeum, and heaped various curses on the dead man. One \[shepherd\], more forward than the rest, began to recite verses from Homer in which Ajax is described as running away-\'Aïas δ\' oùxéti µíuvev\' \[Ajax stayed no longer, Iliad 14.727\]-and a voice burst fiercely from the tomb, \"AMou \"piliov\' \[But I did stay\], and the shepherds, thoroughly frightened, took to their heels. The origin of the voice, you see, was an evil spirit.

And broke him by filthiness-this very wicked woman had designated it thus so that she could bend the vanquished man to any of her stipulations, and he could have absolutely no will \[of his own\] to say yes or no. Straight away and without hesitation she demanded an initial recompense for her violated virtue. \'Play your part\', she said, \'so that your love May be the equal of mine\'. There could be no greater proof of this Than if he conquered Nature and carried out a crime Any by this fresh deed subordinated a father\'s love to his passion. Therefore, since it was already late, he drove his whole flock Into the sheep-folds and finally settled himself in his little house.

First, so that his dear son would put his weak arms round his neck To support himself \[to receive his father\'s\] kisses, he drew out of his bagWhich his son was keen to search, although he had often found it empty When \[his father\] came back from the countryside-an apple and held it out to him. \[The boy\] had scarcely taken his first bite, grinding his teeth because he did not like the sour taste, Than he turned pale and sucked it hesitatingly with the tips of his lips, And lay stretched out on the ground which fell away behind him. If \[his father\] could look at this with equanimity While carrying out his orders with this pledge, It would be strong evidence that his was still cleaving to his new love.

Such an unspeakable crime horrified him to begin with And Nature and passion fought each other in a battle whose outcome was uncertain. But he who introduced a powerful enemy into an empty citadel Hesitated in vain to carry out his instructions, because if he did nothing in response to her first command when she entreating him seductively, The insolent woman immediately passed to violent anger And her will, ever present, was like statute law to him. Now the evening which was obliged to be privy to so great a crime Had brought back to the threshold of his cottage the father entangled in these dreadful things. When his son met him and spontaneously greeted him with a big hug as usual, He groaned, and shifted from foot to foot, and stammered. Yet this did not stop him at all from \[fulfilling\] his wicked promises.

The cruel man Dared to continue to reach for the deadly gifts of murder. Let his son be the victim of an abominable love! His father did not shrink from slaughtering him with his own hand. Straight away Nature, which had allowed herself to be concealed And had withdrawn because of his feigned excuses, came out more ardently and rose up powerfully with grief; And while \[this grief\] fed on the feeble advantages \[he had wanted to gain\] And his ill-considered weeping, the woman stood nearby to give him her advice. The moment he killed \[the boy\], she consoled him And, at the same time, promised to restore \[the boy\] to life and make him live again, If \[the father\] would willingly worship her and, as a suppliant, beg this of her; And he, who had \[morally\] bankrupted himself earlier, Readily agreed to this turn of events, for which he would pay an extremely heavy rate of interest.

And while the fickle man wanted to appease, in any way he could, The gods of his household whom he had dishonoured by the foul murder of his son, He disgraced them even more and polluted them more repulsively Because first of all he threw himself on his knees before the evil spirit And worshipped it forthwith as a divinity. Then he promised the boy to her as payment, If only, with her help, his last-born child could feed himself upon life-giving air once more, \[Begging her\] just as when winds join battle and cast themselves An angry sea, and a sailor trembles in every limb quite uncertain of his life, and is lavish in his pleas to all the gods, And tires them out with his prayers.

The corpse lay on its back on the mound where it had been thrown When life had finally fled and abandoned it, And its mother, very late in the day, began to make haste to bury it. But look! \[The child\'s\] body seemed to stir itself between her hands as she touched him And gradually once again to overcome \[death\] with \[living\] heat. The kind of light those who are half-alive, half-dead show in their downcast eyes, Or which, with difficulty, releases those who have been plunged deep in sleepThese signs of health restored, beyond what people hope, Seize them and give them joy, and with as much eagerness as they can The vigorously warm the powerless limbs And recall now the arms, now the legs, to their living functions.

The boy rose to his feet straight away, then Took a step, can called to his parents in the voice they knew well; Only he was different in appearance from \[the boy\] he had once been, Because he had a face \[rendered\] ugly because the skin was drawn tight over it, And because a gloomy stupidity was in possession of his receding eyes. A foreign life, therefore, quickened the senses which had been deserted And in this apparition restored the breath which had once been there, (As Virbius once obtained the light \[of life\] which had been cut off from him). But the mind was not the same, and the breath Which used to warm his life did not now exercise that same office. An evil spirit provided the mechanism to drive the sluggish bulk, Give it the appearance of life, and imitate the actions of \[human\] behaviour, Breathing \[into it the actions\] which Nature used to carry out for herself. At length, the moment a year had run its whole course And completed its circuit of the dense globe, \[The evil spirit\] abandoned its stopping-place and left the dwelling empty.

It betrayed its flight by a sulphurous smell and a noisy din, And pushed itself out, bursting forth into stinking gusts of air. Then the corpse fell down, thrust away by this second death. There followed a foetid stench and \[the body became\] very heavy. A kind of numbness \[caused by\] unhindered emotion covered the motionless \[child\] Completely with earth, and cut off the widespread strength of the stench So that it could not gain entrance to human nostrils. \[The body\] was carried away from the house by a hook firmly attached to it, And the dishonoured remains were laid under a clean sod cut from the earth, While the father, who had been mad enough to shame Nature this way With his disgraceful act, shuddered at what he had done\'.

<div align="center"><b>Socrates's Counterfeit Head Restoration</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 221-221</div>
There are also the cases of Gabinius, Polycritus, and Publius whose heads predicted the future, and the example from Apuleius about Socrates whose head was cut off by a female inn-keeper who was also an aged sorceress \[veratrix\]. Having cut it off, she joined it to the body again by sticking a sponge \[under the cut\]; but next day, when the unfortunate man, contrary to her instruction, drank from a river, the sponge fell out and he lost his head because it really had been cut off and its restoration was counterfeit, \[Metamorphoses 1.16-18\].

<div align="center"><b>Magicians' Deadly Tricks at the English Queen's Court</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 226-227</div>
Two magicians came together at the court of the English queen in order to put on an entertaining show and made a remarkably friendly agreement to obey each other in one [of their tricks]. The first one told the other to look down from a window, but when he did this, all of a sudden stag's horns sprouted from his head and for a long time everyone wanted to pull faces and mock him. Consequently, rendered powerless by the insult and wanting to avenge himself in a more savage fashion, [the second magician] drew a human figure on a wall with charcoal, then ordered the first magician to go up to the figure. As he was about to do so, he noticed it was devising instant death for him and, filled with horror, he warded it off with prayer.

The other [magician] reminded him forcefully of their agreement and so, under this compulsion, [the magician] went up to [the figure]. The wall seemed to make room for his body as he approached, but once he had come right up to [the wall], he was never seen again anywhere afterwards. In other words, an evil spirit of higher rank had killed him and taken his body away to an uninhabited place or a cave somewhere else.

<div align="center"><b>The Decapitation Illusion and the Lily Counterspell</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 226-227</div>
In N', he says, 'an illusionist [praestigiator] who came from a good family decapitated their host's servant, in a public guest-house, at the request of the other guests. But when he wanted to put the man's head back on again, he noticed there was another magician in the room who was stopping him from carrying out his intention. [The illusionist] asked him not to do that, but when [the magician] would not comply after being asked more than once, the illusionist made a lily grow on the table, and when he cut off its head and flowers, the magician who had been impeding him immediately fell [from his seat at] the table minus his head; and straight away, without hindrance, the illusionist put the magician's head on the servant and then fled the guest-house and the city to avoid being arrested by the magistrate on a charge of murder.

<div align="center"><b>Zyto Swallows the Bavarian Performer</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 226-227</div>
A fourth [example] concerns Zyto of Bohemia, a well-known illusionist, who was engaged in a contest with Bavarians. Jan Dubravius tells us what happened. 'Wenceslaus, Emperor and King of Bohemia', he says, 'was a relation by marriage of John, the Prince of Bavaria, because he had married his daughter, Sophia. His father-in-law knew that his son-in-law took pleasure in stage-plays and magical illusions, and so he filled a wagon with actors and illusionists and took them with him to Prague. There, while the most remarkable of the performers was displaying his professional stunts to delight people's eyes, among the spectators was Zyto, Wenceslaus's magician, his mouth gaping open in a great yawn. He came nearer the Bavarian performer and suddenly swallowed him and all his equipment, with the exception of his shoes which he spat out because they seemed to be covered in mud. Looking for a way to get rid of [what he had swallowed], he unburdened his stomach, which was heavy with its unusual food, into a bathtub filled with water and restored the illusionist, still wet, to the spectators'.

<div align="center"><b>Gilbert and Catillus's Magical Contest</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 227</div>
My fifth [example] will be taken from Olaus [Magnus] Book 3, chapter 20. Here a magician called Gilbert was arguing with Catillus, his instructor, about superiority in the practice of magic. [Catillus] pointed a tiny stick inscribed with Gothic or Ruthenian characters at him, so [Gilbert] took hold of him. [Catillus] became rigid at once and was carried away, trussed up, to an underground cave on an island in a lake called 'Veter' by the Ostrogoths.

<div align="center"><b>The Chaldaean Magician Bound by Holy Prayers</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 228</div>
This is proved by the complaint of the Chaldaean magician in St Augustine who 'kept complaining that the great effort he was making to be successful in purging his soul was rendered ineffectual because a man who had power over this and was touched by envy had bound the Powers he had invoked with holy prayers, the result being that they could not grant what was demanded of them.'

<div align="center"><b>Cynops's Pact with Evil Spirits</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 228</div>
Eusebius tells us the same thing, and quite clearly it applies to the magician Cynops in Prochorus, (whoever he was), [who says] that when 'St John was questioning evil spirits about why they were in the habit of obeying Cynops, one of them replied, "Because the entire power of Satan resides in him, and he has a treaty and pact with every one of our princes. We, too, on our part, have a pact with him and so Cynops obeys us and we him"'. In the absence of such a pact, every attempt made by magicians against evil spirits is useless, since these work only because of the pact, (as I said in question 2.)

<div align="center"><b>John the German Evokes Ancestral Shadows</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 228-229</div>
Let me write down what one reads about that last one in the author of the German adages, because it is appropriate, and not unpleasant, to blunt the boasting of certain people who are themselves morally degenerate, and prattle about the smoky images of their ancestors, and [say] they did things they themselves have not done. Hence, they write that although [John] was illegitimate, he had a great reputation for learning and was promoted to a canonry at Halberstadt, a post not usually granted to anyone unless he came from a good family. The fault common to good families-pride-made his fellow-canons despise him. He was irked by this and finally decided to remedy his annoyance.

He invited his colleagues to dinner and received them in most elegant fashion; and among all his other pleasantries he included mention of a magical performer and suggested that each man would surely like to see his own parents. They were indeed extremely keen to see them and after a while, by means of a wicked incantation, [the magician] evoked the misshapen shadowy forms of a cook, a pub landlord, a professional jester, and a peasant. [The canons] admitted they had sometimes seen these faces in their parents' homes. [John's] own father was shown, too, dressed as a canon, with a fat paunch. The shadowy forms were dismissed, and then [John] said, 'Well now! Tell me honestly, is there anyone's father here you think is better born [than the others]? ' Thoroughly frightened and filled with shame, each of them went back home and from then on did not accuse John of any imperfection in his blood-line.

<div align="center"><b>St Stephen Commands Evil Spirits to Appear</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 229</div>
St Stephen, Bishop of Perm, 'told the evil spirits, who were turning people away to prevent them from listening to him, to show themselves openly in visible form. They did as they were told and appeared-immensely tall, completely black, with horns [on their heads], and breathing out stench and flames', (Sauer in his biography [of St Stephen], under the month of September).

<div align="center"><b>Eleazar Expels Evil Spirit with a Root</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 230-231</div>
In Josephus, Antiquities Book 8, chapter 2, [section 6], Eleazar draws out an evil spirit by using a particular root.

<div align="center"><b>The Fish Heart Drives Away the Evil Spirit in Tobit</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 230-231</div>
There is a passage in Tobit which runs as follows: 'if you put a small part of the heart' [of the fish "Callyonimus"] 'on top of charcoal, its smoke will clear out every kind of evil spirit', and we read later that this was done. My response is that this suffumigation drove the spirit away indirectly. The angel Raphael was the one who expelled the evil spirit directly and despatched him to an uninhabited place. In consequence, the whole action was attributed to Raphael afterwards, 'He (Raphael) chased away the evil spirit from her'.

<div align="center"><b>David's Harp Repels the Evil Spirit from Saul</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 231</div>
They beckon us in the direction of David's harp but do so in vain because in that instance it was not the pleasant tune which was the direct cause of putting the evil spirit to flight. When the King had been soothed by the harmoniousness of the music, as long as David was playing the harp, at any rate, the humours which were agitating him started to settle themselves down to some extent, and in the meantime were in a less suitable state to be affected by the evil spirit's stirring them up. This is why the wicked [spirit] began to retire at that moment-not because it was pleased or offended by the music, but because it would find it difficult to put up with the fact that its possession of its dwelling-place was being thrown into disorder, so to speak, by this improvement in the mingling of [the King's] humours.

<div align="center"><b>The Cursed Box and the Possessed Wife in Kleinbasel</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 232-233</div>
One, recounted by Johannes Nider as follows, will be sufficient. 'A little before the present General Council, a man of wicked life had a house in Kleinbasel and, as it happened, and had come under reasonable suspicion of performing acts of harmful magic [maleficiis]. He had a daughter and married her to a young man in his house, and when at length the old man fell ill, he pointed out a box to his daughter and son-in-law and said, "Don't move this little box, because if you do, you'll regret it. Let it stay where it is after I am dead", and thus not long afterwards the old man expired.

A long time went by. Neither his daughter nor her husband paid any great heed to her father's instruction, and when they moved from their former home to another little house which was big enough [for them], they carried the box to their new home. During the journey, however, it started to weigh so much that [the husband's] strength failed him, and he called on his wife to help him. Whether his wife opened the box later in the house, or whether somehow or other she forgot [what her father had said] about the box, people agree on one thing-the two of them came into their new house with a small child they had begotten. Well now, all of a sudden, the wife rushed at the cradle like a raving mad woman, with the intention of killing the child. Her husband saw this, forcibly seized hold of his wife, and realised she was under the control of an evil spirit. While the evil spirit was being exorcised, it threatened it would not leave unless [her husband] killed her, which he did in spite of the exorcist's trying to prevent him. Next day her husband was making his way along a pavement in the town when, quite unexpectedly, he was struck by a stone falling from the upper part of a water-channel which was coming away [from the building]. It struck him, apparently with the co-operation of an evil spirit, in the face and rendered him so ugly that he scarcely seemed to be a human being.'

<div align="center"><b>St Conon Imprisons Evil Spirits in Fields and Church</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 233</div>
St Conon, for example, about whom the Jesuit Matthias Rader gives the following account in The Succession of the Months among the Greeks, under 'March'. 'He exercised such great force and power over evil spirits that he would put some of them in charge of the fields and make them guardians of the crops. Others he would imprison in sealed-up buildings and bury in the foundation of a church he intended to build.

<div align="center"><b>St Lupus Imprisons an Evil Spirit in Water</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 233</div>
Likewise, St Lupus imprisoned one in Sens, afflicting him with thirst and inserting him, wailing and proclaiming who he was, in a small vessel of cold water he had told someone to bring. (This is what his ancient biographer says in Sauer, under the month of September.)

<div align="center"><b>The Familiar Spirit Demands Gold for a Church Bell</b></div>
<div align="center">"Martin Del Rio - Investigations Into Magic Volume 2" Pages 234</div>
I remember reading in Johannes Caesarius the exemplary story-well worth remembering-of a familiar spirit [paredri].

In this story, what one finds astonishing is that the evil spirit demanded five gold coins as his price and gave orders for a bell to be purchased with them for a poor church so that it could summon the faithful to divine worship every Sunday. There was undoubtedly some deception lying hidden here, and the hope of a larger sum of money was blindingly obvious. It is also remarkable that he caused the soldier no harm.
